1 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Slater v. Blackwood


L.A. No. 30490


Supreme Court of California


15 Cal. 3d 791; 543 P.2d 593; 126 Cal. Rptr. 225; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 270



December 24, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied January 21, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 3265N, Fiorenzo V. Lopardo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We therefore conclude that the order of the trial court sustaining defendants' demurrer on the grounds of res judicata must be sustained. The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff passenger sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) that sustained a demurrer by defendants, driver and car owner, to plaintiff's second complaint on the ground that an earlier judgment was res judicata and barred the second complaint, even when California's guest statute had been rendered unconstitutional in the interim.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff passenger was injured in a car accident in 1969 and filed an action against defendants, car driver and owner, for damages. The trial court granted defendants' motion for non-suit after plaintiff's opening arguments, finding that the evidence would not support recovery under the guest statute, Cal. Veh. Code § 17158. The California Supreme Court later held the guest statute to be unconstitutional, and plaintiff filed a new complaint against defendants under theories of negligence. The trial court sustained defendants' demurrer without leave to amend, finding that plaintiff's claim was barred by res judicata. The court sustained the trial court's order and affirmed the judgment. The court held that plaintiff's second complaint was the same cause of action, even if her theory of recovery had now changed. The court held that the principles of res judicata could not be set aside when the statute relied upon was subsequently held invalid, regardless of the inequity that the ruling might impose. A subsequent change in the law could not be the basis for reviving a dead claim.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and order of the trial court that sustained the demurrer on behalf of defendants, driver and car owner, on the basis of res judicata. The court held that plaintiff passenger could not assert a new claim based upon the same cause of action when her first claim had been barred by a valid statute that had since been found to be unconstitutional.

 

2 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Los Angeles v. Public Utilities Com.


S.F. Nos. 23215, 23237, 23257


Supreme Court of California


15 Cal. 3d 680; 542 P.2d 1371; 125 Cal. Rptr. 779; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 262



December 12, 1975 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The matter arose from respondent Public Utilities Commission's actions on remand in three telephone rate cases. Respondent determined that it lacked legal authority to approve tariffs that would have annually adjusted telephone rates to take account of changing federal tax expenses and, as a result, set a rate that would have created a windfall for the telephone companies to the detriment of the ratepayers.


OVERVIEW: In three telephone rate cases respondent Public Utilities Commission determined, inter alia, that it lacked legal authority to approve tariffs that would annually adjust telephone rates to take account of changing federal tax expenses. As a consequence, respondent found itself compelled to set a rate which would create a windfall for the telephone companies to the detriment of the ratepayers. Respondent took the action in spite of the court's having annulled its previous decisions in the matter for failure to consider lawful alternatives in calculation of federal income tax expense. The court held that because annual adjustment comported both with the governing statutes and the constitution, respondent failed regularly to pursue its authority in failing to consider it, Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 1757. The court remanded the matter, directing respondent to proceed either to take further testimony on the system or to consider its adoption on the basis of the testimony contained in the record of three cases. The court further stated that respondent should weigh its desirability, and set forth the reasons for its ultimate decisions, Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 1705.


OUTCOME: The court held that respondent Public Utilities Commission failed regularly to pursue its authority, and therefore the rates could not stand in their entirety. The court affirmed respondent's order except insofar as it depended upon the erroneous treatment of tax expenses, but remanded in order that respondent could reconsider its action with knowledge of the full scope of its powers.

 

3 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


HFH, Ltd. v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L.A. Nos. 30382, 30383


Supreme Court of California


15 Cal. 3d 508; 542 P.2d 237; 125 Cal. Rptr. 365; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 248; 6 ELR 20062



November 12, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The application of petitioner Von's Grocery Co. for a rehearing was denied December 24, 1975, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Clark, J., was of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writs are discharged, and the peremptory writs denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, partnership and corporation, filed a petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent, the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), to vacate an order sustaining defendant city's demurrer to plaintiffs' complaint alleging that a zoning ordinance enacted by defendants diminished the value of their property and constituted a taking without just compensation.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, partnership and corporation, entered into a contract to purchase property. Plaintiffs conditioned the sale on the seller's ability to procure commercial zoning for the land. Plaintiffs assumed ownership after defendant city rezoned the property as commercial. Plaintiffs then submitted, and defendant approved, a commercial development plan. Five years later, with the land still undeveloped, defendant rezoned it as agricultural. Plaintiffs sought to sell the property and applied to have it rezoned as commercial in order to procure a higher purchase price. Defendant denied the application and rezoned the property as residential. Plaintiffs filed suit alleging that the rezoning diminished the market value of the property and constituted an inverse condemnation. Respondent trial court sustained defendant's demurrer to the complaint. Plaintiffs petitioned for a writ of mandate to challenge the order. The court denied the petition, holding that an inverse condemnation action could not lie where a zoning action resulted in a mere reduction in the market value of the property. The court also found that defendant was immune from suit under Cal. Gov't Code § 818.2.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for a writ of mandate by plaintiffs, partnership and corporation, to challenge an order sustaining defendant city's demurrer to plaintiffs' complaint. Plaintiffs could not maintain an inverse condemnation action where defendant's zoning action resulted in the mere reduction of the market value of plaintiffs' property and defendant was entitled to governmental immunity.

 

4 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Johnson v. Hamilton


L.A. No. 30401


Supreme Court of California


15 Cal. 3d 461; 541 P.2d 881; 125 Cal. Rptr. 129; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 244



October 27, 1975 



DISPOSITION:  Since respondent city clerk accepted petitioner's nominating papers and agreed to consider petitioner as a candidate for the subject election the alternative writ of mandate is discharged and the peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner candidate filed a writ in the Supreme Court of California for inquiry into the constitutionality of the charter of a city that imposed residence requirements of one year and six months respectively upon candidates for city office.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city clerk informed petitioner candidate that he failed to satisfy residence requirements that required a one-year residence and a six month residence, respectively. Petitioner sought review of the constitutionality of these residence requirements. Since the elections were fast approaching, the court issued a writ to respondent to accept petitioner's nominating papers or to show cause why she had not done so. Respondent in her return to the writ informed the court that she had accepted petitioner's papers. The court discharged the writ of mandate and denied the peremptory writ as respondent accepted petitioner's nominating papers and agreed to consider petitioner as a candidate for the subject election. The court found that a 30-day pre-filing residence requirement seemed reasonably necessary and convenient to accommodate the needs of election officials. The court held that any durational residence requirement for candidates for local office in excess of 30 days was violative of the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend IV.


OUTCOME: The court found that the one year and six month residence requirements were unconstitutional. The court discharged the alternative writ of mandate and denied the peremptory writ because respondent city clerk accepted petitioner candidate's nominating papers and agreed to consider petitioner as a candidate for the subject election.

 

5 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Glendale City Employees' Ass'n v. Glendale


L.A. No. 30357


Supreme Court of California


15 Cal. 3d 328; 540 P.2d 609; 124 Cal. Rptr. 513; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 234; 90 L.R.R.M. 2603



October 3, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of defendants and appellants for a rehearing was denied October 30, 1975. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 988944, Robert W. Kenny, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, the judgment is reversed, and the cause remanded for further proceedings in accord with the views expressed in this opinion. Each side shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employees association challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which upheld a memorandum between plaintiff and defendant city. The trial court admitted parol testimony of negotiators to aid in the interpretation of the memorandum's provisions.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed the decision of the trial court. The trial court held, by admitting parol testimony of negotiators, that a memorandum between plaintiff employees association and defendant city was binding. City claimed that the memorandum was not binding, that the trial court erred in its interpretation of the memorandum, and that in any event the memorandum could not be enforced by writ of mandamus. City also argued that the suit was not a proper class action, and that relief was barred by a failure to exhaust administrative remedies. The court held that the agreement entered into under the Meyers-Milias-Brown Act (Act), Cal. Gov't. Code § 3505.1, and approved by the governing board of the local entities bound city and employees association. The court concluded that a salary ordinance did not meet the proper criteria, and that the failure of city to pay salaries in excess of the arithmetic average of surveyed jurisdictions constituted an abuse of discretion and a breach both of the memorandum of understanding and of the city's duty under the Act.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court that held, by admitting parol evidence, that a memorandum between plaintiff employees association and defendant city was binding. The failure of city to pay salaries in excess of the arithmetic average of surveyed jurisdictions constituted an abuse of discretion and a breach both of the memorandum of understanding and of city's duty under statute.

 

6 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Murgia v. Municipal Court for Bakersfield Judicial Dist.


L.A. No. 30426


Supreme Court of California


15 Cal. 3d 286; 540 P.2d 44; 124 Cal. Rptr. 204; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 231; 90 L.R.R.M. 2803



September 24, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the real party in interest for a rehearing was denied October 23, 1975.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue, directing the trial court to vacate its order denying discovery and to proceed in accordance with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner union members filed a petition for writ of mandate, seeking an order directing respondent Municipal Court for Bakersfield Judicial District to vacate an order denying respondents discovery in criminal prosecutions by the state. Petitioners argued that that they were singled out for prosecution because they were union members, that such prosecution violated the equal protection clause, and that they were entitled to discovery.


OVERVIEW: The state initiated criminal prosecutions of petitioner union members. Petitioners sought discovery as to whether penal statutes were being discriminately enforced against petitioners. The request for discovery was denied by respondent trial court. Petitioners filed a petition for writ of mandate, claiming that the penal statutes were being discriminately enforced against them, and therefore petitioners were entitled to discovery of information as to whether law enforcement engaged in a policy of discrimination toward petitioners. The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent to vacate its order denying discovery. The court held that a conscious policy of selective enforcement directed at members of a labor organization was prima facie discriminatory and invalid. The court further stated that criminal defendants were generally entitled to discover all relevant information in the possession of the prosecution that would assist a defendant in the preparation of his defense. Since a defendant could raise intentional discrimination as a ground for dismissal of a prosecution, petitioners were entitled to discovery of information supportive of their claims.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent trial court to vacate its order denying petitioner union members discovery pursuant to the state's criminal prosecution of petitioners. The court held that singling out members of a union for prosecution was violative of the equal protection clauses of the state and federal constitutions, and petitioners were therefore entitled to discovery to support their claim.

 

7 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Safer v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 30364


Supreme Court of California


15 Cal. 3d 230; 540 P.2d 14; 124 Cal. Rptr. 174; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 228; 90 L.R.R.M. 2712



September 19, 1975 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners union members and sympathizers sought a writ of prohibition on respondent Superior Court of Ventura County (California) and district attorney as real party in interest, moved for dismissal, demurred proceedings, or alternatively requested a jury trial on civil charges. Petitioners were charged with criminal misdemeanors but just before trial were served with show cause orders in contempt proceedings under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1209.


OVERVIEW: Union members and sympathizers were arrested and charged with misdemeanors under Cal. Penal Code § 166(4) after protesting in support of farm workers. Shortly before trial, respondent superior court allowed district attorney as real party in interest to serve petitioners with show cause orders in contempt proceedings under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1209. Petitioners moved for dismissal under Cal. Penal Code § 1387, demurred the new proceedings, or alternatively requested a jury trial on the civil charges but the trial court ruled adversely on their arguments. On appeal, the court ruled in favor of petitioners and ordered issuance of a writ of prohibition because respondent court exceeded its jurisdiction by permitting the district attorney to disregard its statutory authority and to prosecute petitioners for contempt stemming from a civil litigation. The court found no legislative authorization to participate in the civil case and further concluded that the intervention of the district attorney introduced the government on one side of the litigation and exposed the disadvantaged litigants to nothing less than their incarceration.


OUTCOME: The court ordered issuance of a writ of prohibition because respondent superior court exceeded its jurisdiction by permitting real party in interest district attorney to disregard statutory authority and prosecute petitioners, union members and sympathizers for contempt stemming from a civil litigation after petitioners were arrested and charged with misdemeanor offense during a protest in support of farm workers.

 

8 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Hale v. Superior Court of San Francisco


S.F. No. 23287


Supreme Court of California


15 Cal. 3d 221; 539 P.2d 817; 124 Cal. Rptr. 57; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 227



September 18, 1975 



DISPOSITION:  The writ shall issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of prohibition under Cal. Penal Code § 1368.1(a) to prevent respondent, judge of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), from conducting petitioner's mental competency hearing until after an indictment or an information was filed against him in his felony case.


OVERVIEW: Respondent superior court judge proposed to have petitioner's mental competency hearing before an indictment or information was issued charging him with the felony of setting fire to a dwelling under Cal. Penal Code § 17(a) & Cal. Penal Code § 447a. The court granted petitioner's request for a writ prohibiting respondent from conducting the hearing before the indictment or information issued because Cal. Penal Code § 1368.1(a) clearly supported petitioner's position. The court held that § 1368.1(a) was constitutional and not only did not deprive petitioner of due process, but promoted his due process rights. The court found that, under the California incompetency commitment law, if petitioner was found incompetent to stand trial, he could have been committed for up to three years under Cal. Penal Code § 1370, and indefinitely thereafter under the civil commitment law. The court held that § 1368.1(a) declared that petitioner could not be subjected to such long-term confinement unless probable cause to believe him guilty of the crime charged was determined first by means of a preliminary hearing or grand jury hearing.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner's request for a writ of prohibition because a person found incompetent to stand trial could be committed for up to three years, and indefinitely thereafter under the applicable civil commitment statute. The controlling statute prevented such long-term confinement unless probable cause to believe the person guilty of the crime charged was first determined by a hearing.

10 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Green v. Layton


L.A. No. 30415


Supreme Court of California


14 Cal. 3d 922; 538 P.2d 225; 123 Cal. Rptr. 97; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 329



August 6, 1975 



DISPOSITION:  The alternative writ heretofore issued, having served its purpose, is discharged, and the peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant candidates sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent city clerk to accept their nominating petitions for filing and to place their names on an election ballot.


OVERVIEW: Appellant candidates were required to file a declaration of intention pursuant to the city election code. When they filed their declarations of intention, appellants signed statements that they would submit an "in lieu" petition instead of paying the filing fee as provided in the election code. Respondent refused acceptance of appellants' nominating petitions because their in lieu petitions contained an insufficient number of signatures. Appellants sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent to accept their nominating petitions and to place their names on the ballot. The court denied the writ because the relief sought was no longer available but held that the code requirement that an irrevocable choice between the two alternatives had to be made at the time a candidate filed his declaration of candidacy was unconstitutional because it not only unfairly burdened a candidate's exercise of the "in lieu" alternative, but it bore no reasonable relationship to the seriousness of his candidacy or to the cost of administration of the nominating process.


OUTCOME: The court denied a writ of mandate to appellants, who were candidates for elective office, because the relief sought was unavailable but held that the code requirement on a candidate to make an irrevocable choice whether to pay a filing fee or submit an in lieu petition at the time of filing his declaration of intention was unconstitutional because it bore no reasonable relationship to the seriousness of his candidacy.

 

11 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re E.


Crim. No. 18456


Supreme Court of California


15 Cal. 3d 183; 538 P.2d 231; 123 Cal. Rptr. 103; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 225



August 4, 1975 



DISPOSITION:  Let a writ of habeas corpus issue. The dispositional order of September 5, 1974, is vacated and petitioner is ordered discharged from any detention and commitment under such order. Petitioner is remanded to the juvenile court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a ward of the juvenile court, sought a writ habeas corpus to be released from confinement in a state mental hospital on the grounds that his commitment by the juvenile court did not comply with the procedures of the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5000 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, a ward of the juvenile court, was originally declared a ward of the court following the sustaining of a petition alleging that he committed theft and received stolen property. At the dispositional hearing, two psychiatrists testified that petitioner suffered from some degree of mental illness and the juvenile court referee ordered petitioner committed to a state mental hospital. Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus to be released from the hospital on the grounds that his confinement was effected without compliance with the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act (LPS Act), Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5000 et seq. The court issued a writ of habeas corpus, discharged the order detaining and committing petitioner, and remanded him to the juvenile court. The court held that the commitment of a mentally disordered minor who was also a ward of the juvenile court could be accomplished only in accordance with the LPS Act, which required procedures such as a 72-hour evaluation, a 14-day intensive treatment, and judicial certification for 90-day commitment periods. The court determined that none of the substantial protections of the LPS Act were afforded petitioner in his commitment.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of habeas corpus, discharged the order detaining and committing petitioner, a ward of the juvenile court, from confinement in a state mental hospital and remanded petitioner to the juvenile court because statutory commitment procedures, which applied to mentally disordered minors who were also a ward of the juvenile court, were not followed.

 

12 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


United Farm Workers v. Superior Court of Santa Cruz County


S.F. No. 23223


Supreme Court of California


14 Cal. 3d 902; 537 P.2d 1237; 122 Cal. Rptr. 877; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 328; 89 L.R.R.M. 3157



July 28, 1975 



DISPOSITION:  The alternative writ of prohibition, having served its purpose, is discharged, and the peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner union members challenged an ex parte restraining order issued by respondent Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California) claiming it violated the freedom of speech clauses of U.S. Const. amend. I, XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 2 and requested a writ of prohibition commanding respondent from enforcing the order. The order required petitioners to refrain from mass picketing of fruit company and from trespassing upon its lands.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest, fruit company, obtained an ex parte temporary restraining order from respondent Superior Court of Santa Cruz County that barred petitioner union members from mass picketing at real party's property and from trespassing thereon. Petitioners sought a writ of prohibition requiring respondent to refrain from enforcing or continuing in effect the temporary restraining order on grounds that they were not given an opportunity to be heard. The court agreed with petitioners and overruled the order although it had already served its purpose, holding that because the case concerned the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I and Cal. Const. art. I, § 2, it was of broad public interest, and that such valuable rights needed protection from unnecessary abridgement. The court held that real party made no effort to inform petitioners of the hearing, and because there was a shortage of legal and factual issues at the hearing because of petitioners' absence, an ex parte restraining order should not have been issued unless a showing was made that it was not reasonably possible to notify petitioners or their counsel and afford them an opportunity to be heard.


OUTCOME: The court found that the ex parte order violated the federal and state constitutional freedom of speech rights guaranteed petitioner union members but the alternative writ of prohibition, having served its purpose, was discharged, and the preemptory writ was denied. The court held that ex parte restraining orders should not be issued unless the opposing parties cannot reasonably be located and afforded an opportunity to be heard.

 

13 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Knodel v. Knodel


L.A. No. 30083


Supreme Court of California


14 Cal. 3d 752; 537 P.2d 353; 122 Cal. Rptr. 521; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 318



July 16, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied August 20, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 322172, William A. Yale, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-husband sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which awarded a sum to respondent ex-wife for arrearages for unpaid spousal support and denied appellant's motion to terminate such support.


OVERVIEW: Respondent ex-wife and appellant ex-husband entered into an agreement in Virginia which set out an amount of spousal support to be paid by appellant. The parties were subsequently divorced in Virginia. In an action instituted in California, respondent was awarded a sum by the trial court for arrearages for spousal support to be paid by appellant, and appellant's motion to terminate such support was denied. The court affirmed first holding that appellant's appeal was timely filed when measured against the order which the court found to be the trial court's final judgment. The court also held that the contractual provisions for spousal support at the time of the divorce decree in Virginia were not subject to modification under the prevailing statute as construed by the high court in that state. The court held that at the time the action of this case was commenced, California law would have allowed spouses to enter into particular agreements for their support which could not be judicially modified. The court held that comity thus required that the Virginia agreement be given full force and effect.


OUTCOME: Judgment awarding respondent ex-wife arrearages for spousal support unpaid by appellant ex-husband and denying appellant's motion to terminate such support affirmed because the contractual provisions for spousal support at the time of the divorce decree in Virginia were not subject to modification under the prevailing statute and this agreement was given full force and effect in California.

 

14 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re M.


Crim. No. 18621


Supreme Court of California


14 Cal. 3d 727; 537 P.2d 406; 122 Cal. Rptr. 574; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 316



July 14, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petitions of both parties for a rehearing were denied August 13, 1975, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 453584, William P. Hogoboom and Thomas Wilfred LeSage, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The order denying a rehearing is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A referee, acting without the consent of a minor or his counsel, found that the minor had committed a criminal act and made orders purporting to declare the minor a ward of the juvenile court under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 and to remove him from his home. The minor filed for a de novo rehearing before the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied the application, and the minor appealed.


OVERVIEW: A referee declared a minor a ward of the juvenile court under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 and sought to remove him from his home. A judge denied the minor's application for a de novo rehearing, and the minor appealed. The Supreme Court of California reversed the judge's order. Actual review of and action upon the application by the judge within the time prescribed was necessary to prevent the referee's acts from exceeding the constitutional limitations restricting such acts to subordinate judicial duties and effectuate legislative intent. The court treated the application as a case in which the proceedings were not taken down by an official reporter, so that the application for rehearing was granted as of right, and the expiration of the time for action on the minor's application would cause it to be automatically granted. The order of the court denying a rehearing was appealable as an order after judgment. The instant ruling did not otherwise affect the validity of similar orders made by a juvenile court judge prior to the filing of the opinion of which an application for rehearing was received by the court 45 or more days before the date of filing of the opinion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judge's order which had denied the minor's application for a de novo rehearing. Actual review of and action upon the application by the judge within the time prescribed was necessary to prevent the referee's acts from exceeding the constitutional limitations restricting such acts to subordinate judicial duties and effectuate legislative intent to limit the period during which such an application could remain pending.

 

15 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Gould v. Grubb


L.A. No. 30411


Supreme Court of California


14 Cal. 3d 661; 536 P.2d 1337; 122 Cal. Rptr. 377; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 313



July 7, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On July 25, 1975, the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. WEC 28345, Richard L. Wells, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, city and incumbent, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held that it was constitutionally impermissible to reserve the top ballot spot for incumbents in an election, and struck down the Santa Monica Charter provision embodying the procedure.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city had an election procedure which afforded an incumbent seeking reelection a top position on the election ballot. Respondents, candidates, sought to declare the procedure unconstitutional. The trial court found the procedure constitutionally impermissible and struck down the Santa Monica Charter provision which embodied the procedure. The court affirmed, holding that the positional preference violated the equal protection clause of the state and federal constitutions. The court likewise held that an alphabetical listing of candidates would result in disparate treatment and was therefore unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that it was constitutionally impermissible to reserve the top ballot spot for incumbents in an election, holding that such a preference violated the equal protection clause of the state and federal constitutions.

 

16 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Rodriguez


Crim. No. 18044


Supreme Court of California


14 Cal. 3d 639; 537 P.2d 384; 122 Cal. Rptr. 552; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 312



June 30, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied July 30, 1975. Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The writ is granted. Respondent is directed to discharge petitioner from custody upon the finality of this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate, who had served 22 years of an indeterminate sentence of one year to life for violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288, sought a writ of habeas corpus to obtain his release from prison.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner inmate was convicted of violating Cal. Penal Code § 288. Petitioner was sentenced to an indeterminate sentence of one year to life. After petitioner had served 22 years of his sentence, he sought a writ of habeas corpus to obtain his release from prison. Petitioner contended that the statutory life maximum term was disproportionate to the offense and, thus, violated both U.S. Const. amend. VIII and Cal. Const. art. I, § 17. Petitioner further argued that the 22 years he had served was likewise excessive punishment and that the Adult Authority abused its discretion both in failing to fix a lesser term than the statutory life maximum term and in failing to grant petitioner parole. The court concluded that the penalty provision of Cal. Penal Code § 288 was constitutional on its face, but it held that the Adult Authority failed to properly interpret and administer the Indeterminate Sentence Law, Cal. Penal Code § 1168. Therefore, the court held that petitioner was entitled to be discharged from his term because he had already served a term that was disproportionate to his offense.


OUTCOME: The court held that petitioner inmate had already served a term that was disproportionate to his offense and, therefore, was entitled to be discharged from his term of one year to life.

 

17 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Beaudreau v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L.A. No. 30360


Supreme Court of California


14 Cal. 3d 448; 535 P.2d 713; 121 Cal. Rptr. 585; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 296



May 29, 1975 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a group of public school students and parents, filed a petition for a writ of mandate to compel the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to permit prosecution of petitioner's action against defendants, a school district and its officials, under the Elementary and Secondary Education Act of 1965, 20 U.S.C.S. § 241(a) et seq., without requiring petitioners to post an undertaking under Cal. Gov't Code §§ 947 and 951.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, a group of public school students and parents, filed a claim against defendants, a school district and its officials, alleging a violation of the Elementary and Secondary Education Act of 1965, 20 U.S.C.S. § 241(a) et seq. Defendants required petitioners to post an undertaking as security for allowable costs under Cal. Gov't Code §§ 947 and 951. The trial court ordered petitioners to post the undertaking within 20 days or face dismissal. Petitioners sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent trial court to permit prosecution of the action without the undertaking, alleging that it violated due process. The court issued the writ, holding that §§ 947 and 951, which were designed to protect public entities against frivolous lawsuits, violated U.S. Const. amends. V, XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 7 and 15. The statutes constituted a taking of property because petitioner was forced to comply or incur a dismissal. Petitioners' claim against defendant was a protectable property interest. A due process hearing was needed to evaluate the merit of petitioners' action as well as the reasonableness of the undertaking.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate compelling respondent trial court to permit petitioners, a group of public school students and parents, to continue a pending action against defendants, a school district and its officials, without posting an undertaking as security for defendants' projected costs of litigation because the applicable undertaking statutes violated due process due to the absence of a hearing requirement.

 

18 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Crockett v. Superior Court of Santa Clara County


S.F. No. 23240


Supreme Court of California


14 Cal. 3d 433; 535 P.2d 321; 121 Cal. Rptr. 457; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 295



May 28, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' application for a rehearing was denied June 26, 1975. Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The petition for the writ is denied and the alternative writ is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought a writ of mandate, alleging denial of their constitutional right to a speedy trial on robbery charges pending against them in respondent Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California).


OVERVIEW: While petitioners were incarcerated in one county, they demanded a speedy trial on robbery charges in another county. When more than 90 days had elapsed since their demands, petitioners filed a motion to dismiss the pending charges for denial of a speedy trial. Respondent trial court granted the motion, and the prosecution filed a new complaint charging commission of the same robberies. Petitioners again sought to dismiss the charges for denial of a speedy trial, but their motion was denied. Petitioners sought a writ of mandate to compel the dismissal of the robbery charges, alleging denial of their constitutional right to a speedy trial. The court denied the petition for a writ of mandate, as petitioners were not denied a speedy trial because they were not prejudiced by the delays that occurred before their arraignment. The court found that no presumption of prejudice existed, as the prosecution was not accountable for any extended delay beyond the 90-day period, and 100 of the 194 days that elapsed after their demands were directly chargeable to petitioners.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioners' request for a writ of mandate based on denial of their constitutional right to a speedy trial of robbery charges pending against them in respondent trial court, as petitioners demonstrated no prejudice against them because of the delays.

 

19 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Burnick


Crim. No. 16554


Supreme Court of California


14 Cal. 3d 306; 535 P.2d 352; 121 Cal. Rptr. 488; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 287



May 15, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied June 11, 1975. Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County. No. M 231566, Donald F. Pitts, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which determined that he was a mentally disordered sex offender within the meaning of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6300 and committed him to a state psychiatric facility for an indeterminate period.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of annoying or molesting a person under the age of 18, Cal. Penal Code § 647a, and contributing to the delinquency of a person under age 21, Cal. Penal Code § 272. In addition, the trial court certified defendant to the superior court for a hearing to determine whether he was a mentally disordered sex offender within the meaning of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6300. The superior court conducted a hearing and determined, by a preponderance of the evidence, that defendant was a mentally disordered sex offender and committed him to a psychiatric facility for an indeterminate period. Defendant appealed, arguing that the proper standard of proof in such a proceeding was proof beyond a reasonable doubt. The court reversed, holding that the due process clauses of Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a) and U.S. Const. amend. XIV required a standard of proof of beyond a reasonable doubt in mentally disordered sex offender proceedings under § 6300. The court determined that the higher standard of proof was required because a finding that a person was a mentally disordered sex offender resulted in significant deprivation of the person's liberty and stigmatization of the person.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order adjudging defendant to be a mentally disordered sex offender because the due process clauses of the California and United States Constitutions required such a finding to be made beyond a reasonable doubt and the superior court made the finding based on a mere preponderance of the evidence.

 

20 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Feagley


Crim. No. 16818


Supreme Court of California


14 Cal. 3d 338; 535 P.2d 373; 121 Cal. Rptr. 509; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 288



May 15, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied June 11, 1975. Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 150202, Robert E. Carey, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which deemed him a mentally disordered sex offender under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6300 who would not benefit by care or treatment in a state hospital. The trial court had committed defendant to the department of mental hygiene for an indefinite period.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with molesting after he stroked the hair of two young girls. The charge was dismissed when he entered a guilty plea to simple battery. Investigation disclosed that defendant had a history of touching women's hair. As a result, the trial court adjourned the criminal proceedings prior to sentencing and instituted the statutory procedure for determining if defendant was a mentally disordered sex offender under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6300 et seq. The jury found that defendant was a mentally disordered sex offender who would not benefit by care or treatment in a state hospital. He was then committed for an indefinite period in the department of mental hygiene. Defendant appealed, contending he was illegally committed to an institution in which adequate treatment was not furnished. The court reversed the order committing defendant, finding that it was error for the trial court to confine defendant for an indefinite period as the court had to presume that the term of imprisonment prescribed by the legislature for an underlying crime committed by a mentally disordered sex offender was adequate to protect society against a potential reoccurrence.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court was reversed because the statutory scheme, which provided for defendant's confinement as a mentally disordered sex offender, violated the cruel and unusual punishment clauses of both the California and federal constitutions as the state could not involuntarily confine a civilly committed mentally disordered sex offender for an indefinite period in a prison setting.

 

21 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wingo


Crim. No. 17942


Supreme Court of California


14 Cal. 3d 169; 534 P.2d 1001; 121 Cal. Rptr. 97; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 284



May 9, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied June 4, 1975. Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A292016, David N. Fitts, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his sentence following his conviction on charges of assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) on the ground that the punishment imposed was cruel or unusual in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 17 and U.S. Const. amends. VIII and XIV.


OVERVIEW: Defendant assaulted an elderly man by repeatedly kicking his head and upper body and was convicted of assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury. He was sentenced to an indeterminate sentence of six months to life and contended on appeal that his punishment was disproportionate to his offense and, thus, was unconstitutionally cruel or unusual. The court concluded that there could be circumstances, as yet undefined, when imposition of a life-maximum sentence for violation of Cal. Penal Code § 245(a) would constitute cruel or unusual punishment and held that when a defendant convicted under a section encompassing a wide range of conduct challenges the statute as imposing cruel or unusual punishment, judicial review had to await an initial determination by the Adult Authority of the proper term in the individual case. When the term was fixed, a court could then analyze the constitutionality of the statute as applied.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury was affirmed on the basis that it was premature to decide whether defendant's prison term would be disproportionate to his offense. Only when a prison term was set, could defendant utilize the remedy of habeas corpus to determine if the penalty was excessive under the facts and circumstances of the crime of which he stood convicted.

 

22 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Romo


Crim. No. 17941


Supreme Court of California


14 Cal. 3d 189; 534 P.2d 1015; 121 Cal. Rptr. 111; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 285



May 9, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied June 4, 1975. Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 50380, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from his conviction for assault with a deadly weapon in violation of Cal. Penal. Code. § 245(a) that was entered in the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California). Defendant contended that jury instruction issues related to accomplice testimony, absence of motive, and oral admissions were erroneously decided. Defendant also contended that the punishment scheme of the statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had been drinking with the victim and another individual in celebration of the birth of a child. An argument ensued over the victim's inability to drive defendant home. This argument escalated into a fight, and defendant stabbed the victim several times. Defendant and the other man fled but were soon apprehended. The other man gave a tape-recorded statement to the police that implicated defendant. This statement was admitted at trial over defendant's objection. The trial court did not instruct on a lack of motive, and it did not give an accomplice instruction. Defendant was convicted of assault with a deadly weapon, and he appealed. The court adopted the opinion of the trial court and affirmed the conviction. The court did note that defendant's attack on the constitutionality of punishment portion of the statute was premature because the maximum term had not been determined. The court concluded that once the maximum penalty had been assessed defendant could pursue the habeas corpus remedy to assert that the sentence was disproportionate under the facts and circumstances of the crime and violative of the U.S. Const. amend. VIII.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of assault with a deadly weapon. The court adopted the opinion of the trial court in all respects but additionally found that defendant's argument as to the sentence being cruel and unusual punishment was dismissed as premature.

People v. Ruiz


Crim. No. 17732


Supreme Court of California


14 Cal. 3d 163; 534 P.2d 712; 120 Cal. Rptr. 872; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 283



May 7, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Imperial County, No. 9494, Victor A. Gillespie, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The trial court is ordered to modify the verdict to provide that defendant is found guilty of one count of possession of heroin in violation of section 11350 of the Health and Safety Code, and to then proceed with resentencing in accordance with the views herein expressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Imperial County (California) that convicted defendant of possession of heroin for sale in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11351. Appellant alleged several errors related to the jury's finding of illicit and knowing possession also concerned the weight to be given his prior narcotics conviction.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was arrested when police officers saw him throw a wad of paper from his vehicle that contained heroin. Although the amount could have been for personal use, the lack of paraphernalia for injection caused police officer to think that appellant possessed the heroin for sale. Appellant was convicted of possession of heroin for sale, and he sought review. He argued that three issues related to the jury's finding of illicit and knowing possession, which were all without any merit. He also raised contentions pertaining to his prior narcotics conviction and the effect it was given. Prosecutor admitted that the evidence did not demonstrate the specific intent necessary to convict appellant of possession for sale and suggested that the court modify the conviction to one of possession for heroin because the evidence did prove that appellant possessed heroin, jury had been instructed on this offense, and there were no other substantial legal issues. The court agreed and reversed the judgment and directed the trial court to modify the verdict to reflect that appellant was found guilty of one count of possession in violation of Cal. Health & Safety. Code. § 11350.


OUTCOME: The conviction for possession of heroin for sale was reversed and trial court was ordered to modify verdict to provide that appellant was found guilty of one count of possession of heroin and to proceed with resentencing. The judgment of conviction was modified as prosecutor informed the court that evidence did not prove specific intent necessary to support possession for sale conviction, but it did prove that appellant possessed heroin.

 

24 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Earley


Crim. No. 17574


Supreme Court of California


14 Cal. 3d 122; 534 P.2d 721; 120 Cal. Rptr. 881; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 281



May 1, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied June 4, 1975. Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 65274, E. Warren McGuire, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: State appealed an order of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which granted petitioner a writ of habeas corpus, contending that the trial court erred in determining that petitioner's conduct did not constitute the crime of kidnapping as prohibited by Cal. Penal Code § 209.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was found guilty on one count of kidnapping for the purpose of robbery and one count of first degree robbery and was sentenced to prison on each count. Thereafter, the court of appeals held that a defendant was entitled to habeas corpus relief if there was no material dispute as to the facts relating to his conviction and if it appeared that the statute under which he was convicted did not prohibit his conduct. The trial court determined that petitioner's conduct did not violate Cal. Penal Code § 209, and state appealed. The court reversed and held that movements to facilitate crimes that were for a substantial distance rather than brief were not incidental thereto and that a movement of 10 to 13 blocks was substantial. Therefore, the movement was not merely incidental to the commission of a robbery even though it may have been solely to facilitate the commission of a robbery because petitioner's asportation of his victim gave rise to dangers not inherent in robbery.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order that granted petitioner a writ of habeas corpus because his conduct was not prohibited by state law on the ground that the trial court erred in determining, as a matter of law, that petitioner's conduct did not constitute kidnapping because petitioner's movement of victim 10 to 13 blocks was not incidental to the commission of the crime of robbery.

 

25 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Orin


Crim. No. 17731


Supreme Court of California


13 Cal. 3d 937; 533 P.2d 193; 120 Cal. Rptr. 65; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 217



April 4, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied May 1, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-124971, Charles M. Hughes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order dismissing counts I and II of the information is reversed. The cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to grant defendant leave to withdraw his plea of guilty to count III, if he so move.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), dismissing two counts of a three-count information.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with attempted robbery, burglary, and assault with a deadly weapon. When the case was called for trial, the prosecutor announced ready to proceed on all counts and that any plea to the third count was unacceptable. Defendant offered to plead guilty to the third count. The trial court accepted defendant's plea and, at sentencing, dismissed the first two counts in the interests of justice. The court noted that the trial court had no authority to substitute itself as the representative of the state in the plea bargaining process and to agree to a disposition of the case over prosecutorial objection. The dismissal of the first two counts was held to be manifestly invalid because it failed to comply with the statutory requirement that the reasons for the dismissal be set forth. The trial court's action was found to be a "package disposition," resulting in more lenient treatment of defendant without any showing that it was in furtherance of justice. The court held that the dismissal was not in the furtherance of justice and constituted an abuse of discretion. The order dismissing the counts was reversed and the case remanded.


OUTCOME: The order dismissing two counts of the information was reversed because it was invalid on its face and constituted an abuse of discretion. The cause was remanded to the trial court with directions to grant defendant leave to withdraw his guilty plea to the third count should he so choose.

 

26 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


San Francisco v. Cooper


S.F. No. 23210


Supreme Court of California


13 Cal. 3d 898; 534 P.2d 403; 120 Cal. Rptr. 707; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 215; 89 L.R.R.M. 2262



April 4, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' application for a rehearing was denied May 1, 1975, and the judgment was modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: Let a writ of mandate issue, compelling the respondent controller to draw and deliver warrants reflecting the salary increases granted by ordinance No. 152-74 and resolution No. 44-9-Sp 1 as well as prejudgment interest at the legal rate on the withheld salary increases. ( Civ. Code, § 3287, subd. (a); see Sanders v. City of Los Angeles (1970) 3 Cal.3d 252, 262-263 [90 Cal. Rptr. 169, 475 P.2d 201].)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, city and school district, filed a petition for writ of mandate that sought an order compelling respondent city controller to implement newly enacted measures that provided for salary increases for city employees and teachers employed by petitioner school district. Respondent taxpayer had filed two separate actions seeking to enjoin enactment of the two measures because of questions about their validity.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, city and school district, enacted an ordinance and a resolution that called for salary increases for city employees and certificated school district employees. Prior to enactment of the ordinance enacting a salary increase for city employees, the employees had participated in a strike against respondent city. Respondent taxpayer filed an action seeking enjoinment of the enactment of the ordinance and the resolution, claiming that the ordinance was enacted by respondent city in response to the strike by city employees. Respondent taxpayer also argued that the resolution regarding salary increases for teachers was violative of the city charter. In response to these actions, respondent city controller refused to implement the ordinance and the resolution. Petitioners sought a writ of mandate, which was granted. The court held that because of separation of powers concerns, it could not sit in judgment of an act of a legislative body, even if the act complained of was in response to an illegal strike. The court further held that the resolution providing for increased teacher salaries was enacted after all board members had conferred regarding the resolution.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ of mandate compelling respondent city controller to draw up salary increases, as enacted by petitioners city and school district, for employees of petitioners. The court held that even if the salary increase for employees of petitioner city was due to the pressures of an illegal strike, separation of powers concerns precluded the court from sitting in judgment of the acts of a legislative body.

 

27 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Wilson v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L.A. No. 30316


Supreme Court of California


13 Cal. 3d 652; 532 P.2d 116; 119 Cal. Rptr. 468; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 197



March 4, 1975 



DISPOSITION:  Let the peremptory writ issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner candidate sought a writ of prohibition to prevent respondent trial court, the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), from enforcing a restraining order and injunction it issued on behalf of real-party-in-interest opposing candidate. The orders enjoined petitioner's publication of newsletters concerning real party in interest's bribery prosecution.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner candidate published newsletters concerning real-party-in-interest opposing candidate's indictment and trial for bribery charges. Real-party-in-interest brought a libel action and sought an injunction to restrain petitioner from publishing the newsletter, which was granted. After modifications, deemed unsatisfactory by respondent trial court, petitioner brought this action for a writ of prohibition to prevent respondent from enforcing the restraining order and injunction. The court issued the preemptory writ, and held that prior restraints on publications which referred to malfeasance of public officers had long been held violative of U.S. Const. amend. I. Further, the court noted, this was so whether or not the statement was false and misleading. Therefore, since there was no doubt the subject preliminary injunction constituted a prior restraint on petitioner's publishing his newsletter, it could not be sustained because the court believed the contents of the newsletter to be untrue. Consequently, the injunction violated petitioner's rights under the U.S. and California constitutions and had to be annulled.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the preemptory writ to issue restraining respondent trial court from enforcing the restraining order and injunction it issued on behalf of real-party-in-interest opposing candidate because the preliminary injunction was a prior restraint on petitioner candidate's free expression and was, therefore, violative of the U.S. and California constitutions.

In re Lisa R.


S.F. No. 23204


Supreme Court of California


13 Cal. 3d 636; 532 P.2d 123; 119 Cal. Rptr. 475; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 196; 90 A.L.R.3d 1017



March 3, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 29224, Joseph A. De Cristoforo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed insofar as it holds that the juvenile court lacks jurisdiction to determine parentage and that appellant has no standing to offer proof that he is the minor's father. 18 The judgment is affirmed in all other respects. Appellant is to recover his costs on appeal.



18 The order appealed from provides for other routine matters relative to Lisa's dependency status which were not placed in issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant alleged father appealed from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which ruled at a dependency hearing pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 600, 729 that the juvenile court did not have jurisdiction to determine paternity of the minor child and that appellant had no standing in the matter.


OVERVIEW: Appellant alleged father appeared at a dependency review hearing of a minor child pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 600, 729. The court ordered that it did not have jurisdiction to determine paternity and that appellant had no standing in the matter. The court held that the juvenile court had jurisdiction to determine paternity of a minor and the juvenile court erred in concluding that it lacked jurisdiction to make that determination. The court also held that the minor and respondent county welfare department were not collaterally estopped to deny that appellant was the minor's natural father. The court held that a presumption which precluded appellant in the instant circumstances a right to offer evidence to prove that he was the father of the minor child was unreasonable, arbitrary and capricious, and a denial of due process under U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court reversed the order of the juvenile court insofar as it held that the juvenile court lacked jurisdiction to determine parentage and that appellant had no standing to offer proof that he was the minor's father. The judgment was affirmed in all other respects.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the juvenile court's determination that it did not have standing to determine paternity and that appellant alleged father had no standing in the matter and the court affirmed the order in all other respects. The court held that denial of appellant's right to offer evidence to prove that he was the father of the minor child was a denial of due process under the Fourteenth Amendment.

 

29 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court of Santa Barbara County


L.A. No. 30178


Supreme Court of California


13 Cal. 3d 430; 531 P.2d 761; 119 Cal. Rptr. 193; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 178



January 29, 1975 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' application for a rehearing was denied February 26, 1975. McComb, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: In light of the above conclusion, petitioner's request for a writ of prohibition restraining the superior court from reviewing any grand jury report prior to filing is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On petition for rehearing, respondent Superior Court of Santa Barbara County sought review of the determination of the Court of Appeal (California) that superior courts had no authority under any circumstances to review a proposed grand jury report prior to filing. Petitioner county prosecutor's request for a writ of prohibition restraining respondent from reviewing any grand jury report prior to filing had been granted.


OVERVIEW: The Santa Barbara County Grand Jury had prepared and filed a preliminary report under its "watchdog" authority that sharply criticized the county pathologist for irregularities in financial and other matters in the county hospital's pathology department. After reviewing the report, respondent Superior Court of Santa Barbara County instructed the grand jury to submit all final reports to respondent for approval prior to filing. Petitioner district attorney requested a writ of mandate and prohibition to restrain respondent from requiring the grand jury to submit all reports to the presiding judge for his approval prior to filing. The request was granted. Respondent petitioned for rehearing, and the court reversed the holding of the court of appeal and denied the writ. The court held that although California statutes had established a broad realm in which the grand jury was authorized to investigate and report, the grand jury's domain was not unlimited; if a proposed grand jury report exceeded established legal limits, the superior court that convened the grand jury and was responsible for its supervision could properly refuse to file the report.


OUTCOME: On rehearing, petitioner prosecutor's request for a writ of prohibition restraining respondent Superior Court of Santa Barbara County from reviewing any grand jury report prior to filing was denied by the court. The court reasoned that if a proposed grand jury report exceeded established legal limits, a superior court which convened the grand jury and which was responsible for its supervision could properly refuse to file the report.

Santa Barbara School Dist. v. Superior Court of Santa Barbara County


L.A. Nos. 30054, 30086


Supreme Court of California


13 Cal. 3d 315; 530 P.2d 605; 118 Cal. Rptr. 637; 1975 Cal. LEXIS 173



January 15, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 96260, John T. Rickard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In L.A. 30054 let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue in accordance with the views herein expressed.

In L.A. 30086 the judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court with direction to enter judgment in favor of defendants on the first and second stated causes of action set forth in plaintiffs' complaint.

Petitioners shall recover costs in L.A. 30054 and defendants shall recover costs in L.A. 30086.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a class action, petitioner school districts and others appealed from and sought a writ of prohibition against respondent court, which had been called upon to determine the validity of a desegregation plan for elementary schools. Also before the court was the validity of the pertinent state statute permitting petitioner board of education to be the common governing board of the high school district and the elementary school district.


OVERVIEW: Before the court was the validity of a desegregation plan for elementary schools. Also before the court was the validity of the pertinent state statute permitting petitioner board of education to be the common governing board of the high school district and the elementary school district. The court held that petitioner was not entitled to a peremptory writ of prohibition restraining respondent court from enjoining the implementation of the Administration Plan for failure of petitioner to comply with the statute at issue but was entitled to a writ restraining respondent's intended action in all other respects. The second issue before the court was reversed and remanded. The court held there was no constitutional infirmity in a system where the legislature designated an elected city board of education to govern a lesser-included elementary school district. The court held that the present method of electing members to petitioner board was not violative of either the United States Constitution or the California Constitution and was valid under applicable state law.


OUTCOME: The court held, in part, that petitioner board of education was not entitled to a peremptory writ of prohibition restraining respondent court from enjoining the implementation of the Administration Plan for failure of petitioner to comply with the statute at issue but was entitled to a writ restraining respondent's intended action in all other respects.

Builders Assn. of Santa Clara-Santa Cruz Counties v. Superior Court of Santa Clara County


S.F. No. 23085


Supreme Court of California


13 Cal. 3d 225; 529 P.2d 582; 118 Cal. Rptr. 158; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 203



December 26, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied February 19, 1975. Files, J., * sat in place of Mosk, J., who deemed himself disqualified. McComb, J., and Clark, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: We conclude that the initiative zoning ordinance enacted by the voters of the charter city of San Jose violates no provision of the state or federal Constitutions. The alternative writ of mandate heretofore issued is therefore discharged, and the petition for writ of mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff association sought a writ of mandate to respondent Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which entered judgment for defendant charter city of San Jose in an action to enjoin enforcement of a zoning ordinance. The court issued an alternative writ of mandate to consider plaintiff's contention that the residents of a charter city could not constitutionally enact a zoning ordinance by initiative.


OVERVIEW: The voters of defendant charter city of San Jose enacted an initiative ordinance to restrict the zoning of land for residential use for a two-year period pending further study of the impact of residential development. The ordinance prohibited the rezoning of land for residential development within impacted areas of the city unless the school districts in which the land was situated certified that the party seeking rezoning had agreed to provide a satisfactory alternative to permanent school construction. Plaintiff association brought an action in respondent superior court to enjoin enforcement of the ordinance. Respondent entered judgment for defendants, and plaintiff sought a writ of mandate. The court held that the ordinance did not unconstitutionally limit the power of the city council to rezone property, unlawfully delegate zoning authority to the school district, establish unreasonable classifications in violation of the equal protection clause, or interfere with the constitutional right to travel. The court thus denied plaintiff's petition for writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the alternative writ of mandate it had issued, and denied plaintiff association's petition for a writ of mandate to respondent superior court. The court found no constitutional impediment to the enactment of zoning measures by initiative and held that the initiative zoning ordinance enacted by the voters of defendant charter city of San Jose violated no provision of the state or federal constitutions.

 

32 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


San Diego Building Contractors Assn. v. City Council of San Diego


L.A. No. 30250


Supreme Court of California


13 Cal. 3d 205; 529 P.2d 570; 118 Cal. Rptr. 146; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 202; 72 A.L.R.3d 973



December 26, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' petition for a rehearing was denied February 19, 1975. Files, J., * sat in place of Mosk, J., who deemed himself disqualified. McComb, J., and Clark, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 338110, Louis M. Welsh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city council appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiff building contractor associations in an action claiming that the city charter and the due process clause of the federal constitution precluded defendant from adopting a zoning ordinance without affording notice and hearing to affected property owners.


OVERVIEW: The city voters enacted an ordinance that established a height limitation for buildings constructed within a prescribed coastal zone, pursuant to an initiative procedure set forth in the city charter. Plaintiff building contractor associations challenged the ordinance as violative of the federal and state due process guarantees, in the absence of notice and hearing. The trial court granted summary judgment to plaintiffs, and defendant city appealed. The court reversed, holding that plaintiffs' due process argument lacked foundation. The city charter authorized the enactment of the challenged ordinance through the initiative process, and the "due process" clause of the federal and state constitutions authorized general zoning legislation without affording affected landowners notice and hearing. Due process "notice and hearing" was mandated only in quasi-judicial or adjudicatory settings, not for the adoption of general legislation, and the city ordinance challenged here was unquestionably a general legislative act, notwithstanding the potential impact on real property values.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment to plaintiff building contractor associations in their challenge to a zoning ordinance enacted pursuant to defendant city's charter. Because the ordinance was neither quasi-judicial nor adjudicatory in nature, the due process requirements of "notice and hearing" to affected property owners prior to enactment of the ordinance was not required.
