Morse v. Municipal Court for San Jose-Milpitas Judicial Dist.


S.F. No. 23115


Supreme Court of California


13 Cal. 3d 149; 529 P.2d 46; 118 Cal. Rptr. 14; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 200



December 17, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  Let the preemptory writ of mandate issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner drug defendant sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent, the Municipal Court for the San Jose-Milpitas Judicial District of Santa Clara County (California), to divert him from criminal prosecution to a special rehabilitative treatment center afforded to first-time drug offenders pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 1000-1000.3.


OVERVIEW: Respondent trial court denied petitioner drug defendant's request to be considered for a diversion program under Cal. Penal Code §§ 1000-1000.3 as untimely because petitioner had filed an unsuccessful suppression motion and had elected to be tried and not diverted. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate and challenged that interpretation of the statute, claiming that it unconstitutionally forced him to relinquish his Fourth Amendment rights. The court issued a writ of mandate to compel respondent to refer petitioner for diversion. The court found that the statute only required a prompt waiver of speedy trial and that defendants eligible for diversion could tender usual pretrial motions prior to their expression of consent to consideration for diversion. The court found the legislative purpose to be very liberal with the twofold purpose of sparing appropriately selected first-offenders the stigma of a criminal record and relieving the congested criminal courts of some relatively minor drug abuse prosecutions. The attachment of a condition that a motion to suppress be deferred until after diversion contradicted the intent of the legislature. The constitutional issue was not reached.


OUTCOME: The court issued the preemptory writ of mandate to require respondent trial court to allow defendant to consent to diversion and to refer the case to the probation department for investigation because the court examined the legislative intent to create a liberal diversion program and interpreted the statute to allow defendants to file pretrial motions before consenting to diversion.

 

34 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Glaire v. La Lanne-Paris Health Spa


L.A. No. 30315


Supreme Court of California


12 Cal. 3d 915; 528 P.2d 357; 117 Cal. Rptr. 541; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 275



December 2, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On December 26, 1974, the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 993878, David A. Thomas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff consumer sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that granted defendant health club and finance companies' demurrers in a class action lawsuit that alleged defendants violated that portion of the Consumer Credit Protection Act commonly known as Truth-in-Lending, 15 U.S.C.S. § 1601 et seq., and exacted interest in excess of the legal rate prescribed in Cal. Const. art. XX, § 22.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff consumer purchased a membership from defendant health club. The price of the membership was $ 408 regardless of whether it was paid in cash or paid in monthly installments. Defendant health club sold the installment contract to defendant finance company and received $ 255 in cash. Defendants had common ownership. Plaintiff brought a class action against defendants alleging violations of Truth in Lending, 15 U.S.C.S. § 1601 et seq., and usury, Cal. Const. art. XX, § 22, violations. On appeal, the court reversed the trial court's order that sustained defendants' demurrers. The court held that when a merchant and a finance company maintained an intimate and continuing relationship centering around the routine discounting of installment contracts, both parties acquired an obligation to disclose the amount of the standard discount as a finance charge payable by the consumer even when the consumer was not given the opportunity to pay a lower cash price. The court also held that the trial court erred when it precluded plaintiff from offering evidence to establish that defendants' practice of discounting notes operated to conceal the imposition of usurious interest charges.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court that sustained the demurrers of defendants, health club and finance company, in plaintiff consumer's action for Truth and Lending violations and usury because defendants had an obligation to disclose the amount of the standard discount as a finance charge and whether the transaction violated the usury law was a question of fact.

 

35 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Leaming v. Municipal Court for San Jose-Milpitas Judicial Dist.


S.F. No. 23038


Supreme Court of California


12 Cal. 3d 813; 528 P.2d 745; 117 Cal. Rptr. 657; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 264



November 22, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  The petition for writ of prohibition is denied. 3



3 Petitioner's contention that he has been denied a speedy trial lacks merit. He was arraigned on a misdemeanor charge of indecent exposure on 21 November 1972. After a number of continuances, trial was set for 13 February 1973. On that date the misdemeanor complaint was abandoned and petitioner was arraigned on this felony complaint. Petitioner may not complain of the fact that the misdemeanor trial was set more than 45 days after his arraignment, because the continuances were at his request. ( Pen. Code, § 1382.) Nor may petitioner complain of the filing of the felony complaint. Dismissal of a misdemeanor complaint does not bar a felony prosecution. ( Pen. Code, § 1387.) There is no evidence in the record to support petitioner's claim that the felony complaint was filed because he refused to plead guilty to the misdemeanor charge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner criminal defendant sought to restrain respondent, the Municipal Court for the San Jose-Milpitas Judicial District of Santa Clara County (California), by writ of prohibition from conducting a preliminary hearing. Petitioner was charged with indecent exposure in a felony complaint alleging three prior convictions of the same offense in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 314.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner criminal defendant was charged with indecent exposure in a felony complaint alleging prior convictions of the same offense in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 314. Petitioner sought a writ of prohibition to restrain respondent, the Municipal Court for the San Jose-Milpitas Judicial District of Santa Clara County, from conducting a preliminary hearing, contending that indecent exposure was a misdemeanor, regardless of prior convictions. He argued that the provision of § 314 making repeated offenses felonies was invalidated entirely when the indeterminate life-maximum sentence for such offenses was declared unconstitutional. The court denied the writ, holding that only the indeterminate term was invalidated and not the entire recidivist provision. The court ruled that the remainder of the recidivist provision was complete in itself. The court determined that the recidivist provision would have been adopted without the severed portion because the legislature clearly indicated that if any part of § 314 was invalidated, such invalidity should not affect other provisions which could be given effect without the invalid provision.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for writ of prohibition and held that petitioner criminal defendant was properly charged with felonious indecent exposure and prior convictions of the same offense. The court held that the recidivist portion making repeated offenses felonies was complete in itself and remained valid when the indeterminate life-maximum sentence was declared unconstitutional and severed from the provision.

 

36 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Fire Fighters Union v. City of Vallejo


S.F. No. 23098


Supreme Court of California


12 Cal. 3d 608; 526 P.2d 971; 116 Cal. Rptr. 507; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 249; 87 L.R.R.M. 2453



October 2, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, No. 53187, Ellis R. Randall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We affirm the judgment as herein modified and remand the case to the superior court with directions to issue a writ of mandamus requiring the City of Vallejo to proceed to arbitrate the issues of "Reduction of Personnel," "Vacancies and Promotions," "Schedule of Hours," and "Constant Manning Procedure" in accordance with this opinion. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Solano County (California) directing it to proceed to arbitration with plaintiff firefighter's union on four disputed contract issues, thereby rejecting defendant's claim that the issues were not subject to arbitration pursuant to Vallejo, Cal., City Charter § 809, which excepted from collective bargaining matters involving the "merits, necessity or organization" of any service.


OVERVIEW: A dispute arose during contract negotiations between defendant city and plaintiff firefighter's union. Defendant agreed to submit some issues to arbitration, but it refused to submit four of the issues, claiming that they involved the "merits, necessity or organization" of the firefighting service and that they were not subject to arbitration, under the exceptions to collective bargaining contained in Vallejo, Cal., City Charter § 809. On writ of mandate, the superior court ordered defendant to proceed to arbitration on those issues and defendant appealed. The appellate court ruled that, because the scope of § 809 paralleled the scope of Cal. Gov't Code § 3504 and the National Labor Relations Act (NLRA), 29 U.S.C.S. § 158(d), state and federal law applied in construing the exception contained in § 809. In modifying the judgment to note a few limited exceptions, and affirming as modified, the court ruled that, under the NLRA and § 3504, issues involving wages, hours, or working conditions, were mandatory subjects of bargaining, including the four disputed issues of hourly schedules, vacancies and promotions for non-supervisory positions, manpower workloads, and reductions in force.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified and, as modified, affirmed because, properly construing the charter provision in a manner consistent with federal law and state statutory provisions, with a few limited exceptions, the issues of hourly scheduling, vacancies and promotions for non-supervisory positions, manpower workloads, and personnel reduction were matters involving wages, hours, or working conditions, and thus were mandatory subjects of bargaining.

 

37 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


California Educational Facilities Authority v. Priest


S.F. No. 23148


Supreme Court of California


12 Cal. 3d 593; 526 P.2d 513; 116 Cal. Rptr. 361; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 248



September 25, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  We conclude the Act here challenged is constitutional. Let a peremptory writ issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, California Educational Facilities Authority and private university, sought an original writ of mandate to compel respondent state treasurer to prepare to sell certain bonds authorized by the California Educational Facilities Authority Act (Act), Cal. Educ. Code § 30301 et seq., because respondent refused to do so claiming that the Act violated U.S. Const. amend. I, Cal. Const. art. I, § 4, and Cal. Const. art. IX, § 8.


OVERVIEW: Respondent state treasurer refused to prepare to sell certain bonds authorized by the California Educational Facilities Authority Act (Act), Cal. Educ. Code § 30301 et seq., because she believed the Act provided unconstitutional government support for the establishment of religion. Petitioners, California Educational Facilities Authority and private university, sought an original writ of mandate to compel respondent to act. The court issued the writ, holding that the Act did not violate the establishment clause of U.S. Const. amend. I, or provisions prohibiting state aid for sectarian purposes set forth in Cal. Const. art. I, § 4 and Cal. Const. art. IX, § 8. The court held that the Act's objective of providing expanded opportunities for college education was a clearly secular purpose. The Act's primary effect neither advanced nor inhibited religion because respondent private university had no religious affiliation and the proposed project had no connection with a religious activity. The Act and proposal did not excessively entangle the state with religion for the same reasons.


OUTCOME: The court issued the writ of mandate sought by petitioners, California Educational Facilities Authority and private university, compelling respondent state treasurer to prepare to sell certain bonds authorized by the California Educational Facilities Act (Act) because the Act did not violate the First Amendment or provisions of the California Constitution prohibiting state aid to religious organizations.

Isobe v. Unemployment Ins. Appeals Board


L.A. No. 30281


Supreme Court of California


12 Cal. 3d 584; 526 P.2d 528; 116 Cal. Rptr. 376; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 247



September 24, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C28971, Robert A. Wenke, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Insofar as the judgment denies disability benefits, it is reversed with directions to grant a writ of mandate compelling the board to vacate its decision and to reinstate the referee's award. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employees sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which denied their writ of mandate to set aside decisions of respondent California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board that rejected claims for unemployment insurance and disability benefits.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners comprised two categories: those claiming entitlement to disability benefits and those claiming the right to unemployment insurance benefits. All petitioners, members of the boilermakers union, went out on strike. Following a presidential order, the union offered during negotiations to return to work immediately on condition that all employees be reinstated. Since it desired to retain persons hired while the strike was in progress, the company refused to accede, and no agreement was ever reached. The referee granted employment benefits on the ground that the trade dispute ended when the union unconditionally offered to return to work and also granted disability. Respondent state Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board reversed the grant of employment benefits. The court agreed that petitioners were not entitled to employment benefits because they had opportunity to voluntarily return to work, and affirmed the denial. Because respondent did not assume jurisdiction over the disability cases within the 10 days provided for appeal, the referee's decision became final as to the disability benefits.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of unemployment benefits because petitioners alone were responsible for their unemployment and held that the referee's decision concerning disability benefits was not timely appealed and thus became final.

McCartney v. Commission on Judicial Qualifications


L.A. No. 30217


Supreme Court of California


12 Cal. 3d 512; 526 P.2d 268; 116 Cal. Rptr. 260; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 242



September 19, 1974 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a state judge, sought a review by writ of the recommendations of respondent Commission on Judicial Qualifications that he be removed from office for misconduct.


OVERVIEW: The Commission on Judicial Qualifications found that petitioner, a state judge, had engaged in both acts of willful misconduct and prejudicial conduct and recommended that petitioner be removed from judicial office pursuant to Cal. Const. Art. VI, § 18. Petitioner sought review of that recommendation with the court disputing the merits of the Commission's findings and arguing that the Commission denied him procedural due process, and that any proven misconduct should be mitigated by his inexperience in office or as a justifiable response to an unprofessional effort by the local public defender to prevent him from hearing criminal cases. The court found that the Commission's findings were proper and that the merits of the Commission's findings were supported by evidence. The court found that the petitioner was entitled to some lenience as a result of his inexperience on the bench and his care in making sure that justice was done, advising criminal defendants, and fairness and initiative in sentencing. As a result, the court rejected the recommendation the petitioner be removed and instead found that he should be censured for the misconduct.


OUTCOME: The court rejected the Commission on Judicial Qualifications recommendation that petitioner be removed from judicial office for misconduct. The court found that there was misconduct but that due to mitigating factors that misconduct should be punished by censure rather than removal.

City of San Jose v. Superior Court


S.F. No. 23055


Supreme Court of California


12 Cal. 3d 447; 525 P.2d 701; 115 Cal. Rptr. 797; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 239; 76 A.L.R.3d 1223



September 6, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petitions of the real parties for a rehearing were denied October 2, 1974. Sullivan, J., did not participate therein. Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The writ of mandamus is granted. The trial court is ordered to vacate its order certifying the class and to enter an order dismissing the class action portion of the above-entitled matter. The alternative writ of prohibition is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city sought a writ of mandate ordering respondent Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) to vacate its order certifying the class and to enter an order dismissing the class action portion of the suit brought by real party in interest property owners seeking recovery for diminution in the market value of their property due to their location under the flight pattern of the municipal airport.


OVERVIEW: In a suit against petitioner city by real party in interest property owners for recovery of diminution in the market value of their property due to its location under the flight pattern of the municipal airport, respondent court issued an order certifying the class. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate. The court held that respondent had jurisdiction to certify the class and that class actions against governmental entities for inverse condemnation and nuisance were not prohibited by the requirements of Cal. Gov't Code § 910. However, respondent abused its discretion by certifying the class. There was an insufficient community of interest due to the fact that each class member's right to recovery depended upon facts peculiar to his unique parcel of land. The class representative failed to adequately represent the members of the class because the suit sought recovery for diminution of market value only and did not include other reasonable claims such as damages for annoyance, inconvenience, discomfort, and actual injuries to the land. The court issued a writ ordering respondent to vacate its order certifying the class and to dismiss the class action portion of the lawsuit.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate in favor of petitioner city, which ordered respondent court to vacate its order certifying the class and to dismiss the class action portion of the underlying lawsuit because there was insufficient community interest to sustain a class suit, and because the class representatives, in violation of fiduciary duty, failed to adequately represent the members of the alleged class.

Knoll v. Davidson

Supreme Court of California


12 Cal. 3d 335; 525 P.2d 1273; 116 Cal. Rptr. 97; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 231



August 15, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On September 12, 1974, the opinion was modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: The petition for relief under section 6403 is denied as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner candidate for public office sought a writ of mandate from the trial court (California), directing respondent registrar of voters to accept her application for candidacy without payment of prescribed filing fees, and to include a statement of qualification in the voter's pamphlet without prepayment of their pro rata share of printing costs, and to issue a declaration that Cal. Elec. Code §§ 6551-6555 was unconstitutional.

Petitioner filed for a peremptory writ of mandate to direct respondent to accept her application and place her name on the ballot without payment of prescribed filing fees, to include a statement of qualification in the voter's pamphlet without prepayment of the pro rata share of printing costs, and to issue a declaration that Cal. Elec. Code §§ 6551-6555 prescribing filing fees was unconstitutional. The court denied the statutory relief sought by appellant under Cal. Elec. Code § 6403 as moot. The court held that California had not provided a reasonable means of access to the ballot and that the statutory filing fee system violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend XIV. The court held that although prepayment of the cost of publishing candidate's statement was not constitutionally required, Cal. Elec. Code § 10012.5 permitted billing candidates such costs.


OUTCOME: The court denied the statutory relief sought by petitioner candidate for public office but held that California had not provided a reasonable means of access to the ballot and that the statutory filing fee system violated the equal protection clause of the U.S. Constitution. The court held that although prepayment of the cost of publishing candidate's statement was not constitutionally required, the statute permitted billing such costs.

State v. Superior Court

Supreme Court of California


12 Cal. 3d 237; 524 P.2d 1281; 115 Cal. Rptr. 497; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 224; 7 ERC (BNA) 1190



August 2, 1974

Adams v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 30215


Supreme Court of California


12 Cal. 3d 55; 524 P.2d 375; 115 Cal. Rptr. 247; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 209



July 19, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory of mandate issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a county jury commissioner, sought a writ of mandate compelling respondent court to set aside an order declaring Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 198 unconstitutional and directing petitioner to select petit jurors without regard to the section's one-year residency requirement.


OVERVIEW: After a criminal defendant challenged the jury panel, claiming that a one-year residency requirement was invalid, respondent court sustained the challenge and directed petitioner jury commissioner to furnish a jury panel from which no jurors had been excluded on residential grounds. The court declared that the statute establishing the one-year residency requirement, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 198, was unconstitutional. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate compelling respondent to set aside the order. The court found that the residency requirement did not violate due process or the equal protection guarantees of U.S. Const. amend. XIV or Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 11 and 21. The court held that because newcomers were not a cognizable class, the classification was not analogous to those based on race or lineage and was not suspect. Further, the court held that the classification bore a rational relationship to a legitimate state interest because one of the historic virtues of the jury being selected from the community was its acquaintance with local conditions, customs, and mores. The court therefore issued a peremptory writ of mandate requiring respondent to set aside its order.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory of mandate issue requiring respondent court to set aside its order requiring petitioner jury commissioner to disregard a state law requiring prospective jurors to have resided in the county for at least one year. The court held that the classification was not suspect and that the requirement was constitutional and bore a rational relationship to a legitimate state interest.

People v. Amor


Crim. No. 17473


Supreme Court of California


12 Cal. 3d 20; 523 P.2d 1173; 114 Cal. Rptr. 765; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 206



July 9, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied August 7, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 285575, William A. Caldecott, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which determined that she was financially able to pay the county for a portion of her legal fees, under Cal. Penal Code § 987.8, for services rendered by the appointed counsel in connection with the charge against her for felony hit and run.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with the crime of felony hit and run, in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 20001. She was given a suspended sentence. After a hearing, the trial court found that defendant was financially able to pay a portion of her legal fees to the county for services of her appointed counsel under Cal. Penal Code § 987.8. She appealed from the order. The court affirmed, holding that § 987.8 did not place an impermissible burden on the exercise of defendant's constitutional right to a jury trial or act as a deterrent to effective representation by counsel. The possibility that defendant might have declined counsel rather than run the risk of being required to pay counsel fees in an unknown amount under § 987.8 did not deprive her of her right to counsel. The court also held that defendant was not deprived of her right to due process because the proceedings against her comported with due process requirements, and the statute did not expressly sanction the taking of her property without compliance with such requirements. The fact that the statute did not expressly provide for notice and a hearing did not render it unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court which required defendant to pay a portion of the legal fees for services rendered by her appointed counsel in connection with the prosecution of her for felony hit and run. The court held that she was not deprived of due process and that her constitutional right to counsel was not infringed upon.

Conover v. Hall


Sac. No. 7972


Supreme Court of California


11 Cal. 3d 842; 523 P.2d 682; 114 Cal. Rptr. 642; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 337



July 2, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for a rehearing was denied July 31, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 215815, William M. Gallagher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state welfare program officials, appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), issuing a preliminary injunction restraining appellants from implementing Cal. Inst. & Welf. Code § 11451.6, which established a standard $ 50 per month work-expense allowance for all non-child care related expenses regardless of the recipient's actual employment-related expenses.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, recipients of aid to families with dependent children (AFDC) whose monthly work-related expenses regularly exceeded $ 50, brought an action challenging a fixed $ 50 standard allowance as incompatible with the provisions of 42 U.S.C.S. § 602(a)(7) and sought declaratory injunction relief. On appeal, the court held that the preliminary injunction was properly issued. The court found that under § 602(a)(7), in determining a welfare recipient's "nonexempt income" for purposes of calculating an AFDC grant, the state had to take into consideration any expenses reasonably attributable to the earning of any such income. The court held that the inflexible $ 50 standard work-expense allowance established by Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11451.6 conflicted with § 602(a)(7) insofar as it precluded those recipients whose actual work-related expenses exceeded the $ 50 figure from the benefit of the inclusion of such additional expenses in the computation of their grants. The court also held that the trial court retained jurisdiction to restrain the implementation § 11451.6 and to relieve indigent respondents from posting an undertaking to secure the state's potential damages.


OUTCOME: The order issuing a preliminary injunction restraining appellants, state welfare officials, from instituting a $ 50 maximum allowance on work-related expenses, exclusive of child care, without requiring respondents, recipients of aid to families with dependent children, to post an adequate undertaking was affirmed; the allowance was incompatible with federal law.

Balen v. Peralta Junior College Dist.


S.F. No. 23096


Supreme Court of California


11 Cal. 3d 821; 523 P.2d 629; 114 Cal. Rptr. 589; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 335



June 28, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied July 25, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 406086, Robert H. Kroninger, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant instructor sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted respondent college's motion for summary judgment in appellant's action to compel respondent to set aside its allegedly wrongful dismissal of appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant instructor brought a suit against respondent college, contending that he qualified as a permanent or probationary employee and could not have been discharged without notice and hearing. Respondent filed a motion for summary judgment that was granted by the trial court, and appellant sought review. The court reversed, holding that appellant was properly classified as a probationary instructor and was entitled by statute to pretermination notice and hearing. The court found that appellant worked for more than three months his first year, was hired thereafter for a full year, and was not considered a permanent or substitute teacher. Thus, he met the statutory prerequisites for probationary employment and retained that status over the course of his employment. The court concluded that because respondent provided no hearing, summary judgment for respondent and dismissal of the action were improper.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court that granted respondent college's motion for summary judgment in appellant instructor's action to compel respondent to set aside its allegedly wrongful dismissal of appellant was reversed because appellant was a probationary employee and was entitled to notice and hearing of his termination by respondent.

Clean Air Constituency v. State Air Resources Bd.


S.F. No. 23093


Supreme Court of California


11 Cal. 3d 801; 523 P.2d 617; 114 Cal. Rptr. 577; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 334; 6 ERC (BNA) 1945



June 27, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the respondent to vacate ARB Resolution No. 73-27G and Emergency Regulation amending California Administrative Code, title 13, section 2008, subdivisions (a), (b), and (d) filed December 28, 1973, and to implement and enforce the NOx installation program in the manner set forth in the NOx legislation.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, clean air advocates, brought a proceeding for a writ of mandate to challenge the decision by respondent, the California State Air Resources Board, to delay implementation of a pollution control program applicable to certain motor vehicles.


OVERVIEW: Legislation was passed requiring respondent, the California State Air Resources Board, to set standards for devices to reduce the emission of oxides of nitrogen (NOx) from the exhaust of certain vehicles and to establish a program for the installation of the NOx control devices. Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 39107.6, 39177.1- 39177.4; Cal. Veh. Code § 4602. On three occasions, respondent voted to delay implementation of the NOx program. Respondent justified the third vote on the grounds that an energy crisis presented cause for further delay. Petitioners, clean air advocates, filed an action for a writ of mandate to challenge respondent's decision to delay the program. The court issued the writ. The court held that respondent had limited authority to delay the NOx program under Cal. Veh. Code § 4602(b) for extraordinary and compelling reasons, so long as the action furthered the NOx legislation's primary goal of speedy and effective purification of the atmosphere. However, the court determined that respondent exceeded its authority in voting for a delay on the ground of an energy crisis because energy conservation was not one of the goals of the NOx legislation.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate in favor of petitioners, clean air advocates, requiring respondent air resources board to vacate its decision to delay implementation of a vehicle emissions program because the board exceeded its authority when it voted to delay the program for reasons related to an energy crisis. The court directed respondent to implement the program in the manner set forth in the enabling legislation.

Cossack v. Los Angeles


L.A. No. 30233


Supreme Court of California


11 Cal. 3d 726; 523 P.2d 260; 114 Cal. Rptr. 460; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 328



June 21, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 981173, Eugene E. Sax, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 43.05.1(B)(1)-(2) to be unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. I, § 11 and the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, a coin-operated amusement game operator, a bowling alley, and a game aficionado, challenged as unconstitutional a municipal ordinance that prohibited the use or placement of coin-operated games. The trial court held that as to games of skill, not chance, the ordinance was unconstitutional. Appellant city sought review. The court affirmed, holding that Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 43.05.1(B)(1)-(2) violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 11 because the prohibition was inconsistent with state statutes that permitted the use of games of skill. The equal protection clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, was also violated because an arbitrary distinction was created between coin-operated games of skill and other recreational games of skill. The court looked to the legislative intent of the ordinance and found that it did not distinguish between games of chance and of skill because it was enacted before mechanical devices such as flippers were added to the games, thereby introducing the element of skill. The court held that the legislature could not, therefore, have intended to prohibit the use of games of skill.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that a municipal ordinance prohibiting coin-operated games was unconstitutional under the California constitution because it was contrary to state statutes in the field and under the Fourteenth Amendment equal protection clause because it created an arbitrary distinction between games of skill.

 

49 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Thornton


Crim. No. 12099


Supreme Court of California


11 Cal. 3d 738; 523 P.2d 267; 114 Cal. Rptr. 467; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 329



June 21, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied July 25, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 328445, Herbert V. Walker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed as to count VII (simple kidnaping in violation of § 207 of the Pen. Code). The judgment is modified insofar as it provides for the penalty of death to provide for a punishment of life imprisonment without possibility of parole. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of convictions by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), for kidnapping for the purpose of robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 209; simple kidnapping, § 207; robbery, § 211; sex perversion, § 288a; and sodomy, § 286. Defendant was sentenced to death and to life imprisonment without possibility of parole.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of convictions for kidnapping, robbery, and sex offenses. Defendant was sentenced to death for the offense of kidnapping for the purpose of robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 209. The court reversed the judgment of conviction of simple kidnapping, Cal. Penal Code § 207, and modified the sentence, insofar as it provided for the penalty of death, to provide for a punishment of life imprisonment without possibility of parole. In all other respects, the judgment was affirmed. The court found that the imposition of the death penalty violated the Cal. Const. art. I, § 6 prohibition against cruel or unusual punishment. The court also found that the conviction for simple kidnapping under Cal. Penal Code § 207 had to be reversed because there was no asportation of the victim into another county.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the conviction of simple kidnapping because the victim was not taken from the county. The court modified the judgment, insofar as it imposed the death sentence, to provide for life imprisonment without possibility of parole. In all other respects, the judgment was affirmed.

 

50 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


County of Nevada v. MacMillen


S.F. No. 23108


Supreme Court of California


11 Cal. 3d 662; 522 P.2d 1345; 114 Cal. Rptr. 345; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 325



June 17, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Nevada County, No. 18893, Harold V. Thompson, Judge. +


+ Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The case is remanded to the trial court with directions to enter judgment for the defendant in accordance with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant district attorney sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Nevada County (California), which held that Cal. Gov't Code § 3600 et seq. was unconstitutional and enjoined appellant from enforcing its provisions.


OVERVIEW: Appellant district attorney challenged the judgment that held that Cal. Gov't Code § 3600 et seq., an act requiring public officials to disclose financial information, was unconstitutional and enjoined appellant from enforcing its provisions. Respondent county argued that the act was unconstitutionally vague and was unconstitutionally overbroad, invading the fundamental right of privacy without serving a reasonable legislative purpose. The court reversed the judgment and held that although the act contained some imprecise and uncertain language, it accomplished its legitimate aims in a nonintrusive and limited fashion and was constitutional. The court held that the act, on its face, contained sufficient assurances that unnecessary intrusions into personal privacy would not occur. The court held that neither the right to privacy, nor the right to seek and hold public office, would inevitably prevail over the right of the public to an honest and impartial government. Although terms in the act were subject to some interpretation, the provisions were not so uncertain and indefinite as to render the act void on its face.


OUTCOME: The lower court's judgment was reversed because the legislation regarding financial disclosure for public officials was not vague or overbroad and the right to privacy did not prevail over the right of the public to an honest and impartial government.

 

51 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Dyas v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L.A. No. 30237


Supreme Court of California


11 Cal. 3d 628; 522 P.2d 674; 114 Cal. Rptr. 114; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 323



June 6, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  Let a writ of mandate issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a writ of mandate with the court that challenged a decision of respondent trial court, which denied defendant's motion to suppress and held that the people, the real party in interest, could admit evidence seized from defendant by a uniformed patrolman of the housing authority under U.S. Const. amend. IV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 19.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with one count of possession of amphetamine for sale pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11351 after a uniformed patrolman from the housing authority seized a bag of amphetamine pills from defendant. Defendant's motion to suppress the evidence was denied by respondent trial court because the people, the real party in interest, successfully argued that the patrolman was a private person. Defendant sought review by writ of mandate. The court granted defendant's writ of mandate because the illegal search and seizure was subject to the exclusionary rule under U.S. Const. amend. IV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 19. The court held that the exclusionary rule did not apply to evidence obtained in a search conducted by a person that was truly a private citizen. The patrolman acted under the color of authority and did not act to protect the interests of a private employer. The housing authority was a public body corporate and politic and exercised public and governmental functions. Merely because the primary mission of the housing authority was not law enforcement did not permit the patrolman to violate defendant's protection from unreasonable searches.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate in favor of defendant and ordered respondent trial court to suppress evidence seized from defendant by a uniformed patrolman from the housing authority because the Fourth Amendment exclusionary rule applied and the people, real party in interest, could not admit evidence that was the result of an unreasonable search and seizure by a person that was not truly a private citizen.

Donovan v. Brown


L.A. No. 30300


Supreme Court of California


11 Cal. 3d 571; 524 P.2d 137; 115 Cal. Rptr. 41; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 319



May 31, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  Respondents are hereby directed to count all write-in votes cast for petitioners in the June 4, 1974, primary election for the office, for which they had filed declarations of write-in candidacy. This order is final forthwith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, write-in candidates, sought a writ of mandate to compel respondents, the Secretary of State and county registrar-recorder, to file declarations as write-in candidates in a primary election pursuant to Cal. Elec. Code §§ 18601-18603, without requiring the payment of any fee or proof of indigency.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, write-in candidates, in an original proceeding before the court, sought a writ of mandate to require respondents, the Secretary of State and the county registrar-recorder, to file petitioners' declarations as write-in candidates in the primary election pursuant to Cal. Elec. Code §§ 18601-18603 without requiring the payment of any fee or any proof of indigency. The court issued an alternative writ of mandate and a conditional order directing respondents, pending final termination of the proceeding, to file petitioners' declarations as write-in candidates without requiring the payment of any fee or any proof of indigency but not to count any votes cast for petitioners unless ordered by the court. The court concluded that the California filing fee system set forth Cal. Elec. Code §§ 6551-6555, and made a prerequisite by § 18603 for the filing of the declaration of write-in candidacy and the counting of ballots, violated the equal protection clause in U.S. Const. amend. XIV and was null and void. Accordingly, respondents were directed to count all write-in votes cast for petitioners for the office for which they had filed declarations of write-in candidacy.


OUTCOME: The court ordered respondents, the Secretary of State and county registrar-recorder, to count all write-in votes cast for petitioners, write-in candidates, in the primary election for the offices for which they had filed declarations of write-in candidacy. The requirement that a fee be paid for the filing of a declaration of write-in candidacy violated the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the U.S. Constitution.

 

53 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Kapperman


Crim. No. 17006


Supreme Court of California


11 Cal. 3d 542; 522 P.2d 657; 114 Cal. Rptr. 97; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 316



May 23, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  Thus, we conclude that the credit under section 2900.5 should be extended to those incarcerated or on parole for felony offenses regardless of the date of their commitment to state prison. We hereby direct the Adult Authority to take appropriate steps to inform petitioner and all other persons under its custody and supervision of the availability of that credit, to ascertain the amount of credit due and to adjust its records accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate requested a writ of habeas corpus, seeking credit against his sentence on two counts of armed robbery for time he had remained in custody between the time of his arrest and his receipt by the California Department of Corrections before March 4, 1972, the effective date of Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5, which provided such credit to persons convicted of felony offenses who commenced their prison sentence on or after that date.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner inmate pleaded guilty to two counts of armed robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211 in June, 1971, and sought credit under Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5 against the term of his sentence for time he remained in custody between the time of his arrest and his receipt by the California Department of Corrections. The court concluded that the limitation in Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5, subd. (c) on the applicability of such credit to inmates received into state prison after March 4, 1972 violated equal protection guarantees in Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 11 and 21 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV in that it constituted a legislative classification that was not reasonably related to a legitimate public purpose. The court held that the credit available under Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5 should be extended to inmates incarcerated or on parole for felony offenses whether committed to state prison before or after March 4, 1972. The court invalidated only the discriminatory prospective limitation in § 2900.5, subd. (c), holding that the remainder of § 2900.5 was not thereby made void.


OUTCOME: The court held that petitioner inmate should have been eligible for a statutory credit for the period of his detention before his delivery to state prison. The court held the prospective limitation in the statute was unconstitutional under equal protection provisions of the state and federal constitutions and extended the credit to those incarcerated or on parole for felony offenses regardless of the date of their commitment to state prison.

Kruger v. Wells Fargo Bank


S.F. No. 23014


Supreme Court of California


11 Cal. 3d 352; 521 P.2d 441; 113 Cal. Rptr. 449; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 302; 65 A.L.R.3d 1266



April 26, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:
Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 627913, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff customer appealed the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which dismissed her complaint charging defendant bank with improperly exercising a banker's lien against her account.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff had a checking and charge account at defendant bank. She deposited unemployment and disability payments in the checking account. Without advance notice, the bank deducted that account's balance and applied it to a charge account delinquency. Plaintiff filed suit for an improper banker's lien, claiming that deposits from governmental benefits were exempt from attachment under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 690.18(a). The lower court dismissed the complaint for failure to present facts stating a cause of action; but it did not address whether plaintiff could amend the complaint. Plaintiff appealed, contending that defendant bank had engaged in state action violating due process under state and federal constitutions. The court held that the right of setoff was private action, not state action, and thus not unconstitutional. The court agreed that unemployment and disability payments were exempt from attachment. It concluded that the trial court had erred in dismissing the complaint and provided plaintiff an opportunity to amend, noting that her complaint had erroneously referred to a banker's lien rather than setoff.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court and ruled that plaintiff 's complaint could be amended to restate the cause of action.

OUTCOME: The court ordered disbarment of petitioner attorney because the record indicated that petitioner committed acts of burglary and grand theft, which constituted moral turpitude and dishonesty, and that integrity of the legal profession required disbarment.

 

56 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Adams v. Dep't of Motor Vehicles


Sac. No. 7959


Supreme Court of California


11 Cal. 3d 146; 520 P.2d 961; 113 Cal. Rptr. 145; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 286; 64 A.L.R.3d 803



April 10, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  The alternative writ is discharged; the peremptory writ is granted. Respondents are ordered not to process or otherwise act on applications for transfer of registration pursuant to any lien sale conducted under the sections of the Civil Code herein declared unconstitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner vehicle owners sought a writ of mandamus, which challenged the constitutionality of the garageman's labor and materials lien provided for by Cal. Civ. Code §§ 3068(a), 3071-3074.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner vehicle owners sought a writ of mandamus in the state supreme court and contended that the garageman's labor and materials lien provided for by Cal. Civ. Code §§ 3068(a), 3071-3074 violated their due process rights. The court granted the writ and noted that principles of procedural due process required the opportunity for a hearing before the state, in aid of a creditor, could deprive a debtor of a significant property interest. The court noted that that the issue was not controlled by whether there was direct governmental involvement because private conduct could become so involved with state action that it was subject to the same due process limitations. The court noted that, unlike the statutorily created garageman's lien, a possessory lienholder had no power of sale under the common law. Furthermore, the sale process was administered, in part, by respondent Department of Motor Vehicles. Thus, enforcement of a lien constituted state action. Moreover, the court noted the lack of the availability of an accelerated hearing process to resolve contested lien claims and concluded that the lien statutes violated petitioners' due process rights.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandamus against the use of the statutory garageman's labor and material lien and held that the statutorily provided lien violated the principles of procedural due process because it permitted the involuntary sale and transfer of vehicles without affording petitioner vehicle owners an opportunity for a hearing.

 

57 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Dulaney v. Mun. Court for San Francisco Judicial Dist.


S.F. No. 23006


Supreme Court of California


11 Cal. 3d 77; 520 P.2d 1; 112 Cal. Rptr. 777; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 280



March 28, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue as prayed for.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, antiwar protesters, sought a writ of prohibition to restrain respondent, the Municipal Court for the San Francisco Judicial District of the City and County of San Francisco (California), from further proceedings in the criminal actions brought against them for alleged violations of San Francisco, Cal., Municipal Code § 690, which made it unlawful to post flyers and notices on utility poles without a license.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, antiwar protesters, were arrested for an alleged violation of San Francisco, Cal., Municipal Code § 690, which declared it unlawful to post flyers and notices on utility poles without a license. Petitioners unsuccessfully sought to obtain from respondent superior court a writ of prohibition to restrain their prosecutions. Petitioners then sought a writ from the court, which was granted. The court noted that relief could be obtained via a writ of prohibition when a statute or ordinance was alleged to be facially unconstitutional. The court also noted that petitioners had standing to attack the ordinance, even though they made no attempt to comply with it. The court opined that regulations that imposed reasonable restrictions on the time, place, manner, and duration of U.S. Const. amend. I expression were permitted if they narrowly limited the discretion of officials. Procedures that granted officials unbounded discretion in the permit process were prohibited because they could have been used to restrain undesirable speech. The court found that the disputed ordinance contained no discernible standards for the issuance of licenses. Therefore, it was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of prohibition against the enforcement of a city ordinance that declared it unlawful to post flyers and notices on utility poles without a license because the ordinance had the effect of a standardless licensing scheme and, therefore, was an invalid prior restraint on the First Amendment rights of petitioners, antiwar protesters.

People v. Superior Court


S.F. No. 23048


Supreme Court of California


11 Cal. 3d 59; 520 P.2d 405; 113 Cal. Rptr. 21; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 278



March 27, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  The alternative writ is discharged and the peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of San Mateo County (California) to set aside its order of diversion and to proceed on the criminal charge of possession of marijuana against real party in interest defendant, where respondent made its determination despite the district attorney's refusal to concur, disregarding the veto power granted to the district attorney under Cal. Penal Code § 1000.2.


OVERVIEW: Respondent superior court ordered real party in interest defendant into the narcotics offense pretrial program of treatment and rehabilitation, under Cal. Penal Code §§ 1000- 1000.4, after he was charged with possession of marijuana, despite the district attorney's refusal to concur. Petitioner state sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent to set aside its order of diversion and to proceed on the criminal charge of possession of marijuana, claiming that Cal. Penal Code § 1000.2 gave the state the right to veto the decision of respondent. On appeal, the court discharged the alternative writ and denied the peremptory writ of mandate, holding that respondent was correct in ruling that such a veto violated the separation of powers under Cal. Const. art. III, § 3 because the decision to divert a defendant into a rehabilitation program pursuant to § 1000.2 was an exercise of judicial power, which could not constitutionally be subordinated to a veto by the state.


OUTCOME: The court discharged respondent superior court's alternate writ and denied the peremptory writ of mandate because the provision in the narcotics pretrial program of treatment and rehabilitation that required the consent of the district attorney before a trial court could order that real party in interest defendant be diverted into the program violated the constitutional doctrine of separation of powers.

Strumsky v. San Diego County Employees Ret. Ass'n


L.A. No. 30009


Supreme Court of California


11 Cal. 3d 28; 520 P.2d 29; 112 Cal. Rptr. 805; 39 Cal. Comp. Cases 924; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 277



March 25, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied April 24, 1974, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Burke, J., and Clark, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 314988. William H. Macomber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county employee's widow appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which, upon review of the decision of defendant county employees' retirement association, denied plaintiff's application for a service-connected death allowance under Cal. Gov't Code § 31787, found that there was no prejudicial abuse of discretion by respondent and denied plaintiff's petition for the peremptory writ.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff county employee's widow made an application to respondent county employees' retirement association for a service-connected death allowance established by Cal. Gov't Code § 31787, which was denied. The trial court denied plaintiff's petition for writ of mandate to set aside respondent's decision. The court reversed and remanded for further proceedings because in denying the writ, the trial court had considered itself bound to apply the substantial evidence standard of review. The court held that plaintiff's right to the pension benefits was a fundamental, vested right, thus, the trial court should have exercised its independent judgment on the evidence to determine if plaintiff's husband's death was service-connected and found an abuse of discretion if the findings were not supported by the weight of the evidence. The court held that the amendment to Cal. Const. art. VI withdrew judicial powers by local agencies that were formerly granted pursuant to Cal. Const. art. XI, thus, the factual findings of the agencies were entitled to no greater deference than those of other agencies that lacked judicial powers.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, which found no abuse of discretion by respondent county employees' retirement association in denying service-connected death benefits to plaintiff county employee's widow, because the trial court improperly used a substantial evidence standard of review to the local agency's decision, which affected a fundamental vested right, when it should have exercised its independent judgment on the evidence.

D'Amico v. Board of Medical Examiners


Sac. No. 7976


Supreme Court of California


11 Cal. 3d 1; 520 P.2d 10; 112 Cal. Rptr. 786; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 276



March 19, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 182624, Frank G. Finnegan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Plaintiffs shall recover costs on both appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant licensing boards appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted summary judgment and a peremptory writ of mandate ordering defendants to examine and to license those plaintiff osteopaths as were found qualified under the provisions of the Osteopathic Act and the Medical Practice Act. Plaintiffs cross-appealed and sought review of the award of attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: After lengthy litigation, including an appeal and a remand, the trial court granted plaintiff osteopaths summary judgment and a peremptory writ of mandate, which ordered defendant licensing boards to examine plaintiffs and to license all who were found qualified. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the trial court was not in error when it entertained plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment instead of conducting a trial on the merits as ordered by the court of appeals. The court held that, although the trial court had employed the wrong equal protection standard of strict scrutiny, the error did not compel reversal. There was no rational relationship between the protection of the public health and the exclusion from licensure of all medical practitioners who had received their training in an osteopathic rather than an allopathic college and held D.O. rather than M.D. degrees. The Osteopathic Act of 1962, together with the 1962 amendment to the Medical Practice Act in Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 2310, denied plaintiffs equal protection of the laws and were therefore to that extent void and of no effect. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in awarding attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and held that the Osteopathic Act of 1962, together with the 1962 amendment to the Medical Practice Act, denied plaintiffs equal protection of the laws and were to that extent void and of no effect. The court awarded plaintiffs costs on both appeals.

In re Foss


Crim. No. 16690


Supreme Court of California


10 Cal. 3d 910; 519 P.2d 1073; 112 Cal. Rptr. 649; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 373



March 14, 1974 



DISPOSITION:  The provision of Health and Safety Code section 11501 precluding parole consideration for petitioner for a minimum of 10 years is invalid under article I, section 6, of the California Constitution and is therefore set aside. The Adult Authority is directed to grant parole consideration to petitioner at such time as is otherwise appropriate under the laws of this state. Petitioner is not entitled to release, however, until such time as the Adult Authority duly determines that he is eligible for parole under the 10-year-to-life term validly imposed by section 11501. The order to show cause is, therefore, discharged and the writ of habeas corpus denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner was convicted in the Humboldt County Superior Court (California) of furnishing heroin and admitted a prior conviction for possession of heroin 14 years earlier. Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus claiming that his enhanced sentence, without the possibility of parole, under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11501, was cruel and unusual punishment in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 6.


OVERVIEW: After being convicted of furnishing heroin and admitting a prior conviction for possession of heroin, both in violation of Cal. Health § Safety Code § 11501, petitioner was sentenced under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11501 without possibility of parole. Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus, contending that the provision of §11501 precluding parole constituted cruel and unusual punishment in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 6. The court concluded that the provision was both cruel, in terms of the additional punishment imposed without regard to the character of either the offender or the offense, and unusually excessive, when compared with punishments imposed in California for more serious crimes and with punishments imposed by other jurisdictions upon the same class of offenders. The court, however, held the parole provision severable from the rest of the statute and held that petitioner's sentence as imposed was valid. The court denied the writ and rejected petitioner's other contentions, that his admission of the prior was invalid and that a jury instruction requiring petitioner to show he was entrapped violated due process.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ of habeas corpus and held that the statutory sentencing provision for furnishing heroin and admitting a prior conviction for possession of heroin was valid. However, the court held that the parole provision was severable and set it aside. The court held that the statutory provision precluding parole consideration was cruel and unusual punishment and invalid under the state constitution.

People v. Hillery


Crim. No. 15053


Supreme Court of California


10 Cal. 3d 897; 519 P.2d 572; 112 Cal. Rptr. 524; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 371



March 12, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 13914, John D. Jelletich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment insofar as it provides for the penalty of death, is modified to provide a punishment of life imprisonment, and as so modified is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's case came before the court on automatic appeal from the Superior Court of Kern County (California), following his conviction of first degree murder and sentence of death and prior reversals and re-impositions of the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's case came before the court on automatic appeal from the superior court following his conviction of first degree murder and sentence of death. On appeal, the court affirmed as modified, holding that because the death penalty had been found unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. I, § 6, defendant's sentence would be modified to life imprisonment. The court held that where defendant failed to move to change venue at the time of trial and raised no issue concerning trial publicity and jury bias on his first appeal, his second appeal, or argument upon recall of the remittitur, the contention was waived. The court held that defendant was not precluded from raising the issue of change of venue at trial or on appeal, where even prior to caselaw breaking new ground in requiring trial judges to take prophylactic measures to prevent publicity from influencing jurors, caselaw made clear that a defendant unable to obtain a fair trial because of prejudicial newspaper publicity had the constitutional right to a change of venue, and there was no evidence defense counsel was unaware of that caselaw or was incompetent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction, modified the penalty to life imprisonment as the death penalty had been found unconstitutional, and held that defendant's failure to object to venue based on prejudicial pretrial publicity was waived for failure to raise it during trial or previous appeals, and defendant could have raised the issue earlier where caselaw made clear his constitutional right to change venue if publicity prevented a fair trial.

Whitfield v. Roth


S.F. No. 23020


Supreme Court of California


10 Cal. 3d 874; 519 P.2d 588; 112 Cal. Rptr. 540; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 370



March 12, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of respondents County of Contra Costa and Skinner for a rehearing was denied April 10, 1974, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 348949, William H. Brailsford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of nonsuit is reversed. The judgment entered on the verdict in favor of the remaining defendants is affirmed.

The prevailing party or parties on each appeal shall recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff patient sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which entered a judgment of nonsuit in favor of defendant county and physician and entered a jury verdict in favor of all the then remaining defendant physicians in an action for damages for medical malpractice.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff patient filed a medical malpractice action against defendant physicians, then amended after discovery to include defendants, county and physician. The trial court entered a judgment of nonsuit in favor of defendants, county and physician, and held that plaintiff's claim was time-barred. On appeal, the court reversed and held that because plaintiff was a minor, the knowledge of the minor's parent determined the time of accrual of the cause of action. The court held that plaintiff's mother was reasonably diligent in pursuing her suspicion of negligence and that plaintiff's cause of action was timely. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment entered on the verdict in favor of the remaining defendants. The court held that although opposing counsel's conduct constituted misconduct when he presented the jurors with gifts, plaintiff's counsel failed to object and thus waived the argument and that the misconduct was not of such an egregious nature that it could not have been cured by a corrective instruction to the jury. The court held that although the out of court statements by physicians were hearsay and were erroneously admitted, that admission was harmless error.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of nonsuit in plaintiff patient's medical malpractice action against defendants, county and physician, because the complaint was timely filed. The court affirmed the judgment entered on the verdict in favor of the remaining defendants, physicians, because there was no reversible error. The court ordered that the prevailing party or parties on each appeal should recover costs on appeal.

 

64 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Walker


Crim. No. 16711


Supreme Court of California


10 Cal. 3d 764; 518 P.2d 1129; 112 Cal. Rptr. 177; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 361



February 14, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied March 20, 1974. McComb, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, No. 6972, Raymond J. Sherwin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the writ of habeas corpus is reversed, and the case is remanded to the Solano County Superior Court for further proceedings not inconsistent with the views expressed herein. Petitioner's sentence of life imprisonment without possibility of parole for the murder is modified by the deletion of the italicized words. Petitioner is therefore entitled to apply to the Adult Authority for parole and to have his application duly considered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of the order by the Superior Court of Solano County (California), which granted defendant's habeas corpus petition which, in effect, set aside defendant's conviction on one count of first degree murder and two counts of first degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had pleaded not guilty by reason of insanity under Cal. Penal Code § 1016, and was thereafter convicted of one count of first degree murder and two counts of second degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 1506. After an insanity hearing was conducted, in which defendant was deemed insane, his execution was stayed. At a second hearing, defendant was adjudicated sane. Thereafter, the Governor of California commuted defendant's sentence to life imprisonment without possibility of parole. Defendant sought habeas corpus relief, which the trial court granted, essentially setting aside the convictions. The state appealed, and the court reversed and remanded. Defendant's statements to the police were voluntary and he was adequately represented by counsel. However, defendant was entitled to relief under the Anderson decision, which held that the death penalty was unconstitutional, and the only valid punishment authorized by Cal. Penal Code §§ 190 and 190.1 for first degree murder was life imprisonment. The court remanded for the sentence to be modified by removing the parole restriction, thereby entitling defendant to apply for parole.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order that granted defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus, holding that the grounds for relief were not supported by the evidence, and remanded for the commuted sentence of life imprisonment without possibility of parole to be modified by deleting the parole restriction, as the death penalty had been found to be unconstitutional, and statutes only authorized a sentence of life imprisonment.

 

65 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Marriage of Fithian


L.A. No. 30165


Supreme Court of California


10 Cal. 3d 592; 517 P.2d 449; 111 Cal. Rptr. 369; 1974 Cal. LEXIS 346



January 3, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. D-45860, Frank Domenichini, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which classified appellant's military retirement benefits as community property in the dissolution of appellant's marriage to appellee wife.


OVERVIEW: Appellant husband received veteran's military retirement pay. In divorce proceedings between appellant and appellee wife, the trial court classified appellant's retirement pay as community property and awarded appellee an interest in the benefits. Appellant argued the treatment of his military retirement payments as community property was unconstitutional, as it frustrated the congressional intent in creating the retirement system. Examining federal case law and legislative history related to the creation of the military retirement system, the court held that designating the military retirement payments as community property did not unconstitutionally frustrate the congressional intent. The statutory language and structure of the system did not have the purpose of excluding a veteran's ex-spouse. The court also held that the benefits were intended as compensation for past, rather than present, service, and as such appellee was entitled to an interest under the state community property laws to the extent that she was married to appellant while he served.


OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed, as appellant's military retirement payments were properly considered community property.

 

66 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Schueren


Crim. No. 16398


Supreme Court of California


10 Cal. 3d 553; 516 P.2d 833; 111 Cal. Rptr. 129; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 169



December 24, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied January 23, 1974. Clark, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-009918, Thomas F. McCarry, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified by (1) adding "subdivision (a)," after "Section 245" and (2) changing "term prescribed by law." to "term prescribed by law, subject to the limitation that defendant's term may not exceed 14 years." The judgment, as so modified, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which, after a jury found defendant guilty of assault with a deadly weapon ( Cal. Penal Code § 245), "a lesser and necessarily included offense," sentenced him to prison for "the term prescribed by law." Defendant's sole attack was upon the sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged by information in a single count of assault with a deadly weapon with intent to commit murder, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 217. A jury found defendant guilty of assault with a deadly weapon, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 245, a lesser and necessarily included offense, and he was sentenced to prison for the term prescribed by law. On appeal, defendant argued the proscription in Cal. Const. art. I, § 6 against cruel or unusual punishment precluded a sentence exceeding 14 years for defendant charged with assault with intent to commit murder (§ 217) and convicted of assault with a deadly weapon (§ 245(a)) as a necessarily included offense. The court concluded the judgment should be modified to limit the maximum term of imprisonment to not more than 14 years and that the judgment as so modified should be affirmed. Noting that nothing in Cal. Const. art. I, § 27 indicated that the word "unusual" could no longer be interpreted literally, the court held that under the circumstances of this case a prison term exceeding 14 years for assault with a deadly weapon under § 245(a) was an unconstitutional application of the penalty provision of that section.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment so as to limit the maximum term of defendant's imprisonment to not more than 14 years and as modified affirmed the judgment. The court held that under the circumstances of this case a prison term exceeding 14 years for assault with a deadly weapon was an unconstitutional application of the penalty provision of the relevant statute.

 

67 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Schullman v. State Bar of California


L.A. No. 30145


Supreme Court of California


10 Cal. 3d 526; 516 P.2d 865; 111 Cal. Rptr. 161; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 167



December 20, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  It is ordered that petitioner be suspended from the practice of law for a period of two years. It is further ordered that within 30 days after the effective date of this order petitioner shall perform the acts specified in rule 955, subdivision (a), California Rules of Court, and that within 40 days after the effective date of this order petitioner shall file with the clerk of this court, with proof of service of a copy on the State Bar at its San Francisco office, an affidavit containing the matters specified in subdivision (c) of the foregoing rule. This order is effective 30 days after the filing of this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney sought review after respondent, the California State Bar Disciplinary Board, found that petitioner was guilty of professional misconduct and recommended that he be suspended from the practice of law for two years.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney was charged in three separate instances with failing to perform legal services after accepting a retainer from his clients. Respondent state bar disciplinary board found that petitioner was guilty of professional misconduct and recommended a two-year suspension from the practice of law. The court held that substantial evidence supported respondent's findings and ordered that petitioner be suspended from the practice of law for two years. Petitioner had failed to provide legal services for his clients after accepting a fee, and petitioner avoided his clients' repeated requests for information. Petitioner received a fair and impartial hearing on the charges. Respondent was not required to engage independent personnel to conduct disciplinary proceedings. The legislature had intended that respondent be self-governing, and the state bar rules afforded petitioner adequate procedural safeguards. Petitioner was not prejudiced by a brief reference to his prior record during the culpability phase of the proceeding; petitioner's prior record was primarily considered for disciplinary purposes after respondent had determined that petitioner was guilty of the current charges.


OUTCOME: The court held that substantial evidence supported findings by respondent state bar disciplinary board that petitioner attorney was guilty of professional misconduct. The court accepted respondent's recommendation that petitioner be suspended from practicing law for two years. Petitioner had received a fair hearing on the charges.
