Swoap v. Superior Court of Sacramento County


Sac. No. 7948


Supreme Court of California


10 Cal. 3d 490; 516 P.2d 840; 111 Cal. Rptr. 136; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 166



December 12, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue as prayed for.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) issued a statewide temporary restraining order that enjoined defendants, state officials, from requiring adult children to reimburse the state, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 12100 and 12101, for aid to the aged extended to their parents, defendants sought a writ of prohibition to prevent the superior court from enforcing its restraining order.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, two recipients of aid to the aged and their adult children brought a class action seeking to enjoin defendants, state officials, from requiring adult children to reimburse the state, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 12100 and 12101, for aid to the aged extended to their parents. The superior court issued a statewide temporary restraining order enjoining defendants. Defendants sought a writ of prohibition to prevent the superior court from enforcing its restraining order. The court let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue. The court held that an adult child of a recipient of aid to the aged under the Old Age Security Law, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 12000 et seq., could constitutionally be required to reimburse the state. The court held that Cal. Civil Code § 206 provided a rational basis for upholding relatives' responsibility against the challenge that such statutes arbitrarily charged welfare costs to one class in society and thereby denied equal protection of the laws.


OUTCOME: The court let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue as prayed for by defendants, because the superior court improperly enjoined defendants from requiring adult children to reimburse the state for aid to aged extended to their parents. The court held that an adult child of a recipient of aid to the aged could constitutionally be required to reimburse the state.

Willens v. Commission on Judicial Qualifications


Sac. No. 7979


Supreme Court of California


10 Cal. 3d 451; 516 P.2d 1; 110 Cal. Rptr. 713; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 163



December 3, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 211181, Oscar A. Kistle, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment granting mandate to compel defendants to approve Willens' request for a disability retirement is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a Commission on Judicial Qualifications and a chief justice of the court, appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted a writ of mandate compelling them to approve the request by plaintiff, a retired judge who had also been indicted for criminal conspiracy and bribery, for disability retirement.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff judge was indicted on charges on criminal conspiracy and bribery, which disqualified him for acting as a judge by reason of Cal. Const. art. VI, § 18(a). Plaintiff was defeated at a subsequent general election; on that same date, he submitted a letter to defendants, a Commission on Judicial Qualifications (commission) and a chief justice of the court, requesting that he be retired pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 75060, on the basis of an alleged disability to discharge the duties of his reason by reason of physical and mental disability; attached to his request were supporting medical affidavits. The request was refused, but the trial court issued a writ of mandate to compel defendants to approve the disability retirement request. On appeal, the court found that substantial evidence had established the principal statutory prerequisite to a disability retirement. Moreover, Cal. Const. art. VI, § 18 disclosed no legislative intent to preclude a disabled judge from obtaining retirement benefits. The court concluded that plaintiff was entitled to a disability pension for the remainder of his life, or until he recovered from his disability; the order below was affirmed.


OUTCOME: A writ of mandate compelling defendants Commission on Judicial Qualifications and chief justice to approve the disability retirement request of plaintiff judge, who had also been indicted on criminal charges and disqualified from acting as a judge, was affirmed. Substantial evidence established the principal statutory prerequisite to a disability retirement, and there was no legislative intent to preclude such retirement benefits.

In re Buckley


Crim. No. 16621


Supreme Court of California


10 Cal. 3d 237; 514 P.2d 1201; 110 Cal. Rptr. 121; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 152; 68 A.L.R.3d 248



October 19, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied November 28, 1973. Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for the writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner lawyer sought habeas corpus relief from an order of the Orange County Superior Court (California), which adjudged him in direct contempt of court and sentenced him to jail and a fine.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner lawyer was sanctioned for making insulting comments to the judge during a trial. The judge found petitioner to be guilty of contempt of court and sanctioned the lawyer to jail and fine. Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus on the grounds that the order of the trial court was insufficient on its face to support the jurisdiction of the trial court and it the order was not supported by the record. Petitioner also alleged that under the circumstances of the case, the trial judge was required to refer the charge of the alleged contempt to another judge for adjudication, and the lack of procedure in contempt matters denied the contemnor the equal protection of the laws. The court denied the writ of habeas corpus. The court held that petitioner's statement to the trial judge was contemptuous on its face, the trial judge was not required as a matter of due process to refer the matter of adjudication of the alleged contempt to another judge, and because petitioner was not convicted of criminal offense his summary adjudication of contempt was proper under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1211.


OUTCOME: Petitioner lawyer's request for writ of habeas corpus was denied because the evidence supported the contempt finding and sanctions imposed because his statements to the trial judge were contemptuous.

Braxton v. Municipal Court for San Francisco Judicial Dist.


S.F. No. 22896


Supreme Court of California


10 Cal. 3d 138; 514 P.2d 697; 109 Cal. Rptr. 897; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 147



October 4, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 627179, Francis McCarty, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the superior court denying a writ of prohibition is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant students challenged the decision of respondent Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied their request for a writ of prohibition to prevent their prosecution under Cal. Penal Code § 626.4. They had brought an action claiming the statute suffered from the defects of first amendment overbreadth, unconstitutional vagueness, and the lack of procedural due process.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which denied appellant student's request for a writ of prohibition to prevent their prosecution under Cal. Penal Code § 626.4 in their action against real party in interest state of California claiming the statute suffered from the defects of first amendment overbreadth, unconstitutional vagueness, and the lack of procedural due process. The court interpreted Cal. Penal Code § 626.4 narrowly to prohibit only incitement to violence or conduct physically incompatible with the peaceful functioning of the campus. The court held that § 626.4 dictated that a student who engaged in disruptive conduct temporarily relinquished his right to remain on campus, and a deprivation hedged with appropriate limiting procedures, did not constitute an unconstitutional incursion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed denial of a writ of prohibition to prevent appellant student's prosecution in an action against real party in interest state of California claiming the statute suffered from the defects of first amendment overbreadth, unconstitutional vagueness, and the lack of procedural due process. The stature prohibits only incitement to violence or conduct physically incompatible with the peaceful functioning of the campus.

 

72 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Ector v. Torrance


L.A. No. 30100


Supreme Court of California


10 Cal. 3d 129; 514 P.2d 433; 109 Cal. Rptr. 849; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 146



October 2, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 997818, Max Z. Wisot, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former employee challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his petition for writ of mandate to compel respondent city to vacate its order terminating his employment and to reinstate him with full back pay, alleging that the requirement of respondent's charter that its officers and employees reside within its borders violated Cal. Gov't Code § 50083 and denied him constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: Appellant former employee sought to compel respondent city to vacate its order terminating his employment and to reinstate him with full back pay, which the trial court denied. Appellant challenged the trial court's judgment on the basis that the residency requirement in respondent's charter for its officers and employees contravened Cal. Gov't Code § 50083 and denied him certain constitutional rights. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, finding the residency requirement permissible under both state law and the federal constitution. Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5(b) permitted a charter city to prescribe in its charter qualifications for its employees, including a residency requirement, and the court therefore, determined that § 50083 applied only to general law cities, and not to charter cities, of which respondent was one. The court also held that the residency requirement did not violate any provision of the federal constitution. The residency requirement did not interfere with any fundamental rights of appellant, it bore a rational relationship to its purposes, and it did not create "suspect classifications" of affected employees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, holding that the residency requirement in respondent city's charter did not contravene a law prohibiting such a requirement for general law cities because respondent was a charter city, and the residency requirement did not violate any of appellant former employee's federal constitutional rights.

Selby Realty Co. v. City of San Buenaventura


L.A. No. 30110


Supreme Court of California


10 Cal. 3d 110; 514 P.2d 111; 109 Cal. Rptr. 799; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 145; 3 ELR 20901



September 27, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 53612, Marvin H. Lewis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed as to the fifth cause of action against the city defendants, and in all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowner appealed orders of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which sustained general and special demurrers to, and dismissed, plaintiff's action seeking declaratory relief, damages, and a writ of mandate against defendant, a city, a county, and several of their officials arising from defendant's general development plan that proposed the extension of various streets over plaintiff's land.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff landowner brought an action against defendant, a city, a county, and several of their officials for declaratory relief, damages, and a writ of mandate arising from the defendant's adoption of a general development land plan that proposed the extension of various streets across plaintiff's land. The court affirmed the dismissal of all of plaintiff's actions except for a request for a writ of mandate to compel the city to issue plaintiff a building permit, the dismissal of which it reversed because plaintiff had stated a cause of action for the writ. The court found that plaintiff failed to state a cause of action against defendant county for declaratory relief, because declaratory relief could not be used to challenge the legislative adoption of the plan, and failed to state a cause of action for inverse condemnation because there was no firm declaration of intent to condemn. The court found that defendant city was not liable under Cal. Gov't. Code § 818.4 for injuries caused by its refusal to issue plaintiff a permit where it was authorized to determine whether the permit should be issued. There was no scheme to take plaintiff's land because there was no taking.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of plaintiff landowner's cause of action for a writ of mandate to compel defendant city to issue plaintiff a building permit because plaintiff stated a cause of action and the complaint did not contain the defect defendant asserted in its demurrer. The court affirmed the dismissal of plaintiff's remaining causes of action because they failed to state a cause of action upon which relief could be granted.

Pettit v. State Board of Education


L.A. No. 30094


Supreme Court of California


10 Cal. 3d 29; 513 P.2d 889; 109 Cal. Rptr. 665; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 140; 78 A.L.R.3d 1



September 7, 1973

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant teacher sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied her petition for a writ of mandate to review and set aside respondent State Board of Education's order revoking her life diploma.


OVERVIEW: Appellant teacher pleaded guilty to outraging public decency. Respondent State Board of Education initiated disciplinary proceedings to revoke appellant's life diploma. At issue was appellant's behavior which included oral copulation with three different partners in a semi-public atmosphere and witnessed by several strangers plus her television discussions of nonconventional sexual behavior. On appeal, the court held that respondent and trial court were entitled to conclude that appellant was unfit to teach based on the very nature of appellant's misconduct and expert testimony that it was likely that appellant would be unable to teach moral principles or to act as an exemplar for her pupils.

The court held that, based on the nature of appellant's sexual misconduct and the opinions of experts that she would be unable to set a proper example for her pupils or to teach them moral principles, appellant was unfit to teach in public elementary schools.

Weber v. City Council of Thousand Oaks


L.A. No. 30099


Supreme Court of California


9 Cal. 3d 950; 513 P.2d 601; 109 Cal. Rptr. 553; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 238



September 4, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. SP 46591, Richard C. Heaton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, residents of territory sought to be annexed, appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which denied their petition for a writ of mandate to compel defendants, city council and city clerk, to terminate proceedings for annexing land to the city.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, residents of the territory sought to be annexed, filed an action against defendants, city council and city clerk, contending that the inclusion of their property in the territory proposed to be annexed without giving the residents within that territory an opportunity to vote on the issue violated their rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The trial court granted defendants' motion for summary judgment and plaintiffs appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the act which provided that residents of inhabited territory were entitled to vote on annexation to a city did not constitutionally require the state to grant a similar right to vote on annexation to residents of a territory that was deemed uninhabited. The court found that the legislative distinction between annexations of inhabited and uninhabited territory did not impinge on the fundamental right to vote because there was not an issue of the exclusion of a class from participation in an election. The court found that the differences in the challenged statute were rationally related to a legitimate state end; thus, there was no violation of plaintiffs U.S. Const. amend. XIV rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the award of summary judgment in favor of defendants, city council and city clerk, because the legislative distinction between annexations of inhabited and uninhabited territory did not impinge on the fundamental right to vote and the challenged statute was rationally related to a legitimate state end.

 

76 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Guerrero v. Carleson


L.A. No. 30079


Supreme Court of California


9 Cal. 3d 808; 512 P.2d 833; 109 Cal. Rptr. 201; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 228



July 30, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 1772, Max Z. Wisot, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff welfare recipients sought review of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that entered an order denying their application for a preliminary injunction prohibiting defendant directors of welfare authorities from reducing or terminating welfare payments to recipients who defendants knew were literate in Spanish but not in English, unless notice of such reduction or termination was given in the Spanish language.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs known to be Spanish speaking received a notice of reduction or termination of benefits in English. Recipients of assistance were entitled to receive timely and adequate notice of any proposed reduction or termination of benefits. They alleged that because they were Spanish literate that they failed to request a hearing within the appropriate time. It was further alleged by plaintiffs that defendant directors were constitutionally mandated to give such notice in Spanish. The trial court denied plaintiffs' application for a preliminary injunction to prohibit defendants from reducing or terminating their benefits. The court found that because English was the predominate language of the United States and the notice came on official letterhead addressed to each plaintiff with a case number, that the government could reasonably assume they would have sought immediate assistance in getting the notice translated. Plaintiffs' assertion that the due process clause and equal protection principles were violated was found to be without support because the expectation they would seek translation was reasonable and resources providing that assistance were available.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment denying the preliminary injunction prohibiting the denial of reduction or termination of plaintiff welfare recipients' benefits because plaintiffs admitted they failed to request a hearing within the time allowed. It was reasonable for the government to assume plaintiffs would use resources provided to have notices translated. There was no violation of due process or equal protection principles.

 

77 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jones


Crim. No. 16563


Supreme Court of California


9 Cal. 3d 546; 510 P.2d 705; 108 Cal. Rptr. 345; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 209



June 5, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A168199, Bernard Lawler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of three counts of selling marijuana under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11531. Defendant argued that the systematic exclusion from the jury of all residents of the district where the crime was committed violated his rights under U.S. Const. amends. VI and XIV.


OVERVIEW: In defendant's prosecution, the trial court entered judgment of conviction of three counts of selling marijuana under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11531. Defendant appealed, contending that the systematic exclusion from the jury of all residents of the district where the crime was committed violated U.S. Const. amends. VI and XIV and deprived him of his right to a trial by an impartial jury of the state and district in which the crime was committed. The court reversed the judgment and ruled that the traditional starting point for determining the community from which jurors were to be selected was the scene of the alleged offense and that, in determining whether the source from which a given jury was selected represented a fair cross-section of the community, the court adhered to a notion of community which at least encompassed the location of the alleged offense. It was the community in which the crime was committed that the jury was required to represent. The court held that the district, however large or small, from which the jury was drawn was required to include the area in which the crime was committed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant of selling marijuana. The court ruled that defendant's constitutional right to a trial by a jury required a jury that was selected from a district that included the site of the alleged offense.

 

78 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Crownover v. Musick


L.A. No. 29917, Sac. Nos. 7904, 7905, 7906


Supreme Court of California


9 Cal. 3d 405; 509 P.2d 497; 107 Cal. Rptr. 681; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 198



May 1, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petitions of the respondents in No. 29917 and of the appellants in Nos. 7904 and 7905 for a rehearing were denied May 30, 1973. Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 170792, Robert L. Corfman, Judge. Superior Court of Sacramento County, Nos. 197128, 197434, 197924, William M. Gallagher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In Sac. 7906 the appeal is dismissed as moot. In L.A. 29917 the order granting a preliminary injunction is reversed. In Sac. 7904 and Sac. 7905 each of the orders denying a preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court of California consolidated appeals from the Superior Court of Orange County and Superior Court of Sacramento County to determine the constitutionality of certain city and county ordinances which prohibited the service of food or drink while providing entertainment by "topless" women and "bottomless" persons of either sex in any establishment.


OVERVIEW: The Supreme Court of California consolidated four cases on appeal to determine the constitutionality of certain city and county ordinances which prohibited the service of food or drink and the providing of entertainment by "topless" women and "bottomless" persons of either sex in any establishment. The court held that the ordinances did not infringe upon the rights of freedom of speech or expression but were valid regulations of conduct and did not violate the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the U.S. Constitution, U.S. Const. amend XIV. Cal. Penal Code §§ 318.5, 318.6 did not adopt or incorporate the local ordinances. The ordinances regulated conduct within the delineated statutory limits, by proscribing specific behavior by particular persons in designated establishments other than theaters and did not expressly prohibit speech. Public morals and public welfare could have justified the prohibitions. The ordinances only regulated the manner in which the service of food was conducted. No suspect classification or fundamental interest was involved. The court overruled In re Giannini, 69 Cal. 2d 563, 72 Cal. Rptr. 655, 446 P. 2d 535 (1968).


OUTCOME: The court held that the ordinances, which prohibited the service of food while providing entertainment by "topless" women and "bottomless" persons of either sex were not unconstitutional and did not violate freedom of speech or the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the U.S. Constitution.

 

79 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Vaughn


Crim. No. 14025


Supreme Court of California


9 Cal. 3d 321; 508 P.2d 318; 107 Cal. Rptr. 318; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 274



April 10, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 33134, William M. Gallagher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Thus, we have concluded that defendant's present attack on the judgment of guilt cannot be sustained. The judgment, insofar as it provides for the penalty of death, is modified to provide a punishment of life imprisonment and as so modified is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which convicted him of assault on a prison guard by force likely to produce great bodily injury, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 4500, contending that the death penalty was improperly imposed and that the he was erroneously allowed to represent himself in the guilt phase and thereafter enter a guilty plea in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 1018.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for assault on a prison guard by force likely to produce great bodily injury, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 4500 and the death sentence that resulted therefrom. The court set aside the death sentence, but otherwise affirmed the conviction, finding no error in the trial court permitting defendant to represent himself at the guilt stage of the proceedings. The court determined that the trial court, after it initially refused defendant's request for self-representation, permitted the same after concluding that defendant was competent to do so based upon prior prosecutions for the same offense, his knowledge of the rules of evidence, and the retention of the public defender as a consultant to defendant during the proceedings. The court concluded that the trial court's acceptance of defendant's guilty plea did not violate Cal. Penal Code § 1018 because the public defender, who had participated in the trial from its early stages, was reappointed to represent defendant and both defendant and counsel represented to the court that defendant had been fully apprised of the consequences of his plea and thereafter made an informed decision to enter it.


OUTCOME: The court set aside the death penalty but otherwise affirmed defendant's conviction for assault on a prison guard by force likely to produce great bodily injury, finding that the trial court permitted defendant to represent himself at the guilt stage only after being convinced of his competence to do so and accepted defendant's guilty plea after appointment of counsel and confirming the plea was entered with knowledge of its consequences.

 

80 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Sykes v. Superior Court of Orange County


L.A. No. 30063


Supreme Court of California


9 Cal. 3d 83; 507 P.2d 90; 106 Cal. Rptr. 786; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 177



March 16, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied May 9, 1973.



DISPOSITION: The People having failed to establish good cause for the untimely delay in rearraigning petitioner for plea, the respondent was compelled to grant petitioner's motion to dismiss the pending charges. Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the dismissal of those charges.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent the Superior Court of Orange County (California) to dismiss a felony information pending against him or a writ of prohibition staying the criminal proceedings. Petitioner contended that he was denied his right to a speedy trial under Cal. Const. art. I § 13 and U.S. Const. amend. VI when, after 228 days, the People, the real party in interest, had not commenced the action.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was in prison congruent to a guilty plea for a felony. He was issued a writ of habeas corpus that ordered he be rearraigned. Approximately five months later his motion to dismiss was denied. The court found that the right to a speedy trial was a fundamental right secured by U.S. Const. amend. VI and Cal. Const. Art. I, § 13. The court also stated that Cal. Penal Code § 1382 provided that, absent a showing of "good cause," a defendant accused of a felony was entitled to have the charges against him dismissed if he was not brought to trial within 60 days after the filing of criminal charges or after particular events necessitating a retrial thereof. The court further stated that, though § 1382(2) did not apply on its face where a once-convicted person was released from prison on a writ of habeas corpus ordering that he be rearraigned for plea, the self-executing provision of the Constitution required its application. Last, the court stated that the People's reason, that they were unaware of the order to rearraign petitioner, failed to show good cause for the delay and issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing the dismissal of petitioner's charges.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate ordering respondent to dismiss the charges against petitioner. The court held that the People, real party in interest failed to establish good cause for the untimely delay in rearraigning petitioner for plea and thus violated his right to a speedy trial.

 

81 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Thompson v. Mellon


S.F. No. 22984


Supreme Court of California


9 Cal. 3d 96; 507 P.2d 628; 107 Cal. Rptr. 20; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 178; 65 A.L.R.3d 1029



March 16, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory write of mandate issue directing respondent city clerk to file petitioner's nomination papers and to place his name upon the ballot for the municipal election of April 10, 1973, if he complies with all other requirements for becoming a candidate for City Councilman of the City of Santa Cruz. This order is final forthwith. 8



8 This paragraph confirms our order in identical language filed on February 23, 1973.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner candidate sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent, the city clerk for the City of Santa Cruz (California), to file petitioner's nomination papers and to place his name upon the ballot for the municipal election. Respondent refused to perform such duties because petitioner did not satisfy the residency requirement of Santa Cruz, Cal., Charter § 602. Petitioner claimed that § 602 violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Respondent, the city clerk for the City of Santa Cruz, refused to file petitioner candidate's nomination papers and to place petitioner's name upon the ballot for a municipal election because petitioner did not satisfy the two-year residency requirement contained in Santa Cruz, Cal., Charter § 602. Petitioner candidate sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent to perform those duties. The court issued a peremptory writ directing respondent to file petitioner's papers and to place him on the ballot. The court agreed with petitioner's contention that § 602 violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV because it was not necessary to achieve a compelling governmental interest. The court conceded that the city had a compelling interest in requiring candidates for elected office to maintain their residence within city limits. The court ruled, however, that the crucial date for such purposes was the date of filing the papers for candidacy. Thus, the court ruled, requiring a candidate to establish residency within the 30-day period preceding an election was permissible, but any period in excess of 30 days was violative of equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate, thus directing respondent clerk to file petitioner candidate's nomination papers and to place his name on the ballot for a municipal election. The city charter provision, which required candidates to have two-years of residency in the city in order to be eligible to run for elected office, was not necessary to achieve a compelling governmental interest. Thus, the provision violated equal protection.

 

82 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Brown v. Merlo


Sac. No. 7947


Supreme Court of California


8 Cal. 3d 855; 506 P.2d 212; 106 Cal. Rptr. 388; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 265; 66 A.L.R.3d 505



February 20, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Butte County, No. 47498, J. F. Good, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the superior court with instructions to permit plaintiff to proceed with his cause of action in negligence.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Butte County (California) entered in favor of defendant pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 17158 in a personal injury action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was injured in a car accident while riding as a passenger in defendant's car. The trial court found in favor of defendant based on Cal. Veh. Code § 17158 which deprived an injured automobile guest of any recovery for the careless driving of his host unless the injury results from the driver's willful misconduct or intoxication. The court reversed, holding that by depriving automobile guests of a right of action for negligence which is afforded to all other passengers or pedestrians, the California guest statute drew an irrational classification condemned by both the Federal and California Constitutions' equal protection guarantees. Neither the "hospitality" nor "elimination of collusive lawsuits" rationales constituted a rational basis for the statute's classification scheme. Thus, the court held that the statute was unconstitutional. The court remanded the case with instructions to permit plaintiff's cause of action in negligence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded, holding that by depriving automobile guests of a right of action for negligence which is afforded to all other passengers or pedestrians, the California guest statute drew an irrational classification condemned by both the Federal and California Constitutions' equal protection guarantees.

84 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Liberty Mut. Ins. Co. v. Fales


S.F. No. 22914


Supreme Court of California


8 Cal. 3d 712; 505 P.2d 213; 106 Cal. Rptr. 21; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 249



January 23, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 615702, Leland J. Lazarus, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. In the interests of justice, appellant is to be awarded costs. (Cal. Rules of Court, rule 26(a).)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Uninsured appellant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which held that appellant was precluded from seeking affirmative relief from appellee insurer because Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 340(3) imposed a one-year limitation upon personal injury actions.


OVERVIEW: Appellee insurer issued an automobile insurance policy to the insured. The insured was involved in an automobile accident with uninsured appellant. Appellee insurer paid the insured and initiated an action fourteen months after the accident against appellant for the monies paid. The court awarded appellee insurer judgment, finding that appellant was precluded from seeking relief because Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 340(3) imposed a one-year limitation upon actions for personal injuries damages. The court affirmed the judgment, but held that appellant was not precluded from seeking affirmative relief. Appellant simply failed to seek affirmative relief by means of a cross-complaint. If appellant had filed a cross-claim, the court would have entertained the action because appellee insurer was not entitled to a longer limitations period than appellant. The three-year limitation period found in Cal. Ins. Code § 11580.2(g), within which an insurer must initiate an action against an uninsured motorist, was not intended to deprive uninsured motorists of affirmative relief.


OUTCOME: Although finding error in the lower court's reasoning that appellant was time-barred from seeking affirmative relief, and finding neither party was entitled by statute to a longer limitations period, the court affirmed the judgment in appellee insurer's favor because appellant failed to file a cross-complaint.

Supreme Court of California


8 Cal. 3d 661; 504 P.2d 1249; 105 Cal. Rptr. 785; 1973 Cal. LEXIS 245

DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue as prayed.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner small claims litigant sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent courts to allow her to file a notice of appeal and to prosecute an appeal from an adverse judgment entered in respondent small claims court without being required to file the undertaking prescribed by law.


OVERVIEW: In a proceeding for a writ of mandate, petitioner small claims litigant sought to be allowed to file a notice of appeal and to prosecute an appeal from an adverse judgment entered in respondent small claims court without being required to file the undertaking prescribed by law. The court held that the requirement of an undertaking, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 117l, or a deposit of money in lieu of an undertaking, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code 117ll , in connection with an appeal from a judgment entered in small claims court constituted a taking of property prior to a due process hearing with right to counsel, and that such requirement was constitutionally impermissible. A peremptory writ of mandate was issued.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate, holding that the requirement of an undertaking deprived petitioner small claims litigant of her property without due process of law.

 

86 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Lynch


Crim. Nos. 16232, 16237


Supreme Court of California


8 Cal. 3d 410; 503 P.2d 921; 105 Cal. Rptr. 217; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 262



December 4, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied January 3, 1973.



DISPOSITION: The writ is granted and petitioner is ordered discharged from custody.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate applied for a writ of habeas corpus and contended that the indeterminate sentence, which was imposed by a California trial court as the aggravated penalty for second-offense indecent exposure provided by Cal. Penal Code § 314, violated the prohibition against cruel or unusual punishments under Cal. Const. art. I, § 6.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner inmate contended that the aggravated penalty for second-offense indecent exposure provided by Cal. Penal Code § 314 violated the prohibition against cruel or unusual punishments under Cal. Const. art. I, § 6. Under the recidivist provision of § 314, petitioner had been sentenced to prison for an indeterminate term, with a maximum life sentence. The court granted the writ, ordered petitioner discharged from custody, and held that the particular indeterminate sentence imposed pursuant to § 314 was unconstitutional because of the unreasonably high maximum term. The court held that when a defendant under an indeterminate sentence challenged that sentence as cruel or unusual punishment, the test was whether the maximum term of imprisonment permitted by the statute punishing the offense exceeded the constitutional limit, regardless of whether a lesser term may have been fixed in his particular case. The court held that the recidivist provision of § 314 was void because the increased punishment provided was far more severe than those of other recidivist statutes penalizing conduct that was more serious and it changed the crime from a misdemeanor to a life-maximum felony.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner inmate's writ of habeas corpus and ordered petitioner discharged from custody because the indeterminate penalty with a maximum life sentence for a second offense of indecent exposure violated the prohibition of the California Constitution against cruel or unusual punishments.

People v. Murphy


Crim. No. 15221


Supreme Court of California


8 Cal. 3d 349; 503 P.2d 594; 105 Cal. Rptr. 138; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 258



November 27, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied December 29, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A250552, Raymond Choate, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment, insofar as it provides for the penalty of death, is modified to provide a punishment of life imprisonment and as so modified is affirmed in all other respects. ( People v. Anderson, supra, 6 Cal.3d 628.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered a jury verdict convicting defendant of conspiracy to commit first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code §182, and first degree murder of defendant's wife in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 187and 189, and sentenced defendant to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for conspiracy to commit first degree murder and first degree murder of his wife, and was sentenced to death. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's conviction, holding that the warrantless tape recordings of the conversations between defendant and state witnesses were not obtained in violation of his rights under U.S. Const. amend. IV because a search warrant for the purpose of secret surveillance was not required where the persons with whom defendant was conversing consented to the recordings of the conversations. The recordings were also not obtained in violation of defendant's rights under U.S. Const. amend. V because defendant was not deprived of his freedom of action in any significant way at the time of the recordings. Further, defendant's statements in the recordings did not violate U.S. Const. amend. VI because the right to counsel did not apply in a pre-indictment setting. The photographs of the victim were properly admitted into evidence because defendant made no objection at trial to the admission of the evidence, and the evidence, although gruesome, was relevant, and any prejudice involved did not amount to grounds for reversal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions because the warrantless tape recordings of the conversations between defendant and state witnesses were not obtained in violation of his rights under U.S. Const. amends. IV, V, and VI. The court modified defendant's sentence to provide a punishment of life imprisonment.

People v. Barksdale


Crim. No. 15866


Supreme Court of California


8 Cal. 3d 320; 503 P.2d 257; 105 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 256



November 22, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied December 29, 1972. McComb, J., Burke, J., and Sullivan, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the San Leandro-Hayward Judicial District of Alameda County, No. 332370-C, Thomas L. Foley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Review was granted of an order certified by the superior court, after the Municipal Court for the San Leandro-Hayward Judicial District of Alameda County (California) sustained the demurrer of defendant physician to a complaint charging him with a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 274.


OVERVIEW: Defendant physician performed an abortion in a non-hospital setting and was charged with violation of Cal. Penal Code § 274. His demurrer to the complaint was sustained by the municipal court, but the superior court reversed and certified the issue. The court held that portions of the 1967 Therapeutic Abortion Act (Act), Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 25952, 25954, 25951(b) and (c), and the first sentence of § 25953, were unconstitutionally vague. The court ruled that it was not possible to tell what degree of impairment must result from a continuance of pregnancy in order to "gravely impair" a prospective abortee's health, and the statute did not adequately define what impairment of "mental health" would justify abortion. Thus, the language establishing the medical criteria upon which abortions could be approved was not sufficiently certain to meet minimal standards of due process. The court found no constitutional impediments to those portions of the Act that required abortions to be performed by licensed physicians in accredited hospitals, or that limited the performance of abortions to the first 20 weeks of pregnancy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the municipal court sustaining the demurrer of defendant physician because the statute prescribing the rules for legal abortions was unconstitutionally void for vagueness in that it failed to give adequate notice of what degree of impairment to a woman's health was necessary to justify an abortion.

Curtis v. Board of Supervisors


L.A. No. 29873


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 942; 501 P.2d 537; 104 Cal. Rptr. 297; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 236



September 19, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of respondents Somers and Great Lake Properties, Inc. for a rehearing was denied October 25, 1972.



DISPOSITION: The peremptory writ of mandate as prayed shall issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner residents challenged Cal. Gov't. Code § 34311 as violating U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Calif. Const. art. I, §§ 11, 21, and appellate courts exercised original jurisdiction as the issues presented were of great public importance. Petitioners argued respondent county board could not constitutionally refuse to hold an election on the issue of incorporation because owners of more than 51 percent of the total land by value filed protests.


OVERVIEW: The court held Cal. Gov't. Code § 34311 violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 11, 21 because the court held no compelling state interest justified § 34311's provision for protests by landowners to bar an election on incorporation by all voters. Petitioner residents had requested respondent county board to call an incorporation election, but, as required by § 34311, respondent refused to do so after opponents, who owned more than 51 percent of the land by value of the proposed area of incorporation, filed protests. The court applied strict scrutiny as voting was a fundamental interest and the opportunity § 34311 provided to bar an election was held a burden on the right to vote. The court rejected respondent's argument that the compelling interest in protecting property owners justified the statutory scheme and noted that issues argued as justifications regarding property owners, such as zoning and taxes, also affected non-property owners such that the statute bore no rational relation to a state interest. The court severed § 34311 from the incorporation statutes as doing so left a viable statutory scheme for incorporation.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate, which required respondent county board to call an election as requested by petitioner residents because the court held the statutory provision, which prevented the election on the grounds of protests filed by owners of 51 percent of the affected land by value, was unconstitutional and violated the equal protection clauses of the state and federal constitution.

People v. McKinnon


Crim. No. 15379


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 899; 500 P.2d 1097; 103 Cal. Rptr. 897; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 233



September 13, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR-16929, Robert O. Staniforth, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders appealed from are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People challenged the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which granted the motion of defendants to suppress narcotics evidence obtained by an airline employee, while dismissing charges against one defendant and setting aside the information as to the second defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with transport and possession of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 11531, 11530.5. Defendants filed motions to suppress drug evidence based on an illegal search and seizure. The trial court granted the motions, dismissed the charges as to one defendant, and set aside the information as to the second defendant. The People appealed, claiming that the search by an airline employee, who was not acting as an agent of the police, was legal. In agreement, the court reversed the trial court's order, holding that chattel consigned to a common carrier for shipment could lawfully be searched based upon probable cause to believe it contained contraband. There was no general police-instigated exploratory search. Rather, as employee acting on his own initiative opened and inspected a container believing it contained marijuana. He was then entitled to show the contents to law enforcement personnel, who recognized and smelled the marijuana. Predicated on such probable cause, the officer's subsequent search of the packages before him was constitutionally reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's suppression of drug evidence at defendant' drug trial, finding that search of a container by an airline employee was not an illegal search and seizure.

In re Roderick P.


Crim. No. 16155


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 801; 500 P.2d 1; 103 Cal. Rptr. 425; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 226



August 16, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 407546, Marvin A. Freeman, Judge, and Consuelo Marshall, Referee.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant minor sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County, Juvenile Division (California), which found defendant to have committed manslaughter and declared him a ward of the court.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found that defendant minor committed manslaughter and declared him a ward of the court. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, concluding that the evidence was insufficient to establish guilt and that the waiver of defendant's constitutional rights was invalid. The court found that there was no substantial evidence to link defendant to the homicide, and there seemed to be substantial evidence that defendant did not commit the act charged. The court held that defendant had not validly waived his Miranda rights and that his statements should not have been admitted into evidence. In view of defendant's unrefuted retardation and immaturity, and the fact that it was his first offense, it was unlikely that he was able to comprehend the extent of the rights he waived. Furthermore, his inability to appreciate the consequence of his statements and the waiver seemed to be reinforced by the fact that he had been asleep prior to the arrival of the police at 12:15 a.m. and his parents had awakened him just minutes before the police advised him of his Miranda rights and obtained the waiver in question.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's finding that defendant committed manslaughter, holding that the evidence was insufficient to establish guilt. The court also held that defendant's waiver of his constitutional rights was invalid, and his statements should not have been admitted into evidence.

People v. Perry


Crim. No. 15048


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 756; 499 P.2d 129; 103 Cal. Rptr. 161; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 224



August 2, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, No. 5813, Edmund M. Moor, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: Defendants have cited us to no reversible error, and we have found none. The judgment of conviction is affirmed as to both defendants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Solano County, (California), that convicted them of murder in the first degree on the grounds of the improper admission and corroboration of accomplice testimony, improper admission of evidence other crimes and wrongful acts and evidence obtained in a warrantless search.


OVERVIEW: Defendants challenged their convictions for murder in the first degree on the grounds that the trial court erred in admitting insufficiently corroborated accomplice testimony, in admitting evidence of other wrongs and evidence obtained from a warrantless search. Error was also assigned to jury instructions and prosecutorial remarks. The court affirmed defendants' convictions holding that there was sufficient corroborating evidence to support the accomplice testimony, where both defendants exhibited behavior that supported an inference of consciousness of guilt, as one defendant attempted to flee and hide his identity and the other defendant attempted to escape from jail. The court held that evidence of other wrongs was properly admitted within the trial court's discretion as part of testimony relating to flight or escape. The court found that although the prosecution made improper comments concerning defendants' attorney, the closing comments were harmless and did not have a likely effect on the outcome of the verdict. The court held that access to the accomplice's medical records was properly denied because there was no waiver of psychotherapist-patient privilege.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions of murder in the first degree holding that there was sufficient corroborating evidence to support the accomplice testimony from evidence of flight, concealment and escape that inferred defendants' consciousness of guilt. The court did not address defendants' claims of illegal warrantless search because any possible error in admitting the contested evidence was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt.

L. v. Superior Court of Los Angeles


L.A. No. 29964


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 592; 498 P.2d 1098; 102 Cal. Rptr. 850; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 215



July 17, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  The alternative writs heretofore issued are discharged and the petition for peremptory writs of prohibition and mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner minor sought peremptory writs of prohibition and mandate to review a judgment from the juvenile court (California) which concluded that petitioner was unfit for juvenile court and ordered him to be prosecuted under the general criminal law after petitioner committed four robberies and one murder.


OVERVIEW: When the juvenile court concluded that petitioner minor was unfit for juvenile court and ordered him to be prosecuted under the general criminal law after petitioner committed four robberies and one murder, petitioner sought peremptory writs of prohibition and mandate. The court denied the petition. The trial court's conclusory statements on unfitness for juvenile court were supported by substantial factual record. The trial court properly took into consideration the nature of the alleged crimes, the circumstances surrounding their commission, and the minor's past record and lack of rehabilitation in another state's juvenile court. Assuming that the presiding judge ordered a rehearing at the request of the district attorney rather than solely on the judge's own motion, the court could find no prejudice to petitioner since the issue of fitness was fully and fairly reheard de novo by a judge other than the one who ordered the rehearing. The other state's records showed no background of prior violent behavior, but they sufficiently showed a behavior pattern of delinquency beginning before and not improving after his commitment as a juvenile delinquent at the age of 14.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for peremptory writs of prohibition and mandate because the trial court's conclusory statements on petitioner's unfitness for juvenile court were supported by a substantial factual record.

Bryan v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L.A. No. 29939


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 575; 498 P.2d 1079; 102 Cal. Rptr. 831; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 213



July 17, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  The alternative writ heretofore issued is discharged and the petition for peremptory writs of mandate and prohibition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a juvenile, filed a writ of prohibition in California court seeking to prevent a criminal prosecution of petitioner in adult court for murder.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, a juvenile, admitted to the unlawful killing of another and was committed to the youth authority for confinement. However, the youth authority determined petitioner was not suitable for rehabilitation or confinement within their system and returned petitioner to the juvenile court. Petitioner sought to prevent his prosecution for murder in a court other than the juvenile court. The court denied petitioner's writ of mandate. The court held that petitioner was not subject to double jeopardy by having an adult proceeding. The court held that there was continuing jeopardy and the transfer of petitioner for criminal prosecution was not constitutionally forbidden. The court also held that petitioner received individualized consideration from the youth authority prior to its rejection of petitioner as unsuitable for rehabilitation. The court further mandated that petitioner's admission of guilt in the juvenile proceeding was inadmissible at petitioner's adult criminal proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's writ of prohibition against an adult prosecution of petitioner because double jeopardy did not attach when the juvenile court transferred him to the criminal court after the youth authority refused to accept him as a minor.

Nightingale v. State Personnel Board


L.A. No. 29961


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 507; 498 P.2d 1006; 102 Cal. Rptr. 758; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 207



July 12, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 963722, F. Ray Bennett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant civil service employee sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate instructing appellee, State Personnel Board, to set aside its decision in which appellant was suspended from her employment for failure of good behavior under Cal Gov't Code § 19572(t).


OVERVIEW: Appellant civil service employee was suspended from work, under Cal. Gov't Code § 19572(t), by appellee, State Personnel Board, after appellee determined that appellant used her position to attempt to expedite the hearing of a matter involving a friend. Appellant filed a writ of mandate, which the trial court denied. The court affirmed, concluding that the language § 19572(t) was not unconstitutionally vague because appellant could appreciate, in advance, that her proposed conduct would constitute a "failure of good behavior" likely to bring discredit to her employer. Section 19572(t) did not require that appellant's failure of good behavior be known to the public in order for the behavior to cause discredit to her employer or that she communicate the misconduct to others. The evidence supported the findings of the trial court and appellee that appellant's conduct was adequate cause for the discipline imposed and that appellant was not being penalized for exercising her rights. The court found that appellant's argument that she denied discovery rights was barred by res judicata, and, in any event, she had the benefit of the full range of discovery to which she was entitled.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court denying appellant civil service employee's writ of mandate, because appellant's conduct constituted a failure of good behavior under the statute, the statute was not unconstitutionally vague, the penalty imposed by appellee, the State Personnel Board, was not unduly severe, and appellant had the benefit of the full range of discovery to which she was entitled.

 

96 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Benn


Crim. No. 16112


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 530; 498 P.2d 433; 102 Cal. Rptr. 593; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 209



July 12, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 258800, John A. Loomis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is reversed with directions to the Los Angeles Superior Court to grant petitioner a new resentencing hearing in accordance with the views and procedures expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which ordered stricken only one of two prior drug convictions, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11501, and ordered resentencing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of sale of heroin, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11501. Two alleged prior convictions for narcotics offenses were found to be true. Defendant sought to have both prior convictions stricken so that the sentencing judge would not include them in deciding the length of sentence. The trial court struck only one conviction. The court reversed, holding that the sentencing judge did not appreciate the scope of judicial discretion conferred on him by Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11718. Although the sentencing judge had before him reports on the prior offenses and petitioner's prison behavior, it was clear from the statements made by him at the resentencing hearing that he gave them no consideration in his ultimate determination. The court held that the sentencing judge improperly assumed that his discretion was limited to a determination whether the sentences for the prior convictions qualified as felony or misdemeanor sentences and that after such a determination, he was precluded from any further exercise of judicial discretion. The court directed the trial court to grant defendant a new resentencing hearing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment ordering only one of defendant's two prior convictions stricken, because the sentencing judge did not appreciate the scope of his judicial discretion in determining whether he could strike a conviction. The court directed the superior court to grant defendant a new resentencing hearing.

People v. Sirhan


Crim. No. 14026


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 710; 497 P.2d 1121; 102 Cal. Rptr. 385; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 223



June 16, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied July 26, 1972.
Writ of certiorari denied Sirhan v. California, 410 U.S. 947, 93 S. Ct. 1382, 35 L. Ed. 2d 613, 1973 U.S. LEXIS 3542 (1973)
Writ of habeas corpus denied In re Sirhan, 2000 Cal. LEXIS 4428 (Cal., May 24, 2000)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion denied by Sirhan v. Galaza, 2004 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 32608 (C.D. Cal., July 7, 2004)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Sirhan v. Brazelton, 76 F. Supp. 3d 1073, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 189218 (C.D. Cal., 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-233421, Herbert V. Walker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to provide a punishment of life imprisonment instead of death for the murder and as so modified is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) decision that convicted defendant, after a jury trial, of the first degree murder of a presidential candidate and imposed the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from his first degree murder conviction and death sentence involving a presidential candidate's death. Appellate court affirmed defendant's conviction holding that there was sufficient evidence to support defendant's first degree murder conviction because based on defendant's own testimony that he committed the murder with malice aforethought, the jury was able to reasonably imply that the murder was wilful, deliberate and premeditated. Defendant's death sentence was reduced to life imprisonment because the death penalty had been ruled cruel and unusual punishment under Cal. Const. art. I, § 6. Trial publicity surrounding defendant's earlier guilty plea did not deny defendant a fair trial because there was no evidence the publicity prejudiced defendant or influenced the jury's verdict. Introduction of paper found in defendant's bedroom and of an envelope found in the trash did not violate defendant's privilege against self-incrimination and were not unreasonable searches and seizures as the paper was discovered after a relative gave proper search consent, and defendant had no expectation of privacy in the trash.


OUTCOME: Appellate court affirmed defendant's conviction but reduced his sentence to life imprisonment. Based on defendant's own testimony, there was sufficient evidence to support the murder conviction. Trial publicity surrounding defendant's early guilty plea was not prejudicial. Admitting defendant's papers into evidence did not violate his Fourth and Fifth Amendment rights.

 

98 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Raffaelli v. Committee of Bar Examiners


S.F. No. 22841


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 288; 496 P.2d 1264; 101 Cal. Rptr. 896; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 193; 53 A.L.R.3d 1149



May 24, 1972 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner lawyer filed an application for original writ to compel respondent Committee of Bar Examiners to certify him to the Supreme Court of California for admission to the practice of law, notwithstanding that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6060(a) required that an applicant for admission to the California State Bar was required to be a citizen of the United States.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner lawyer sought admission to the practice of law, but respondent Committee of Bar Examiners refused to certify him on the basis of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6060(a), which provided that an applicant for admission to the California State Bar must be a citizen of the United States. The court granted the writ because application of § 6060 constituted a denial of equal protection of law in violation of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. 1, §§ 11, 21. The court applied close judicial scrutiny which required that the classification under review could not only relate to the purposes of the law, but was required to constitute a necessary means of accomplishing a legitimate state interest and serve to promote a compelling state interest. The court rejected respondent's asserted state interests, and concluded that the challenged classification did not even have a rational connection with the applicant's fitness or capacity to practice law. The court overruled Large v. The State Bar, 218 Cal. 334, 23 P.2d 288 (1933), which had upheld the statutory exclusion of aliens from the bar.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ of review to petitioner lawyer, finding that the requirement that an applicant for admission to the California State Bar be a United States citizen was unconstitutional, and ordered respondent Committee of Bar Examiners to determine whether petitioner fulfilled the remaining certification requirements, and if so, to certify him to the court for admission to the practice of law.

 

99 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Navarro


Crim. No. 15795


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 248; 497 P.2d 481; 102 Cal. Rptr. 137; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 191



May 23, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 45598, Paul Thomas Gallagher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The proceedings are remanded to the court below for resentencing pursuant to the mandatory provisions of section 3051.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After being convicted of selling and furnishing heroin, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11501 and having a prior conviction for assault with a deadly weapon, Cal. Penal Code § 245, defendant appealed his prison sentence imposed by the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which held that defendant was an addict and precluded from commitment to a treatment program under the narcotic addicts law, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3000 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of selling and furnishing heroin and had a prior conviction for assault with a deadly weapon. The trial court found that under the narcotic addict law, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3000 et seq., defendant was an addict with a prior conviction and precluded from commitment to a treatment program under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3052. Because the district attorney did not concur with a commitment, the trial court found Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3051 did not apply. Defendant appealed his prison sentence, and the court remanded for resentencing. The court held that those portions of § 3051 and § 3050 requiring district attorney's concurrence for the trial court to order commitment violated the separation of powers, Cal. Const. art. VI, § 1; art. III., and that those portions were severable. Section 3052 only included prior felonies, and defendant's prior conviction was a misdemeanor. The court held that defendant's honorable discharge from his prior conviction entitled him to have the penalties and disabilities released pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 1772, 1779, and included release from the ineligibility provisions of § 3052.


OUTCOME: The court remanded for resentencing pursuant to the mandatory provisions of the narcotic addicts law. The court held that a portion of the law which required the concurrence of the district attorney for commitment to a treatment program violated the state constitution's separation of powers. Defendant's prior conviction was not a felony that disqualified him from treatment program eligibility.

 

100 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Miller


Crim. No. 15749


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 219; 496 P.2d 1228; 101 Cal. Rptr. 860; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 188



May 19, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 150768, W. Howard Hartley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which denied his motion to suppress evidence obtained through an allegedly illegal search and seizure, before convicting him of marijuana possession.


OVERVIEW: Police discovered appellant asleep in the front seat of his car in a private parking lot after he had run out of gas. Police arrested appellant on an outstanding traffic warrant. At the time of arrest, there was electronic equipment and appellant's coat in the back seat of appellant's car. The police asked permission to take the coat and equipment into custody for safekeeping but appellant refused. The police took the property anyway and found marijuana during a search of the coat pockets. Appellant challenged his conviction of marijuana possession, alleging that the search and seizure was unconstitutional. The court reversed the conviction, holding that police did not have the right to take appellant's property into custody after he refused. Furthermore, the police should not have searched the coat's pockets even if appellant had given permission to put the items into safekeeping. The search was not incident to a lawful arrest because the officers did not have any belief of probable cause to arrest relating to the equipment or coat at the time of arrest.


OUTCOME: The court reversed appellant's conviction because the search and seizure was a violation of appellant's constitutional rights, as the police had no probable cause for arrest.

In re Franklin


Crim. No. 15842


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 126; 496 P.2d 465; 101 Cal. Rptr. 553; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 184



May 12, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  On the basis of the foregoing discussion we conclude that petitioner's original commitment to Atascadero was valid. The order to show cause, having served its purpose, is discharged, and the writ of habeas corpus denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus challenging the order of the Los Angeles Superior Court (California), which acquitted defendant by reason of insanity of violating Cal. Penal Code § 148.1 (false bomb report), and committed defendant to a state hospital for the insane. Defendant alleged that the release procedure set forth in Cal. Penal Code § 1026 was invalid as it denied him due process and equal protection of the laws.


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus that challenged the validity of his commitment to a state mental facility after he was found not guilty by reason of insanity for making a false bomb report. He contended that Cal. Penal Code § 1026 was unconstitutional. The court denied defendant's writ and concluded that his original commitment was valid. The court held that the procedures set forth in § 1026, whereby persons may be confined in a state hospital for a minimum period pending a hearing on the question whether they should be released to society, fulfilled the requirements of due process and equal protection of the laws because persons acquitted by reason of insanity were reasonably deemed to fall within a special or exceptional class, having committed a criminal offense, and having proved themselves insane by a preponderance of the evidence and, under such circumstances, a rational basis existed for the difference in treatment accorded such persons. The prehearing commitment period under Cal. Penal Code § 1026(a) was reasonably necessary to provide a minimum opportunity for institutional observation and examination regarding defendant's present sanity.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus and concluded that petitioner's original commitment to the mental institution was valid because the state procedures for confinement and release fulfilled the requirements of due process and equal protection of the laws.

 

102 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Volkswagen Pacific, Inc. v. Los Angeles


L.A. No. 29842


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 48; 496 P.2d 1237; 101 Cal. Rptr. 869; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 180



May 10, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 907145, William H. Levit, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded for further proceedings in conformity with the views herein expressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant car companies challenged the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found that all taxes, interest, and penalties were properly assessed against appellants by respondent city. The Court of Appeal, Second Appellate District, Division One (California) reversed, and a hearing was granted by the court for the purpose of giving further consideration to the issues raised.


OVERVIEW: Appellant car companies operated automobile dealerships in respondent city and maintained an inventory at respondent's port, but did not hold inventory or have business offices within the city limits. Pursuant to Los Angeles Municipal Code §§ 21.00, 21.03, and 21.167, respondent assessed business license taxes against appellants, measured by each appellant's gross receipts from sales to area dealerships. Appellants sought a refund of the taxes, which the trial court denied. The lower court reversed the trial court's order. On appeal, the court agreed with the lower court and adopted the lower court's decision, which reversed the order of the trial court, because respondent did not apportion the tax to account for selling activity outside the city limits. Appellants were not immune from tax under U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, cl. 2 because they broke bulk when they distributed and sold the imported automobiles to local dealers. The tax was not barred under U.S. Const. art. I, § 8 given appellants' sufficient local business incidences.


OUTCOME: Adopting the decision of the lower court, the court reversed the trial court's order that denied recovery to appellant car companies because respondent city did not apportion the tax to account for extraterritorial sales. The import-export clause did not apply where appellants distributed and sold the imported automobiles, and the commerce clause did not bar the tax because appellants carried on sufficient local business activities.

Sunset Amusement Co. v. Board of Police Comm'rs


L.A. No. 29882


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 64; 496 P.2d 840; 101 Cal. Rptr. 768; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 181



May 10, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for a rehearing was denied June 29, 1972. Peters, J., and Sullivan, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 963713, Bayard Rhone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, owners and operators, appealed from the judgment by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their petition for writ of mandamus that sought to compel defendant Board of Police Commissioners of the City of Los Angeles to renew appellants' permit to operate a roller skating rink.


OVERVIEW: Defendant Board of Police Commissioners of the City of Los Angeles denied renewal permits to plaintiffs, owners and operators, to operate their roller skating rink for two consecutive years. Plaintiffs petitioned for a writ of mandamus in the lower court to compel defendant to renew the permits, which was denied. Plaintiffs appealed and the court affirmed. The court held that former Los Angeles Municipal Code § 103.31, which set forth grounds for defendant's denial of permits, did not violate plaintiffs' U.S. Const. amend. I right to free speech and association, as defendant exercised its permit powers in a reasonable manner to promote the interest of the public. There was substantial evidence supporting defendant's finding that plaintiffs' operation of the rink did not comport with the peace, health, safety, convenience, good morals, and general welfare of the public, as plaintiffs failed to provide adequate parking facilities, and failed to control or prevent disturbances on or near the premises. Adequate notice was provided to plaintiffs by ordinance, by conditions expressed in the permit and compelling public necessity, and by the notice provided by defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the petition for writ of mandamus filed by plaintiff owners and operators to compel defendant Board of Police Commissioners of the City of Los Angeles to renew their permit to operate a roller skating rink because the ordinance authorizing the denial did not violate plaintiffs' First Amendment rights to free speech and association, and plaintiffs had adequate notice of their violations.
