Young v. Gnoss


S.F. No. 22888


Supreme Court of California


7 Cal. 3d 18; 496 P.2d 445; 101 Cal. Rptr. 533; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 178



May 4, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  It follows that petitioners are not entitled to the relief prayed for with respect to the June 1972 primary. Since there is no reason to believe that any of the parties to this proceeding will not accede to our holdings herein, no purpose would be served by issuing a writ of mandate to compel such compliance with respect to the November 1972 general election. (See Legislature v. Reinecke (1972) 6 Cal. 3d 595, 604 [99 Cal. Rptr. 481, 492 P.2d 385].) For this reason, the petition for peremptory writ is denied. Petitioners shall not recover their attorneys' fees, but all parties shall recover their costs from the State of California. (See Code Civ. Proc., § 1095.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner voters applied for writ of mandate challenging the constitutionality of the provisions of Cal. Const. art. II, § 1 and Cal. Elec. Code § 203, which imposed durational residence requirements of 90 days in the county and 54 days in the precinct as a prerequisite to voting in California, and which closed the voter registration books 54 days before an election.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner voters were denied right to vote in elections because of residency requirements under Cal. Const. art. II, § 1 and Cal. Elec. Code § 203, which imposed durational residence requirements of 90 days in the county and 54 days in the precinct as a prerequisite to voting in California. Petitioners applied for a writ of mandate challenging the constitutionality of the laws. The court held that art. II, § 1 and § 203 were unconstitutional. The court found that both laws violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, since the state could not demonstrate that its residency requirements promoted a compelling government interest and was necessary in that it was the least burdensome means available to achieve that goal. The court noted that the prevention of electoral fraud and the informed use of the ballot by voters knowledgeable about the issues, while compelling governmental interests, were not necessarily achieved by those laws. The court ordered that no residency requirements in excess of 30 days could be constitutionally imposed, and general voter registration must remain open at all times except during the 29 days immediately preceding an election.


OUTCOME: The court held that durational residence requirements for voter registration violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, but refused to issue a writ of mandate to petitioner voters because it found no reason to believe that any of the parties to the proceeding would not accede to its holdings; therefore, no purpose would be served by issuing a writ of mandate to compel such compliance with respect to the next election.

Halpin v. Superior Court of San Bernardino County


L.A. No. 29949


Supreme Court of California


6 Cal. 3d 885; 495 P.2d 1295; 101 Cal. Rptr. 375; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 173



April 24, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the real party in interest for a rehearing was denied May 23, 1972.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the respondeat superior court to suppress the evidence obtained in the execution of the search warrant and from the interception of petitioner's call to his wife from the telephone at the jail.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which denied their motion to set aside the indictment and to suppress evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with several controlled substance offenses in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 182 and Cal. Heath & Safety Code §§ 11531, 11530.5. The trial court denied their motion to set aside their indictments and to suppress evidence obtained from an allegedly invalid search warrant and from an incriminating tape recording of telephone conversation between them made while one defendant was in custody. The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing the lower court to suppress the evidence. The court declared the warrant which resulted in the seizure of marijuana invalid because the observations presented to the issuing magistrate did not corroborate claims of illegal activity and thus did not meet the test that required the magistrate to be informed of some of the underlying circumstances from which an informant drew his conclusions. The court held that the contents of the conversation between defendants were inadmissible because the conversation was a wire communication within the meaning of 18 U.S.C.S. § 2510(1) and was intercepted within the meaning of § 2510(4) and that the interception was not authorized in accordance with § 2516.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing the lower court to suppress the evidence. The court held that the warrant for the search was invalid and the interception of a telephone call between petitioners defendants was unauthorized.

 

106 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Myers


Crim. No. 15948


Supreme Court of California


6 Cal. 3d 811; 494 P.2d 684; 100 Cal. Rptr. 612; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 167



March 21, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Humboldt County, No. 6021, Thomas M. Montgomery, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment, as to each conviction, is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Humboldt County (California) convicting him of drug and paraphernalia possession, following denial of his motion to suppress evidence that was found during a warrantless search of his home and car on grounds that defendant was not entitled to protection under U.S. Const. amend. IV because he was an outpatient from a narcotics rehabilitation center under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3151.


OVERVIEW: After he was found guilty of a drug offense, but before judgment, defendant was committed to a narcotics rehabilitation center pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3051. Nearly two years after he was released as an outpatient, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3151, the parole officer assigned to supervise defendant assisted sheriff's deputies in searching defendant's home and car, upon suspicion that he was dealing in narcotics. They justified the warrantless search on the grounds that, because he was an outpatient under § 3151, he was not entitled to protection under U.S. Const. amend. IV. Defendant's motion to suppress was denied and he was subsequently convicted of drug and paraphernalia possession. Defendant appealed. In reversing, the court ruled that one's status as an outpatient did not, of itself, allow warrantless searches. Persons such as defendant did not lose their Fourth Amendment protections solely on the basis of such status and they were not deemed to have waived such rights. The narcotic addict commitment program was intended to be nonpunitive and rehabilitative and suspension of Fourth Amendment rights was not reasonably related to such purpose.


OUTCOME: The convictions were reversed because defendant, as an outpatient, was entitled to Fourth Amendment protection from warrantless searches, such that the search of his home and car were illegal; defendant's rehabilitation was for nonpunitive purposes; he did not lose important constitutional rights bases solely on his status; and he did not waive his rights against unreasonable searches by accepting outpatient status.

Mathews v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


L.A. No. 29913


Supreme Court of California


6 Cal. 3d 719; 493 P.2d 1165; 100 Cal. Rptr. 301; 37 Cal. Comp. Cases 124; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 161



February 29, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied March 30, 1972. Peters, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Board following reconsideration is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Applicant wife of deceased petitioned for review of the decision of the Workmen's Compensation Appeals Board (California), which denied compensation to deceased on the ground that he was the initial aggressor in the altercation that resulted in his death.


OVERVIEW: Decedent employee died from injuries sustained during an altercation with another employee. The Workmen's Compensation Appeals Board determined that decedent was the initial physical aggressor and was therefore precluded from receiving death benefits under Cal. Lab. Code § 3600(g), and decedent's wife petitioned for review. Decedent's wife contended that the initial physical aggressor finding was error. She also contended that § 3600(g) was unconstitutional in light of Cal. Const. art. XX, § 21, which empowered the legislature to create workmen's compensation benefits irrespective of fault of a party. The court held that decedent was the initial physical aggressor by definition as he initially put his opponent in reasonable fear of bodily harm. The court relied on the legislative history of the workmen's compensation act and of Cal. Const. art. XX, § 21 and concluded that the legislature was not constitutionally prohibited from conditioning the right to compensation upon absence of wilful misconduct. Accordingly the judgment was affirmed because the court found that § 3600(g) was constitutional and that the board did not err in failing to award compensation.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because the court determined that the statutory preclusion of death benefits for a worker who was an initial physical aggressor within the meaning of the governing statute was constitutional and as such the board did not err in denying benefits to decedent.

McDermott v. Superior Court of San Francisco


S.F. No. 22844


Supreme Court of California


6 Cal. 3d 693; 493 P.2d 1161; 100 Cal. Rptr. 297; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 158



February 25, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the real party in interest for a rehearing was denied March 22, 1972.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondent courts to reset petitioners' bail so as to eliminate any penalty assessments which may have been added to the amounts determined to be reasonable bail to insure their appearance in court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought a writ of mandate or prohibition regarding the order of respondent, Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which required petitioners to deposit a 25 percent penalty assessment pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 13521 along with their bail in order to obtain release from custody while awaiting trial.


OVERVIEW: After petitioners were charged with various criminal violations, respondent, trial court, required them to post bail and also to deposit the 25 percent penalty assessment (assessment), pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 13521, in order to obtain their release from custody. Thereafter, petitioners sought a writ of mandate or prohibition to stop respondent from requiring the assessment on the ground that the assessment amounted to excessive bail and, therefore, violated Cal. Const. amend. art. I, § 6. On review, the court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent to eliminate any assessment as applied to petitioners' bail. The court noted that it could not determine whether the assessment was included in the amounts set as reasonable bail or was levied in addition thereto. The court found that the plain language of § 13521 required the assessment to be added to the reasonable bail and thus amounted to an exacted, excessive bail. The court distinguished assessments from fines and commented that the posting of funds, although described as "bail" and penalties in excess thereof, for purposes other than insuring an accused's appearance in court, was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of prohibition directing respondent, trial court, to eliminate from petitioners' reasonable bail any penalty assessment, as exacted pursuant to statute, that was added to the bail. The court noted that any additional sum exacted, for the purpose to secure the appearance of petitioners in court, as bail or a fine was an unconstitutional excessive bail.

 

109 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Anderson


Crim. No. 13617


Supreme Court of California


6 Cal. 3d 628; 493 P.2d 880; 100 Cal. Rptr. 152; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 154



February 18, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied March 17, 1972, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. McComb, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 8039, Robert W. Conyers, Judge.



DISPOSITION: (See fn. 45) The judgment, insofar as it provides for the penalty of death, is modified to provide a punishment of life imprisonment and as so modified is affirmed in all other respects. 45



45 Inasmuch as today's decision is fully retroactive, any prisoner now under a sentence of death, the judgment as to which is final, may file a petition for writ of habeas corpus in the superior court inviting that court to modify its judgment to provide for the appropriate alternative punishment of life imprisonment or life imprisonment without possibility of parole specified by statute for the crime for which he was sentenced to death. Petitions should be filed in the court of territorial jurisdiction in the first instance and transferred by that court to the sentencing court in the event the court with territorial jurisdiction was not the sentencing court.

The issue of the right to bail in cases in which the law has heretofore provided for the death penalty has been raised for the first time by the People and amici curiae on petition for rehearing. Although this question was never an issue in this case, we deem it appropriate to note that article I, section 6, of the California Constitution and section 1270 of the Penal Code, dealing with the subject of bail, refer to a category of offenses for which the punishment of death could be imposed and bail should be denied under certain circumstances. The law thus determined the gravity of such offenses both for the purpose of fixing bail before trial and for imposing punishment after conviction. Those offenses, of course, remain the same but under the decision in this case punishment by death cannot constitutionally be exacted. The underlying gravity of those offenses endures and the determination of their gravity for the purpose of bail continues unaffected by this decision. Accordingly, to subserve such purpose and subject to our future consideration of this issue in an appropriate proceeding, we hold that they remain as offenses for which bail should be denied in conformity with article I, section 6, of the Constitution and Penal Code section 1270 when the proof of guilt is evident or the presumption thereof great.

 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant who was found guilty of first degree murder, attempted murder, and first degree robbery appealed a decision of the trial court, San Diego County (California), under Cal. Penal Code § 1239, which denied his motion for new trial and fixed the penalty at death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant who was guilty of first degree murder, attempted murder, and first degree robbery was sentenced to death. The judgment was affirmed and at the second trial, on the issue of penalty for the murder, the jury again imposed the death penalty. A motion for a new trial was denied and defendant automatically appealed under Cal. Penal Code § 1239. Defendant contended that there was error in selecting the jury, certain evidence was improperly admitted, prosecutor was guilty of prejudicial misconduct, and that the death penalty constituted cruel and unusual punishment. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment in most respects but modified the death penalty to punishment of life imprisonment. The court stated that capital punishment was unconstitutionally cruel and that a cruel punishment was proscribed irrespective of whether it was excessive. The court also stated that a prisoner under a death sentence could file a petition for writ of habeas corpus requesting the court to modify its judgment to provide for an appropriate alternative punishment and that bail should be denied in conformity with Cal. Penal Code § 1270 when proof of guilt was evident.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment in all respects, but modified the penalty of death to punishment of life imprisonment. The court concluded that capital punishment was impermissibly cruel and incompatible with the dignity of man. The court stated that any prisoner, under a sentence of death, could file a petition for writ of habeas corpus inviting the court to modify its judgment and provide an alternative punishment.

Bekiaris v. Board of Education


S.F. No. 22805


Supreme Court of California


6 Cal. 3d 575; 493 P.2d 480; 100 Cal. Rptr. 16; 1972 Cal. LEXIS 151

PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Stanislaus County, No. 103873, Robert D. Carter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the case is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings in conformity with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff probationary teacher challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California), which denied him a writ of mandate to compel defendant Board of Education of the City of Modesto to set aside plaintiff's allegedly wrongful dismissal from employment and to reimburse him for damages suffered as a result of lost employment. Plaintiff contended that he was discharged for exercising his constitutionally protected rights.

The court agreed that defendant failed to give adequate consideration to plaintiff's contention because the administrative record was infected with legal error.

The court remanded, directing the trial court to issue a writ of mandate ordering defendant to show whether the real reason for plaintiff's dismissal was dissatisfaction with his political activities.

Monroe v. Trustees of California State Colleges


L.A. No. 29926


Supreme Court of California


6 Cal. 3d 399; 491 P.2d 1105; 99 Cal. Rptr. 129; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 228



December 30, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied January 26, 1972. McComb, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles, No. 944 675, Richard Schauer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner professor appealed an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles (California) dismissing his petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent, Trustees of the California State Colleges, to reinstate him to his former position and to restore his benefits and salary.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner professor was discharged from his teaching position on the basis of his refusal to sign the "Levering Oath," a loyalty oath formerly embodied in Cal. Gov't Code § 3103. An appellate court had upheld the oath in the face of a constitutional attack, but subsequently, it was found to be unconstitutional. After this decision, petitioner wrote to respondent for reinstatement but was denied. Petitioner then sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent. The trial court sustained a general demurrer to his first amended petition with leave to amend and, upon petitioner's failure to amend, entered an order dismissing his petition. On appeal, the court held that as a matter of law he left his classification with reinstatement rights and thus that, pursuant to Cal. Educ. Code § 22607, he was now entitled to reinstatement. His prior dismissal rested solely on his presently recognized exercise of a constitutionally protected right. The judgment of the trial court was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court ruling against respondent Trustees of the California State Colleges. Petitioner professor was entitled to reinstatement as a matter of law because he left his classification with reinstatement rights solely on his presently recognized exercise of a constitutionally protected right.

 

112 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Love v. Keays


L.A. No. 29925


Supreme Court of California


6 Cal. 3d 339; 491 P.2d 395; 98 Cal. Rptr. 811; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 222



December 17, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' petition for a rehearing was denied January 13, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 957068, Jerry Pacht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant government officials appealed a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiff tenants in their action alleging that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1174 violated equal protection principles.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 526a, plaintiff evicted tenants initiated an injunctive action against defendant government officials. Plaintiffs contended that defendants enforced writs of restitution in unlawful detainer actions pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1174, but refused to accept from plaintiffs claims for exemption of certain types of property, as provided in Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 690 et seq. The trial court granted plaintiffs summary judgment on the grounds that § 1174 violated equal protection principles. On appeal, the court reversed the decision. Section 1174 did not violate equal protection principles because plaintiffs were not entitled to file a claim for exemption. The writ involved restitution of the premises, so that plaintiffs' property was not levied upon under the writ. The statute did not allow one class of plaintiffs exemption, while denying another class of plaintiffs exemption.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision because the statute, which dealt with the enforcement of judgments in unlawful detainer actions, did not violate equal protection principles. Plaintiff tenants were not entitled to file claims for exemption from execution because the writ of restitution involved the premises, not the plaintiffs' property.

114 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Hayes v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 29861


Supreme Court of California


6 Cal. 3d 216; 490 P.2d 1137; 98 Cal. Rptr. 449; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 212



December 6, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  The alternative writ is discharged and the petition for a peremptory writ of mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of mandate compelling respondent, Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), to dismiss the California criminal action on the ground that respondent made no attempt to comply with petitioner's requests that he be returned to the state for a speedy hearing. Petitioner was confined in the Nevada State Prison and was subject to a California detainer based on his violation of a probation order of respondent.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner sought a writ of mandate compelling respondent superior court to dismiss the California criminal action on the ground that respondent made no attempt to comply with petitioner's requests that he be returned to this state for a speedy hearing as to reinstatement of probation or pronouncement of sentence. On appeal, the court declined the relief requested by petitioner. The court concluded, however, that if petitioner wished respondent to dispose finally of the pending action he could invoke the procedure set out in Cal. Penal Code § 1203.2a. That statute provided that a defendant who had been granted probation without imposition of sentence and who was then committed to a prison in this state for another offense could obtain final disposition of the case in which he received probation by requesting imposition of sentence in his absence and without his being represented by counsel. The court held that the legislature's limitation of the procedure to those committed to prison "in this State" denied the constitutionally required equal protection of the laws. Accordingly, the court discharged the alternative writ and denied the petition for a peremptory writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: Court discharged the alternative writ and denied the petition for a peremptory writ of mandate which petitioner sought to compel respondent superior court to dismiss the California criminal case. The court held that if petitioner wished respondent to dispose of the pending case he could invoke the procedure set out for a defendant who was committed to prison "in this state" to obtain final disposition of case in which he received probation.

 

115 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Kaplan v. Superior Court of Orange County


L.A. No. 29878


Supreme Court of California


6 Cal. 3d 150; 491 P.2d 1; 98 Cal. Rptr. 649; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 208



December 1, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the real party in interest for a rehearing was denied December 29, 1971. Wright, C. J., McComb, J., and Burke, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review, by statutory writ of prohibition, of a decision by the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which denied defendant's motion to suppress the evidence. His motion to set aside the information was also denied. Defendant was held to answer to a felony charge of selling a restricted dangerous drug in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code §11912.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought to suppress evidence on the ground of unlawful search and seizure. Defendant also sought a motion to set aside the information for lack of probable cause. Both motions were denied and defendant sought a writ of prohibition to review the lower court's decision. Real party in interest, the People, contended that only the person searched could complain -- i.e., that defendant had no standing to assert the illegality of a search of a third person and prevent the evidence from being used against him at a trial under Cal. Evid. Code § 351. On appeal, the court rejected the People's argument. The court concluded that the legislature did not intend to repeal the rule, and defendant was therefore entitled to the relief for which he prayed. The court concluded that defendant had standing to complain of the admittedly illegal search and seizure of a third person. The court held that since there was no competent evidence to support the information, defendant was therefore entitled to a writ of prohibition to restrain further proceedings against him on this charge.


OUTCOME: The court let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue to restrain further proceedings against him on the charge of selling a restricted dangerous drug. The court concluded that defendant had standing to complain of the admittedly illegal search and seizure of a third person, and since there was therefore no competent evidence to support the information, defendant was entitled to the writ.

County of Los Angeles v. Ortiz


L.A. No. 29899


Supreme Court of California


6 Cal. 3d 141; 490 P.2d 1142; 98 Cal. Rptr. 454; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 207; 68 A.L.R.3d 538



November 30, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. 915497, 921675, 932191, Richard Barry, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The order of the trial court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which refused to tax as costs against respondent county the fee charged by an expert witness for his testimony and report in eminent domain proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Respondent county instituted proceedings in eminent domain to acquire land held by appellant property owners. Subsequently, appellants sought to tax as costs against respondent the fee charged by an expert witness for his testimony and report. Respondent objected to the allowance of these items, and the trial court found in respondent's favor. On appeal, appellants asserted that the county must pay the fees of the expert witness by virtue of the prohibition of U.S. Const. amend. V and Cal. Const. art. I, § 14 against the taking of private property for public use without just compensation. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that because allowable costs are of policy as distinguished from constitutional dimension, determination of costs which were permissibly recoverable remained with the legislature rather than the courts. The court held that because there was no statutory authority for awarding litigation costs, as that term had been used in this case, the trial court properly upheld the county's objections to the cost bills.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court, which denied appellant property owners' request to tax cost of expert witness fees to respondent county. The court held that because there was no statutory authority for awarding litigation costs, the trial court properly upheld respondent's objections to the cost bills.

In re Cortez


Crim. No. 15671


Supreme Court of California


6 Cal. 3d 78; 490 P.2d 819; 98 Cal. Rptr. 307; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 202



November 18, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied December 16, 1971.



DISPOSITION: The writ is granted and petitioner is remanded to the custody of the Superior Court of Riverside County with directions to that court to vacate its order of November 2, 1970, to issue an order to show cause why petitioner's prior conviction should not be stricken for purposes of sentencing, and to proceed in accordance with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an incarcerated prisoner, sought a writ of habeas corpus to obtain relief from a judgment of the Superior Court for the County of Riverside (California), which convicted him of four counts of selling or offering to sell heroin and sentenced him on each count to the state prison enhanced by a prior narcotics felony conviction.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner challenged his sentence for his convictions of four counts of selling or offering to sell heroin that was enhanced by a prior narcotics felony conviction under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11718. The prisoner claimed that the sentencing court denied his constitutional rights in not granting him a hearing at which he could be present and be represented by counsel. The court found that the trial court did not grant the prisoner a hearing, did not appoint counsel for him, determine that he desired and was competent to represent himself, and did not order that he be brought in person before the court, but merely reviewed the record on file in the case and refused to strike the prior conviction. The court previously held that § 11718 violated the California Constitution and invited persons to file petitions in the exercise of discretion to dismiss the prior convictions. The court, thus, granted the writ and remanded the case to the trial court to show cause why the prisoner's prior conviction should not be stricken for purposes of sentencing.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ of habeas corpus and remanded to the trial court to show cause why petitioner criminal's prior conviction should not be stricken for purposes of sentencing. The court held that the trial court wrongfully did not grant petitioner a hearing and did not appoint him counsel prior to determining whether his prior felony conviction should be stricken.

The court concluded that respondent erred in his determination that existing regulations provided no means by which petitioner could obtain the needed individualized nursing care. 119 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


State Dep't of Corrections v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


Sac. No. 7895


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 885; 489 P.2d 818; 97 Cal. Rptr. 786; 36 Cal. Comp. Cases 638; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 292



October 28, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  The award is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, Department of Corrections sought review of a decision by respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeals Board (California), which increased the compensation award to an employee because of serious and wilful misconduct of petitioner's employee. The question before the court was whether under Cal. Gov't Code § 818, an award can be increased because of serious and wilful misconduct of an employee when the employer is a state agency .


OVERVIEW: An employee at a state prison suffered an industrial injury as the results of serious and wilful misconduct of another employee. Respondent, Workmen's Compensation Appeals Board, increased the employee's award by one-half pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code § 4553. Appellant, Department of Corrections, contended that Cal. Gov't Code § 818, prohibited punitive damage awards against a state agency. In addition, appellant contended the award violated Cal. Const. art. XX, § 21. The court affirmed the award of additional benefits because the injury was caused by serious and wilful misconduct of a fellow employee. The Court held that to be classified as "punitive damages," the award must be in excess of actual loss. That under the present workmen's compensation program, employees are awarded only partial compensated for their damages. And that under Cal. Lab. Code § 4553, appellant was penalized in the sense that it was required to pay a higher amount of compensation by reason of the serious and wilful misconduct, the employee, however, does not receive more than full compensation for his injuries. Thus, the increased award was not a penalty.


OUTCOME: The award of the Workmen's Compensation Appeals Board was affirmed. Requiring the Department of Corrections to pay an increased award because of the serious and wilful misconduct of one its employees that caused the injury was not a penalty since the injured employee does not receive more than full compensation for his injuries.

Van Nuys Publ'g Co. v. Thousand Oaks


L.A. No. 29866


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 817; 489 P.2d 809; 97 Cal. Rptr. 777; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 287; 3 ERC (BNA) 1178



October 18, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied November 17, 1971. McComb, J., and Burke, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 49834, Richard C. Heaton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff publishing company challenged a decision from the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which rendered judgment in favor of defendant city upholding the constitutionality of an "anti-littering" ordinance, Thousand Oaks, Cal. City Ordinance 98, § 4.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff publishing company filed an action that sought to enjoin defendant city from enforcing an "anti-littering" ordinance alleging that the ordinance unconstitutionally abridged First Amendment Rights. U.S. Const. amend. I. The trial court rendered judgment in favor of defendant and held that the ordinance was constitutional. Upon review, the court reversed. The court held that the ordinance was unconstitutional where its provisions went far beyond proscribing those activities that constituted littering and prohibited persons from being allowed to enter onto property to distribute or circulate written material. The court found that the First Amendment afforded persons the right to practice house-to-house distribution of written material and that the requirement of the ordinance that distributors obtain prior consent to deliver the written material would operate to curtail this means of communication and was unconstitutional. The court found the ordinance was also unconstitutional where the city could have enacted a less restrictive alternative for protecting "unwilling listeners" by prohibiting distributors from delivering material to occupants that expressed an objection.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that held that a city "anti-littering" ordinance was constitutional. The court held the ordinance unconstitutionally proscribed the protected activity of distributing and circulating written materials door-to-door and was overly broad. The court also found that because there was less restrictive alternatives to prevent littering and to protect unwilling listeners the ordinance was unconstitutional.

Bogacki v. Board of Supervisors


L.A. No. 29791


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 771; 489 P.2d 537; 97 Cal. Rptr. 657; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 285



October 8, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 94564, Alexander B. Yakutis, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a former permanent county employee sought a writ of mandate to direct respondent, the Board of Supervisors of Riverside County (California), to reinstate him to his former position from which he was terminated for insubordination and reimburse him for lost compensation, after respondent denied his request.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, a former county employee, was discharged from his employee status by his supervisor on the grounds of insubordination. The court denied petitioner's request for a writ of mandate to direct respondent, the county board of supervisors, to reinstate him and reimburse him for lost compensation. Riverside County, Cal., Ordinance No. 440 and Riverside County, Cal., Resolution No. 440-805(2)(d) granted respondent the authority to declare petitioner eligible for future county employment, but did not grant it the authority to restore him to the position from which he was removed. The court found that there was substantial evidence supporting petitioner's dismissal and that the head of petitioner's former department had the absolute power to remove from his particular department an employee deemed unsuitable by him within the terms of Ordinance No. 440 and Resolution No. 440-805(2)(d).


OUTCOME: The court denied the request by petitioner, a former permanent county employee, for a writ of mandate because there was substantial evidence supporting his dismissal. The governing resolution granted respondent, the county board of supervisors, the authority to declare him eligible for future county employment, but not to restore him to the position from which he was removed.

People v. Mason


Crim. No. 15480


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 759; 488 P.2d 630; 97 Cal. Rptr. 302; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 284



September 22, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR-20491, Robert O. Staniforth, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of dismissal is hereby vacated and the cause remanded to the Superior Court of San Diego County for further proceedings not inconsistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People challenged a decision from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which dismissed burglary charges against defendant. Defendant asserted that law enforcement officers had obtained evidence without a warrant and without his consent in violation of his U.S. Const. amend. IV rights.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a probationer, was charged with burglary. At a pre-trial hearing, defendant filed a motion to suppress evidence, contending that it had been obtained by an illegal search in violation of his Fourth Amendment rights. The trial court granted defendant's motion to suppress. The court reversed the decision of the trial court. The court noted that one of the conditions of defendant's probation required him to submit to search and seizure at any time with or without a search warrant whenever requested to do so. The court concluded that the probation condition, by use of the words "with or without a search warrant," had been intended to restrict defendant's right to refuse a warrantless search. The court found that when defendant in order to obtain probation specifically agreed to permit a warrantless search he had voluntarily waived whatever claim of privacy he might otherwise have had.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court. It found that the trial court improperly excluded evidence obtained as a result of a warrantless search of defendant probationer, because when defendant in order to obtain probation specifically agreed to permit warrantless searches he had voluntarily waived whatever claim of privacy he might otherwise have had.

County of Alameda v. Carleson


S.F. Nos. 22820, 22816, 22817, Sac. No.7898


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 730; 488 P.2d 953; 97 Cal. Rptr. 385; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 282



September 21, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 408961, Robert H. Kroninger, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant welfare rights organization challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which issued a peremptory mandate that ordered respondent Director of the Department of Social Welfare to issue an emergency regulation which implemented provisions of the Social Security Act set forth in 42 U.S.C.S. § 602(a).


OVERVIEW: Provisions of the Social Security Act set forth in 42 U.S.C.S. § 602(a) required the states to implement regulations designed to provide an incentive to employment for recipients of Aid to Families with Dependent Children (AFDC) grants by permitting them to disregard a portion of their earned income in determining whether their level of income qualified them for an AFDC payment. The disregard was available to all families except those whose earned income exceeded their standard of need and whose needs had not been met by an AFDC payment within the past four months. The Director issued a regulation providing that any family whose earned income exceeded their standard of need was entitled to the disregard if it had received an AFDC grant within the past four months. The trial court ordered respondent to issue an emergency regulation denying benefits in such cases. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that Congress intended to promote employment incentives and that the emergency regulation conflicted with the mandate in Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11008 to permit the exclusion of earned income to the maximum extent permitted by federal law.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the trial court to order defendant Director of the Department of Social Welfare to rescind the emergency regulation and to take whatever steps might be necessary to reinstate, with retroactive benefits, all prior recipients of AFDC improperly terminated from the AFDC program. The emergency regulation would conflict with the statutory mandate to permit the exclusion of earned income to the maximum extent permitted by federal law.

Burrey v. Embarcadero Municipal Improv. Dist.


L.A. No. 29837


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 671; 488 P.2d 395; 97 Cal. Rptr. 203; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 278



September 13, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' petition for a rehearing was denied October 13, 1971.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner residents of respondent Embarcadero Municipal Improvement District (EMID) instituted a mandamus proceeding that challenged the voting provisions under 1969 Cal. Stat. 174, which governed election of the board of directors of the district on the ground that the provisions violated the one person, one vote rule required by the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend XIV.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Embarcadero Municipal Improvement District (EMID) was created to promote development of uninhabited land. The district provided major services and utilities which cities normally supplied their residents. Pursuant to 1969 Cal. Stat. 174, the governing board of the district was elected pursuant to a system which vested long-term and virtually total control of the district in the hands of the developing landowner, with non-landowning residents having no vote at all. Petitioner residents of EMID instituted a mandamus proceeding that challenged the voting provisions. The court first held that the mandamus proceeding was proper because the issue was of great public importance and needed to be resolved promptly, in light of the upcoming governing board election. The court held that the one person, one vote rule which had previously been applied to state and federal governmental elections also applied to local government units having general governmental powers over a geographical area. The court held that EMID had shown no compelling state interest in the scheme, and that the voting scheme was therefore unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend XIV.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate, which directed respondent Embarcadero Municipal Improvement District and its board of directors to conduct its elections in accordance with the one person, one vote rule required by the Fourteenth Amendment.

Methodist Hosp. of Sacramento v. Saylor


Sac. No. 7869


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 685; 488 P.2d 161; 97 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 279



September 13, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner hospitals sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent department of public health to adopt necessary rules and regulations to entertain petitioners' applications for state insurance on private construction loans pursuant to the Health Facility Construction Loan Insurance Law. The case was before the court on an alternative writ issued by the Court of Appeal (California).


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to the Health Facility Construction Loan Insurance Law (Law), petitioner hospitals applied for state insurance on construction loans to finance the building of health facilities. Respondent department of public health, which administered the program, declined to entertain the applications because bond counsel had raised questions regarding the legislature's power to authorize the issuance of debentures under the program. Petitioners then filed a proceeding for a writ of mandate to compel respondent to adopt necessary rules to consider the applications. The court observed that the Law was enacted after the adoption of Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 21.5, the purpose of which was to remove restrictions on the legislature's ability to create liabilities against the credit of the state insofar as necessary to permit enactment of a program of health facility construction loan insurance such as the Law. Thus, because there were no prohibitions in the constitution on the legislature's plenary authority in this regard, the court ruled that the Law was constitutional and the use of debentures was a reasonable interpretation of the language of § 21.5. Thus, the court issued the writ.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the issuance of a peremptory writ of mandate to compel respondent department of public health to adopt rules and regulations necessary to entertain the applications of petitioner hospitals for insurance on construction loans pursuant to the Health Facility Construction Loan Insurance Law (Law) because it found that the Law was constitutional.

Perrine v. Municipal Court for East Los Angeles Judicial Dist.


L.A. No. 29852


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 656; 488 P.2d 648; 97 Cal. Rptr. 320; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 276



September 3, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner bookstore operator sought a writ of prohibition to restrain respondent Municipal Court for East Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California) from taking further proceedings in criminal actions charging him with violating Los Angeles, Cal., Ordinance 5860, § 321, for prohibition of the operation of a bookstore without first securing a license from the Los Angeles County Public Welfare Commission.


OVERVIEW: The Los Angeles County Public Welfare Commission denied petitioner bookstore operator's application for a bookstore license without opinion. Petitioner appealed the decision to the license appeals board. While the appeal was pending, petitioner was twice arrested and charged with operating a bookstore without a license. Respondent trial court overruled petitioner's demurrers to the complaints and set the case for trial. Petitioner sought the writ of prohibition to restrain respondent from proceeding with the trials. The court issued the preemptory writ of prohibition because the city ordinance set no adequate standards for issuing licenses. Thus, it constituted an invalid prior restraint on the exercise of freedoms guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend. I and was unconstitutional on its face. The court also held that it was constitutionally impermissible to deny an applicant a license to operate a bookstore solely upon the ground that he had suffered a prior criminal conviction. Since the licensing ordinance was unconstitutional on its face, respondent lacked jurisdiction of the criminal proceedings against petitioner.


OUTCOME: The court issued a preemptory writ of prohibition because the city ordinance set no adequate standards for issuing licenses and, thus, constituted an invalid prior restraint on the exercise of freedoms guaranteed by the First Amendment. Since the licensing ordinance was unconstitutional on its face, respondent trial court lacked jurisdiction over the criminal proceedings against petitioner bookstore operator.

 

127 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Ryman v. American Nat'l Ins. Co.


L.A. No. 29827


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 620; 488 P.2d 32; 96 Cal. Rptr. 728; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 274



August 30, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 154703, Robert P. Kneeland, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which held in favor of defendant insurance company in its suit to recover medical and hospital benefits under the insurance policy.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were injured in a car accident and brought action against defendant, an insurance company, to recover medical and hospital benefits under an insurance policy. Defendant asserted that the policy had lapsed for nonpayment of premium prior to the date on which the injuries had been incurred. Plaintiffs claimed that the policy had been reinstated at the time of the accident and alleged that the insurance company had accepted a late-tendered premium more than a week before the accident occurred. The court entered judgment for defendant. Plaintiffs appealed. The court concluded that the policy was in effect at the time of the accident, because under the terms of the policy, the policy was automatically reinstated if the company accepted a payment of an overdue premium without issuing a conditional receipt and without requiring an application for reinstatement. The company did accept a late payment by plaintiffs and did not notify plaintiffs until several weeks later that its initial acceptance was only a conditional one. Thus, the policy was reinstated upon acceptance and was in force on the date of the accident.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of defendant, finding that the insurance policy in question was reinstated upon defendant's acceptance of a late payment tendered by plaintiffs and was in full force on the date of the accident.

 

128 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Serrano v. Priest


L.A. No. 29820


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 584; 487 P.2d 1241; 96 Cal. Rptr. 601; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 273; 41 A.L.R.3d 1187



August 30, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' petition for a rehearing was denied October 21, 1971, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. McComb, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 938254, Robert W. Kenny, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded to the trial court with directions to overrule the demurrers and to allow defendants a reasonable time within which to answer.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, public school children and parents, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained defendant state and county officials' demurrers for failure to state a cause of action. Plaintiffs sought declaratory and injunctive relief against defendants, who were charged with administering the California public school financing system, claiming the system was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, public school children and parents, brought an action against defendant state and county officials, claiming the public school financing system was discriminatory and violated the constitution because it was primarily based on wealth generated from local property taxes. The action was dismissed for failure to allege a sufficient cause of action, and plaintiffs appealed. The court held that the system denied equal protection to plaintiffs and others similarly situated. Without deciding the merits, the court held that plaintiffs sufficiently stated their causes of action, reversed the dismissal, and remanded. The court held that the finance system discriminated against the poor because it made the quality of a child's education a function of the wealth of his parents and neighbors. The court recognized the right to an education was a fundamental interest and that wealth was a suspect classification. The court applied the strict scrutiny standard to determine whether the system violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and it found no compelling state interest which was furthered by the financing system.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded with directions to overrule the demurrers. The court concluded that education was a fundamental interest and that the state education finance system, based on local real property taxes, was discriminatory on the basis of wealth in violation of the equal protection clause.

 

129 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Jolicoeur v. Mihaly


S.F. Nos. 22826, 22827, L.A. No. 29906


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 565; 488 P.2d 1; 96 Cal. Rptr. 697; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 272



August 27, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  Let the peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondent voting registrars to determine each individual petitioner's residence for voting purposes strictly in accordance with Elections Code, sections 14280- 14292, and by the same standards, tests, and procedures as are applied to persons 21 years of age or older.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner minors invoked the original jurisdiction of the court, seeking writs of mandate to order respondents to register petitioners according to the same procedures and qualifications followed for adult registrants under Cal. Elec. Code § 14280-14292.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner minors, who were unmarried, sought to register to vote in the jurisdiction which they claimed to be their actual permanent residence. Respondents refused to register petitioners, because they did not register at their parents' address. The court granted petitioners a writ of mandate, because they were entitled to vote where they resided based on U.S. Const. amend. XXVI. Respondents improperly refused to accept affidavits of residence from unmarried minors unless the residence claimed was that of the minor's parents. Respondents misapplied Cal. Gov't Code § 244(d), (f) in providing that an unmarried minor's residence was that of his parents and could not be changed by the minor's action alone. The court relied on other California law governing parent-child relations in reaching its determination. Thus, both under federal constitutional law and state law, respondents were required to treat all citizens 18 years of age or older alike for purposes relating to voting.


OUTCOME: The court granted the request of petitioner minors for a writ of mandate to order respondents to register them to vote. Respondents were required by both federal constitutional law and state law to permit emancipated minors to vote where they actually resided, rather than requiring that they vote where their parents lived.

 

130 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Randone v. Appellate Dep't of Superior Court


Sac. No. 7885


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 536; 488 P.2d 13; 96 Cal. Rptr. 709; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 271



August 26, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On September 23, 1971, the opinion was modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandamus issue directing the appellate department to issue an order directing the trial court to dissolve the challenged attachment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner debtors filed an application for an original writ to review respondent lower court's decision to maintain an attachment by real party in interest, unsecured contract creditor, under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 537(1).


OVERVIEW: In a collection action, real party in interest, unsecured contract creditor, obtained a writ of attachment against petitioner debtors pursuant to Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 537(1). After unsuccessful attempts to dissolve the attachment and exhaustion of procedural measures on appeal, petitioners applied for an original writ to review respondent lower court's decision to maintain the attachment. The court granted the petition to issue a peremptory writ of mandamus, concluding that the prejudgment attachment procedure under § 537(1), which permitted the attachment of petitioners' property without prior notice or hearing upon the filing of the contract action, violated procedural due process as guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend. V and XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 13. Due process required that, except in extraordinary circumstances, an individual be guaranteed a right to notice and hearing before deprivation of a significant interest. Section 537(1), however, was not narrowly drawn to confine attachments to extraordinary situations but instead broadly permitted the attachment of a debtor's necessities of life prior to a hearing on the validity of a creditor's claim.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the issuance of a peremptory writ of mandamus directing respondent lower court to issue an order requiring the trial court to dissolve the challenged attachment. The prejudgment attachment procedure permitting real party in interest, unsecured contract creditor to attach petitioner debtors' property without prior notice or hearing upon the filing of its action violated procedural due process standards.

Brown v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 29879


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 509; 487 P.2d 1224; 96 Cal. Rptr. 584; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 269



August 19, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the respondent court to vacate its orders dismissing the action and sustaining the demurrer, to overrule the demurrer and to permit defendants reasonable time to answer or otherwise plead.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Secretary of State sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent court to vacate an order dismissing an action seeking imposition of civil penalties on the real party in interest for alleged failure to comply with campaign election laws requiring certain disclosures.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner Secretary of State sought to impose penalties on the real party in interest for violating campaign election laws in Cal. Elec. Code § 11890, which required that associations disclose certain financial information. After respondent court dismissed the action, petitioner filed for a writ of mandate, which the court granted. Respondent was ordered to vacate its dismissal order. Respondent erred in concluding that § 11890 resulted in invidious discrimination against those campaigning for and against state propositions and was therefore unconstitutional. Petitioner had a beneficial interest in the matter, because he bore overall responsibility for administering the challenged disclosure laws. There was no showing that the disputed disclosure requirements resulted in invidious discrimination between ballot measures and candidate elections. The uncertainty engendered by respondent's order of dismissal required final resolution, so petitioner could be properly and fully informed on his public responsibilities. Thus, the mandate order compelled a determination on the merits of the underlying action.


OUTCOME: The court granted the request of petitioner Secretary of State for a writ of mandamus against respondent court following dismissal of an action to enforce election laws requiring certain disclosures by associations, such as the real party in interest. Petitioner was entitled to resolution of the case on the merits to enable him to fulfill his duties to enforce such laws.

 

132 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Younger v. County of El Dorado


Sac. No. 7896


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 480; 487 P.2d 1193; 96 Cal. Rptr. 553; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 268; 1 ELR 20429; 3 ERC (BNA) 1010



August 17, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner people sought a writ of mandate commanding respondent counties to pay amounts allotted to them under the Tahoe Regional Planning Compact, Cal. Gov't Code § 66801.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to its powers under the Tahoe Regional Planning Compact (compact), Cal. Gov't Code § 66801, the planning agency apportioned an amount of its budget to respondent counties. Respondents, however, refused to pay the sums required to support the agency's activities. As a result, petitioner people sought to require respondents to make the payments by burning a mandamus action. Respondents contended, however, that they were not required to make any payments because the compact was unconstitutional and void. The court rejected respondents' arguments and issued a peremptory writ of mandate based on its conclusion that the compact did not violate Cal. Const. art. XI, §§ 11, 12, or 13. The constitutional provisions, which conferred upon local governmental bodies broad powers over purely local affairs, were not violated because the compact conferred powers upon the agency for regional purposes only and did not delegate the power to enact penal legislation to the agency. Further, the compact provision permitting respondents to impose a property tax pay the sums at issue was constitutional because the funds were raised for regional rather than local purposes.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition for a writ of mandate commanding respondent counties to pay amounts allotted to them under the Tahoe Regional Planning Compact (compact). The compact did not violate state constitutional provisions that provided local governmental bodies with power over local matters because its subject matter was of regional concern.

 

133 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


California Beer Wholesalers Asso. v. Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board


L.A. Nos. 29858, 29859


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 402; 487 P.2d 745; 96 Cal. Rptr. 297; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 262



August 11, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the real party in interest for a rehearing was denied September 8, 1971. Burke, J., did not participate therein. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board is annulled; the cause is remanded for proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, beer wholesalers association and director, sought review of an order of respondent, Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board (California), which granted a wholesale beer and wine license to real party in interest beer and wine retailer under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 25502 and 25506.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest beer and wine retailer sought and received a wholesale beer and wine license for a separate division of its corporation under an order from respondent, Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board. Petitioners, beer wholesalers association and director, appealed the order, and the court reversed and remanded holding that such grant of licenses was prohibited under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 25502 and 25506. The court held that although § 25502 actually forbade a beer and wine wholesaler from holding an off-sale retail license, it also necessarily prohibited the holder of an off-sale retail license from obtaining a beer and wine wholesale license. The court held that once the retailer of alcoholic beverages acquired a wholesale beer and wine license, that retailer automatically held the wholesale license; he was accordingly a beer and wine wholesaler holding a retail liquor license, and this integration of licenses was specifically forbidden by § 25502. The court held that to rule otherwise would be to create the anomalous situation that the right to hold both licenses would depend upon the fortuity of the order in which a party applied for them.


OUTCOME: Order granting a wholesale beer and wine license to real party in interest beer and wine retailer was annulled and the cause remanded because, under the applicable statutes, an entity holding an off-sale retail beer and wine license was prohibited from also holding a wholesale beer and wine license.

Flournoy v. Priest


Sac. No. 7900


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 350; 486 P.2d 689; 95 Cal. Rptr. 793; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 257



July 9, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue as prayed. This judgment is final forthwith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the State Controller, sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent, the State Treasurer, to register a demand and adopt a resolution providing for the issuance of state notes as authorized by Cal. Gov't Code §§ 17300-17302.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, the State Controller, sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent, the State Treasurer, to register a demand and adopt a resolution providing for the issuance of state notes as authorized by the Cal. Gov't Code §§ 17300- 17302. These sections were part of the Cal. Const. ch.223, which was enacted as an urgency measure to provide an additional means of temporary borrowing to meet cash flow needs, and avoid more costly registered warrants. Respondent refused to register petitioner's demand and to adopt a resolution providing for the issuance of notes on the ground that ch. 223 violated the California Constitution. She contended that the issuance of notes without an election would create a state indebtedness in violation of the Cal. Const., art. XVI, § 1; that such issuance would result in payments from the state treasury without warrants duly drawn thereon by the Controller in violation of the Cal. Const., art. XIII, § 21; and that ch. 223 was invalid as an urgency measure because changed the duties of the Controller and Treasurer in violation of the Cal. Const., art. IV, § 8. After review, the court found no merit in any of these contentions so the writ was issued.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate compelling respondent, the State Treasurer, to register petitioner, the State Controller's, demand and to adopt a resolution providing for the issuance of notes because the California constitution was not violated.

 

135 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Smith


Crim. No. 15161


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 313; 486 P.2d 1213; 96 Cal. Rptr. 13; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 253



July 7, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 11089, Paul R. Borton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is reversed and the matter is remanded to the superior court for proceedings not inconsistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order entered by the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which directed that his commitment to the California Youth Authority be extended for a period of five years pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 1800-1803. Defendant contended that the provisions of § 1802 violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV because it irrationally and unreasonably discriminated between classes of wards.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for rape when he was a juvenile and committed to the California Youth Authority. Prior to his release the state obtained an order from the trial court which extended his commitment for a five-year period, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§1800-1803, on the ground that he was dangerous to the public. Defendant contended that § 1802 violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court reversed the order and remanded to the trial court. The court first held that, based upon its decision in a companion case, defendant was entitled to a jury trial on the issue of whether he was dangerous. The court held that the differences that existed among various classes of Youth Authority wards for purposes of establishing the maximum period of confinement under an original commitment were not relevant to the later determination required by § 1800. The court observed that although the state needed only to show a rational connection between a statute and the legitimate purpose, where a fundamental interest in freedom was involved, the state had the burden to establish that it had a compelling interest to make the distinctions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that confined defendant for an additional five-year period. The court held that defendant was entitled to have a jury decide whether he was dangerous. The court also held that the distinctions set forth in the statutory provisions that governed continued commitment procedures was arbitrary and wholly unjustifiable. The case was remanded to the trial court for compliance with the opinion.

In re Gary W.


Crim. No. 15215


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 296; 486 P.2d 1201; 96 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 252



July 7, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 275-631, Robert A. Wenke, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is vacated and the matter is remanded to the juvenile court for proceedings not inconsistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order entered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted the state's Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 1800- 1803 petition to extend defendant's confinement beyond the period for which he was originally sentenced as a juvenile. Defendant contended that his continued confinement violated the cruel and unusual punishment and equal protections provisions of U.S. Const. amends. VIII and XIV.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced to confinement as a juvenile offender. Prior to his release, the state contended that defendant, now an adult, was dangerous to society and filed a petition under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 1800- 1803 to extend his confinement beyond his release date. The trial court ordered confinement. The court vacated the order and remanded the matter to the trial court. The court held that the statutory schemes of §§ 1800-1803 did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment. The court found that the confinement procedures were civil in nature because they did not require penal institution housing, and their purpose was to mandate treatment for defendant. The court stated that if defendant was not receiving mandatory treatment, he could seek his release through a habeas corpus procedure. The court also held that, except for mental illness and juvenile proceedings, in the absence of a compelling state purpose for the distinction between classes of persons subject to a commitment pursuant to § 1800 and to other classes of persons subject to involuntary confinement, the right to jury trial is a requirement of both due process and equal protection of the law.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the continued confinement order and remanded the case to the trial court. The court held that the statutory procedures to extend confinement of a juvenile beyond his release date did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment. The court also held that defendant was entitled to a jury trial because there was no compelling state purpose that distinguished his confinement proceeding from other confinement procedures.

Blair v. Pitchess


L.A. No. 29848


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 258; 486 P.2d 1242; 96 Cal. Rptr. 42; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 250; 45 A.L.R.3d 1206



July 1, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 942966, Jerry Pacht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county officials sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) granting summary judgment to respondents resident taxpayers. The district court had entered a permanent injunction in the taxpayers' action, which alleged that California's claim and delivery law, Cal. Code Civ. Proc. §§ 509- 521, violated the California and United States Constitutions.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers sought to enjoin the county officials from expending their own time and the time of other county officials, paid for with county funds, in executing the claim and delivery process. The county officials contended that the debtors involved waived their Fourth Amendment rights by consenting to the seizure of the property. The court held that the claim-and-delivery law, Cal. Code Civ. Proc. §§ 509- 521, was violative of federal and state constitutional provisions prohibiting unreasonable searches and seizures and forbidding the denial of due process of law. In so holding, the court rejected the contention that in most cases a debtor contractually waived his constitutional protection by signing a clause contained in most sales contracts giving the seller authority to enter and repossess upon default. Under the law, the debtors were deprived of their right to due process whether or not such a waiver had been signed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment permanently enjoining the county officials from taking property without a hearing or entering a private place to search and seize personal property without probable cause because the court determined that the claim and delivery law was unconstitutional.

Esteybar v. Municipal Court for Long Beach Judicial Dist.


L.A. No. 29822


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 119; 485 P.2d 1140; 95 Cal. Rptr. 524; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 241



June 22, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the real party in interest for a rehearing was denied July 21, 1971.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent Municipal Court for the Long Beach Judicial District (California) to proceed upon her charge of possession of marijuana, a violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530, as if she had been arraigned on a misdemeanor complaint, contending that Cal. Penal Code § 17(b)(5) violated the separation of powers prohibition, Cal. Const. art. III, § 1, in requiring the consent of the prosecutor.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was charged with possession of marijuana, an offense which could have been treated as either a felony or a misdemeanor. At the preliminary hearing, the municipal court attempted to hold petitioner on a misdemeanor charge, but it was prevented from exercising its discretion because the prosecuting attorney withheld consent under Cal. Penal Code § 17(b)(5). Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to compel the municipal court to proceed upon her case as if she had been arraigned on a misdemeanor. The court held that because a prosecutor could not be vested with authority to foreclose the exercise of a judicial power, requiring his consent to determine that an offense was a misdemeanor violated the doctrine of separation of powers, Cal. Const. art. III, § 1. Under separation of powers, petitioner was entitled to have an independent determination of whether she should have been held to answer on a felony or a misdemeanor. That the power was conferred on the municipal court by statute did not prevent its exercise from being a judicial act. The exercise of a judicial power could not be conditioned upon the approval of either the executive or legislative branches of government.


OUTCOME: The court granted a writ of mandate issue to compel the municipal court to proceed upon the case as though petitioner had been arraigned on a misdemeanor because the requirement that the prosecutor consent before respondent could exercise the power to determine that a charged offense was to be tried as a misdemeanor was a violation of the separation of powers doctrine.

Estate of Horman


L.A. No. 29832


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 62; 485 P.2d 785; 95 Cal. Rptr. 433; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 237



June 10, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 932697, James F. Judge, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As to claimants Uliana Andreevna Gumen, Evdokia Andreevna Lavrik, Anna Ivanovna Petlevany and Vera Ivanovna Vinichenko the judgment is reversed and the trial court is instructed to enter judgment that the State of California is entitled by escheat to the distribution of the interests to which those claimants would otherwise have been entiled. Pursuant to rule 26(a) of the Rules of Court, each party shall bear his own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of a decree by the Court of Appeal, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two (California), ordering that respondent nonresident aliens were entitled to distribution of specified proportionate shares of decedent's estate.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court ordered that respondent nonresident aliens were entitled to inherit the estate of decedent, and the state sought reversal of the decree. The state contended respondents did not appear and demand their interest in the estate within five years from the time of succession as required by Cal. Prob. Code § 1026. The trial court had ruled that the statute had been tolled. The court reversed the judgment in favor of the state. The court held that the trial court erred in ruling that the statute of limitations in § 1026 was tolled. The court held that the trial court acted within its discretion in permitting the state to assert the issue of the statute of limitations at the second trial because respondents did not claim surprise not was it suggested that any additional evidence with respect to the issue might have been adduced. The court held that the law of the case doctrine did not apply because the § 1026 problem was not raised by either party nor expressly determined by the trial court. The court held that § 1026 did not constitute an invidious discrimination against respondents depriving them of equal protection of laws under U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and ordered that decedent's estate escheat to the state rather than be distributed to respondent nonresident aliens. Respondents did not appear and demand their interest in the estate within five years from the time of succession as statutorily required. Application of the statute to respondents did not deny them equal protection of the laws.
