People v. Rizer


Crim. No. 15047


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 35; 484 P.2d 1367; 95 Cal. Rptr. 23; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 232



May 27, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, Floyd C. Dodson and Vernon W. Hunt, * Judges.


* Retired judge of the municipal court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the convictions for issuing a check without sufficient funds and assault with a caustic chemical in Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California).


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded not guilty by reason of insanity to a charge of issuing a check without sufficient funds, and in a separate case defendant entered the same plea to a charge of assault with a caustic chemical. As provided in Cal. Pen. Code § 1016, a defendant who pleaded not guilty by reason of insanity, without also pleading not guilty, thereby admitted the commission of the underlying offense. Defendant was found to be sane at the time the offenses were committed, and a judgment of conviction was entered. Defendant filed a motion for a new trial, which was denied. Defendant appealed and the court reversed because the record did not show that defendant knowingly waived the right to a jury trial, the right to confront accusers, and the privilege against self-incrimination. The waiver of these three fundamental rights cannot be assumed from a silent record.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the convictions because the record did not show that when defendant entered the guilty plea, defendant understood that he was waiving the right to trial by jury, the privilege against compulsory self-incrimination, and the right to confront his accusers.

Sail'er Inn, Inc. v. Kirby


L.A. No. 29811


Supreme Court of California


5 Cal. 3d 1; 485 P.2d 529; 95 Cal. Rptr. 329; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 230; 3 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 550; 46 A.L.R.3d 351; 3 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P8222



May 27, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  For the reasons stated, we find section 25656 invalid. Let the peremptory writ of mandate issue compelling the Director of the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control to cease license revocation proceedings based upon section 25656 of the Business and Professions Code and to cease enforcement of the section.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, liquor license holders, sought a writ of mandate to prevent respondent, the department of alcoholic beverage control, from revoking their licenses because they hired women bartenders, in violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25656. The court took jurisdiction, not requiring exhaustion of administrative remedies, even though petitioners were not charged but wished to employ female bartenders and feared enforcement.


OVERVIEW:  Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25656 banned women who were not the licensee or the wife of the licensee, from tending bar. Petitioners, liquor license holders, claimed that it violated Cal. Const. art. XX, § 18, 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000e-2, and the equal protection clauses of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 11, 21. The court found that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25656 violated Cal. Const. art. XX, § 18. The desire to protect women from occupational hazards was not a reason to exclude them. The court held that the statute violated the Civil Rights Act of 1964. It was not a bona fide occupational qualification exception because bartending was neither dangerous nor required strength, unpossessed by women, to maintain order. The court held that the statute violated the equal protection clauses of United States and California Constitutions. It limited the fundamental right of women to pursue a lawful vocation. Courts were divided on whether classifications based on sex should be treated as suspect. The court held them suspect because sex was immutable and had a stigma of inferiority. The court applied strict scrutiny and held that the state had no compelling interest.


OUTCOME: A peremptory writ of mandate was issued in favor of petitioners, liquor license holders, compelling respondent, director of the department of alcoholic beverage control, to cease enforcement and license revocation proceedings based upon an unconstitutional ban on women bartenders. The ban violated the Civil Rights Act of 1964, the Equal Protection Clauses of federal and state Constitutions, and the California Constitution.

Dillon v. Mun. Court for Monterey-Carmel Judicial Dist.


S.F. No. 22774


Supreme Court of California


4 Cal. 3d 860; 484 P.2d 945; 94 Cal. Rptr. 777; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 365



May 17, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue as prayed for.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner peace groups sought a writ of prohibition against respondent Municipal Court for the Monterey-Carmel Judicial District of Monterey County (California), which overruled petitioners' demurrer to a complaint charging petitioners with violating City of Seaside, Cal., Municipal Code § 9-108, by obstructing the free use of the streets on a parade route. The demurrer had asserted that § 9-108 violated U.S. Const. amends. I and XIV.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner peace groups were charged with violating City of Seaside, Cal., Municipal Code § 9-108, which prohibited parades from obstructing the streets and sidewalks without obtaining a permit from the city. The court granted petitioners' request for a writ of prohibition restraining respondent court from taking further proceedings in the action. The court held that § 9-108 was unconstitutional on its face, violating U.S. Const. amends. I and XIV. The court found that statutes requiring licenses for parades were constitutional if they regulated only the time, place, and manner of the marches and if they were fairly administered by officials within a narrow range of discretion. The court held that § 9-108 did not meet this standard because it did not specify the body charged with receiving the application and issuing the permit, and it contained no standards to govern officials in deciding to grant or deny permits. The ordinance also contained no guarantee that a permit would issue if the application met the conditions of the ordinance. The court also held that the ordinance could not be severed in order to maintain the charges against petitioners under a constitutional section.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner peace groups' writ of prohibition restraining respondent court from taking further proceedings against petitioners, who were charged with violating an ordinance prohibiting parades from obstructing the streets without a city permit. The court held that the ordinance violated the First Amendment because it did not specify the body to issue the permits and gave officials unlimited discretion to grant or deny permits.

 

143 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


T.N.G. v. Superior Court


S. F. No. 22777


Supreme Court of California


4 Cal. 3d 767; 484 P.2d 981; 94 Cal. Rptr. 813; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 359



May 6, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the real party in interest for a rehearing was denied June 17, 1971, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged and the petition for writ of mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, detained juveniles, sought a writ of mandamus to compel respondent, Superior Court of San Francisco (California), to seal and expunge their detention records. Petitioners asserted that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 781, was unconstitutional under the Equal Protection Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, detained juveniles, had been arrested for passing out anti war leaflets near a high school. They were transported to a police station, where they were released from custody. No further proceedings were brought against them. Petitioners unsuccessfully requested that the respondent, Superior Court of San Francisco, hold Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 781, unconstitutional, and seal and expurgate their detention records because they were required to disclose the fact of their arrest on all future job or higher education applications. Petitioners filed a petition for writ of mandamus against respondent court. Upon review, the court declined to issue a writ. The court refused to find § 781 unconstitutional because its provisions did not violate U.S. Const. amend. XIV. It held that petitioners, since they had been detained and not arrested under juvenile code, could state on all application forms that they had not been "arrested," "detained," or the like. The court noted that the law clearly intended to safeguard juveniles from the adverse use of arrest and detention records, although the juvenile court could retain the records.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ of mandamus and discharged the alternative writ, because under juvenile law petitioner detained juveniles were not "arrested," they did not have to disclose they were detained, the confidentiality provisions of juvenile law protected their records, and the juvenile court was given authority to retain but withhold the release of records.

 

144 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Zeilenga v. Nelson


Sac. No. 7881


Supreme Court of California


4 Cal. 3d 716; 484 P.2d 578; 94 Cal. Rptr. 602; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 354



May 3, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Butte County, J. F. Good, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Inasmuch as the issues raised by the petition for writ of mandate to compel the county clerk to certify petitioner Zeilenga as a candidate have become moot, the election having been held, the judgment is affirmed. Petitioners will recover costs herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed a decision from the Court of Appeal, Third District (California), which affirmed a decision from the trial court upholding the constitutionality of the Butte County Charter provision on the durational residency requirements to run for the position of supervisor.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners filed a petition for writ of mandate to compel respondent county clerk to certify petitioner as a candidate for supervisor. The petition was denied because the residency requirement for a supervisor in the county was five years. Petitioner appealed. The court first determined the issue was not moot because the issue would arise in the future and would also affect petitioner's eligibility in the following election. The court then concluded that the decision in Sheehan v Scott, 145 Cal. 684 (1905) should be overruled. Respondent failed to show any compelling interest why a citizen coming into the state had to wait five years before becoming eligible to the office of county supervisor. Using a strict scrutiny standard to determine the validity of the residency requirement, the court found the five-year residence requirement was excessive and arbitrary and violated the U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court held that to the extent the petition to compel respondent county clerk to certify petitioner as a candidate had become moot, the decision was affirmed, but it direct in future elections that respondent disregard the residence requirement of candidates for the county board of supervisors as set forth in the county code.

 

145 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Associated Home Builders etc., Inc. v. City of Walnut Creek


S.F. No. 22787


Supreme Court of California


4 Cal. 3d 633; 484 P.2d 606; 94 Cal. Rptr. 630; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 347; 43 A.L.R.3d 847; 1 ELR 20223; 2 ERC (BNA) 1490



April 26, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, Richard E. Arnason, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff subdivider sought review of an adverse judgment entered in the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), challenging the constitutionality of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 11546 and Walnut Creek, Cal., Mun. Code § 10-1.516, and the implementation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 11546 by the defendant City of Walnut Creek.


OVERVIEW: Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 11546 authorized local governing boards to require that subdividers must, as a condition to the approval of a subdivision map, dedicate land or pay fees in lieu thereof for park or recreational purposes. Defendant city passed Walnut Creek, Cal., Mun. Code § 10-1.516 to implement this statutory provision. Plaintiff subdivider brought a suit for declaratory and injunctive relief, asserting that the statute violated the Equal Protection Clause and Due Process Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and the parallel clauses of the state constitution because they deprived a subdivider of his property without just compensation. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment for defendant, finding the statute was justified by the general public need for recreational facilities, and was supported by Cal. Const. art. XVIII, relating to the maintenance and preservation of open space. The statute was a valid exercise of the state's police power because it was sufficiently related to the health and welfare of subdivision residents even if there was no showing of a direct relationship between a particular subdivision and an increase in the community's recreational needs.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of defendant city, rejecting plaintiff subdivider's assertions that the statute and ordinance requiring the dedication of land or payment of a fee for recreational purposes prior to a subdivider's map being approved were unconstitutional. This dedication condition was sufficiently related to the subdivision resident's health and welfare.

 

146 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


United Farm Workers Organizing Committee v. Superior Court of Monterey County


S.F. No. 22791


Supreme Court of California


4 Cal. 3d 556; 483 P.2d 1215; 94 Cal. Rptr. 263; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 340; 77 L.R.R.M. 2208



April 15, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue restraining respondent court from enforcing, by contempt proceedings or otherwise, all portions of the challenged injunction save that section which enjoins petitioners from representing that Antle's products are not handled by union personnel or that Antle's field workers are not represented by a bona fide union.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a union organizing committee, sought a writ of prohibition to restrain respondent, the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), from enforcing the provisions of a preliminary injunction, asserting the injunction was invalid because it restrained activities arguably protected under the National Labor Relations Act, 29 U.S.C.S. §§ 157, 158, and because it infringed upon their rights of free speech under U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, a union organizing committee and its members, called a strike, established picket lines at real party in interest agricultural employer's operations, and instituted a boycott against real party in interest's agricultural products. Respondent superior court issued a preliminary injunction prohibiting petitioners from publicizing that real party in interest was engaged in a labor dispute and from boycotting or organizing boycotts of real party in interest's products. Petitioners successfully requested a writ of prohibition. The court preliminarily found that state court jurisdiction was not preempted by the National Labor Relations Act (Act), 29 U.S.C.S. § 151 et seq., because agricultural workers were excluded from the Act. The court restrained respondent from enforcing its injunction, finding the injunction overly broad, and finding that it impinged upon petitioner's rights under U.S. Const. amend. I; the injunction effectively enjoined petitioners from making any appeal whatever to the consuming public to purchase the products of real party in interest's competitors.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of prohibition restraining respondent superior court from enforcing an injunction it issued except the portions preventing petitioners from representing that real party in interest employer's products were not handled by union personnel and that its field workers were not represented by a union. Respondent could not enjoin noncoercive truthful efforts to communicate the facts of a labor dispute to the public.

 

147 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Briscoe v. Reader's Digest Ass'n


L.A. No. 29813


Supreme Court of California


4 Cal. 3d 529; 483 P.2d 34; 93 Cal. Rptr. 866; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 338; 57 A.L.R.3d 1; 1 Media L. Rep. 1845



April 2, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied April 29, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Robert W. Kenny, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to overrule the general demurrer and to rule upon the points presented by the special demurrer. Appellant shall recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) sustaining defendant magazine's demurrer without leave to amend in the suit alleging defendant willfully and maliciously invaded his privacy by publishing an article that truthfully stated he had hijacked a truck 11 years earlier. Defendant claimed a right to publish under U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: Defendant magazine reprinted a story truthfully recounting that plaintiff hijacked a truck, but not relating that the crime occurred 11 years earlier, and since that time, plaintiff had rehabilitated himself and led an exemplary life. The court reversed the granting of defendant's general demurrer without leave to amend, explaining that, although truthful reports of recent crimes and the names of suspects or offenders were deemed protected by the First Amendment, defendant might not have had the right to identify an actor in a report of a long past crime. The court said that where a person had rehabilitated himself, society should have permitted him to continue in the path of rectitude. Additionally, limitation to newsworthy persons and events did not foreclose an interpretation to allow damages where revelations might have been so intimate and so unwarranted as to outrage the community's notions of decency. The court ruled that a jury might have found that defendant crossed that line.


OUTCOME: In the suit plaintiff brought for willful and malicious invasion of privacy, the court reversed the order granting defendant magazine's general demurrer without leave to amend, and directed the trial court to overrule it. The trial court was also directed to rule on defendant's special demurrer.

 

148 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mutch


Crim. No. 14182


Supreme Court of California


4 Cal. 3d 389; 482 P.2d 633; 93 Cal. Rptr. 721; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 321



March 24, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied April 22, 1971. Wright, C. J., did not participate therein. Kaus, J., * participated therein. McComb, J., and Burke, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


* Assigned by the Acting Chairman of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, John W. Preston, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The motion to dismiss the appeal is denied. The cause is retransferred to the Court of Appeal for the Second Appellate District with directions to recall its remittitur in People v. Mutch, Crim. 12915, and to issue a new remittitur vacating the judgment on counts I and II and affirming the judgment on counts III and IV.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought postconviction relief from the decision of the Court of Appeal affirming the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of two counts of kidnaping for the purpose of robbery and two counts of robbery pursuant to a jury verdict and sentenced defendant to life imprisonment with possibility of parole on the kidnaping counts and to the terms prescribed by law on the robbery counts.


OVERVIEW: In the course of a robbery defendant and an accomplice forcibly moved victims from one room in a business premises to another. Defendant was convicted of two counts of kidnaping for the purpose of robbery and two counts of robbery pursuant to a jury verdict. The trial court sentenced defendant to life imprisonment with possibility of parole on the kidnaping counts and to the terms prescribed by law on the robbery counts. The court of appeal affirmed the conviction. Defendant then sought postconviction relief. The court relied on its own precedent and held that movement across a room or from one room to another could not reasonably be found to be asportation within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 209. Additionally such movements were merely incidental to the robberies and did not substantially increase the risk of harm beyond that inherent in the robberies. The court therefore denied state's motion to dismiss the appeal, and ordered the cause retransferred to the court of appeal. The court of appeal was directed to recall its remittitur and to issue a new remittitur vacating the kidnapping counts.


OUTCOME: The court of appeal was directed to recall its remittitur and to issue a new remittitur vacating the kidnaping counts because defendant's actions during the robbery did not constitute kidnaping in light of controlling precedent.

Wood v. Public Utilities Com.


S.F. No. 22715


Supreme Court of California


4 Cal. 3d 288; 481 P.2d 823; 93 Cal. Rptr. 455; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 313



March 8, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' application for a rehearing was denied April 29, 1971. Tobriner, J., and Sullivan, J., did not participate therein. Schauer, J., * and Sims, J., + participated therein. Peters, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Writ of Review Denied September 20, 1990, Reported at 1990 Cal. LEXIS 4369.


* Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.


+ Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought review of a decision of the Public Utilities Commission, (California) ordering dismissal of cases brought against them challenging the validity of certain credit rules.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners challenged certain credit rules created by the Public Utilities Commission (commission). These rules were adopted as part of the utilities' rate tariffs for the purpose of reducing bad debt losses and provided that unless credit was established in one or another of several specified ways, an applicant was required to make a deposit to secure utility service. The court of appeals upheld the credit rules, finding that they did not violate the Equal Protection Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV. Permitting some persons to escape the deposit requirement and compelling others to pay or meet credit conditions was reasonable in light of the purpose behind the rules, which was to reduce bad debt losses. The commission could have reasonably concluded that persons who could satisfy none of the alternative methods of establishing credit other than by making a deposit presented greater risks of bad debt losses than those who could satisfy one or more of those alternatives.


OUTCOME: The lower court's order was affirmed because credit rules established by the Public Utilities Commission did not violate the Equal Protection Clause because the rules were rationally related to the classifications drawn.

 

150 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Calderon v. Los Angeles


L.A. No. 29752


Supreme Court of California


4 Cal. 3d 251; 481 P.2d 489; 93 Cal. Rptr. 361; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 311



March 2, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Robert W. Kenny, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, residents and voters, challenged the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which granted judgment on the pleadings for defendant city in plaintiffs' action against defendant for a writ of mandate commanding defendant to cease using voter registration as the basis for its councilmanic districting and to compel use of population as the standard therefor.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, citizens and voters, challenged the ordinance enacted by defendant City wherein apportionment of voting districts for city council was done by a one voter, one vote method, as opposed to the one person, one vote method. Plaintiffs argued that the ordinance violated the equal protection clause. The trial court held that, because the apportionment resulted in each district containing substantially equal numbers of registered voters, it was constitutionally sound. On appeal the court, after reviewing the caselaw behind redistricting for both local and federal elections, held that, unless the apportionment plan produced districts containing roughly equal numbers of people as opposed to registered voters, the plan failed. The court then reversed the judgment on the pleadings in favor of defendant City because the registered voter apportionment standard appeared constitutionally faulty in view of the substantial variance between population and registered voters on a per-district basis; and because the 10 percent provision authorized too great a deviation from equality.


OUTCOME: The judgment on the pleadings in favor of defendant City was reversed because the registered voter apportionment standard appears constitutionally faulty in view of the substantial variance between population and registered voters on a per-district basis; and because the 10 percent provision authorizes too great a deviation from equality.

 

151 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Bixby v. Pierno


L.A. No. 29689


Supreme Court of California


4 Cal. 3d 130; 481 P.2d 242; 93 Cal. Rptr. 234; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 303



February 23, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Ralph H. Nutter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff and intervenor appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which refused to exercise its independent judgment and denied their petitions for a writ of mandamus to compel defendant agency to set aside its decision approving a recapitalization plan.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff and intervenor sought judicial review of an agency decision determining that a proposed recapitalization plan was supported by substantial evidence. The trial court denied their petition for a writ of mandate and refused to review the agency's decision. Plaintiff and intervenor then appealed. On review the court noted that the agency's decision was based on its expertise in the matter and that the determination that the recapitalization plan was fair, just, and equitable and should not be disturbed absent an abuse of discretion or a finding that the record was not supported by substantial evidence. The court noted that, because the decision was supported by substantial evidence, no independent review was authorized by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5. The court further noted that the legislature gave the agency wide discretion to make such decisions and as long as no fundamental right that has vested was abridged, judicial review was also precluded. The court therefore affirmed the judgment of the trial court in its refusal to review the agency's decision.


OUTCOME: The denial of the writs and refusal to review the agency decision was affirmed because the agency's findings were supported by substantial evidence in the light of the entire record, and because the conclusions were reasonable in view of the broad statutory discretion conferred upon the agency, its decision had to be upheld.

 

152 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Los Angeles v. Shell Oil Co.


L.A. No. 29670


Supreme Court of California


4 Cal. 3d 108; 480 P.2d 953; 93 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 302



February 22, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied March 26, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, John Stuart Frazer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to set aside the findings of fact and conclusions of law; to determine a proper apportionment of defendant's gross receipts in conformity with the views herein expressed and, to that end, to receive such additional evidence as it may deem necessary or advisable; thereafter, having reexamined and redetermined all issues, to make and file findings of fact and conclusions of law in conformity with the views herein expressed; and to enter judgment accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant oil company appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which awarded plaintiff city a monetary award as a deficiency of business license taxes levied against defendant for the years 1958-1961.


OVERVIEW: Defendant oil company shipped gas from its refinery out of city to its bulk terminal in the city. However, it then sold gas to out of city buyers. Plaintiff city was awarded a significant sum as a deficiency of business license taxes levied against defendant for the years 1958-1961. Defendant appealed the judgement arguing that that tax exceeded plaintiff's constitutional powers and was a violation of defendant's constitutional rights as a taxpayer insofar as it was based upon the total gross receipts derived by defendant from wholesale sales of gas delivered from within the city to retailers located outside the city. The court agreed noting that only gross receipts directly attributable to selling activities carried on in the city were taxable. The court held that an apportionment should have been made to determine the exact amounts directly attributable to sales carried in and out of the city limits. The court therefore reversed and remanded the judgment for a new apportionment.


OUTCOME: The judgment assigning a tax deficiency to defendant oil company was reversed and remanded for a new apportionment because defendant proved with clear and cogent evidence that the tax imposed was unconstitutional as applied because it taxed gross receipts other than those which were directly attributable to selling activities carried on in the city.

People v. Luros


Crim. No. 13153


Supreme Court of California


4 Cal. 3d 84; 480 P.2d 633; 92 Cal. Rptr. 833; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 301



February 18, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Bernard S. Selber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order setting aside the indictment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged an order of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California) setting aside respondents' indictment for conspiring to violate Cal. Penal Code § 311.2, on the basis that the prosecution failed to introduce any competent evidence of contemporary community standards by which the grand jury could have been guided in determining whether there was probable cause to believe that respondents' books were obscene.


OVERVIEW: Respondents were indicted for conspiring to violate Cal. Penal Code § 311.2 by willfully preparing, publishing, printing, and distributing four obscene books. The trial court set aside the indictment, finding that the prosecution had failed to introduce any competent evidence of contemporary community standards by which the grand jury could have been guided in determining whether there was probable cause to believe that respondents' books were obscene. The court of appeals reversed, holding that it was not necessary to receive evidence as to contemporary community standards in order to determine the sole issue of probable cause. Thus, failure to introduce evidence of contemporary community standards before the grand jury did not render the indictment defective.


OUTCOME: The order setting aside the indictment was reversed because the failure to introduce evidence of contemporary community standards before the grand jury did not render the indictment defective.

 

154 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Cohen


L.A. No. 29814


Supreme Court of California


4 Cal. 3d 41; 480 P.2d 300; 92 Cal. Rptr. 684; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 298



February 11, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' petition for a rehearing was denied March 9, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, James D. Tante, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to redetermine the amount of inheritance tax due in accordance with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that sustained respondent husband's objection to the imposition of inheritance tax on the assets of a community property trust.


OVERVIEW: Respondent husband and his wife entered a declaration of trust. The bulk of their property was transferred to a community property trust. Respondent's wife died. Pursuant to the declaration of trust, respondent was to receive all of the income from the trust for his lifetime and he had the power to revoke or amend the trust without limitation. The state sought to collect inheritance taxes. The trial court held that respondent was exempt from inheritance taxes. The state appealed. The court found that when a decedent spouse conveyed to a surviving spouse a power of appointment over any portion of the decedent spouse's half interest in the community property, the surviving spouse was liable for inheritance taxes levied on the value of the property subject to the power of appointment in excess of the value of a life estate in the decedent spouse's half of the community property, Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 13694. The court reversed because respondent's power of appointment could be exercise in favor of anyone, Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 13692. Respondent was required to pay inheritance taxes by virtue of his power to revoke or amend his deceased spouse's half of the trust property.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the lower court that sustained respondent husband's objection to the imposition of inheritance tax was reversed because when respondent's spouse conveyed to respondent a power of appointment over her half interest in the community property, respondent was liable for inheritance taxes levied on the value of the property subject to the power of appointment in excess of the value of a life estate in the decedent spouse's half.

 

155 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ribero


Crim. No. 13746


Supreme Court of California


4 Cal. 3d 55; 480 P.2d 308; 92 Cal. Rptr. 692; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 299



February 11, 1971 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment from the Court of Appeals, (California), where the court had denied his petition for an order permitting a late notice of appeal under Cal. R. Ct. 31(a).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner had entered a not guilty plea to a charge of possession of a check or money order with intent to defraud in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 475a. He alleged that he thereafter changed his plea to guilty in reliance on a statement by his attorney that upon so doing he would be committed to the state rehabilitation center as a narcotic addict. At sentencing, the court informed him that he would be committed to prison. Petitioner then attempted to withdraw his guilty plea. The court refused, and sentenced him to prison. He asked his attorney to appeal, which the attorney failed to do. When he attempted to appeal on his own behalf, his notice of appeal was not filed as untimely. He sought an order from the court of appeals permitting a late notice of appeal under Cal. R. Ct. 31(a), which was denied. The court reversed. Advice or assistance of counsel in filing the notice of appeal was meaningless where counsel did not also advise or assist in preparation and filing of the required statement. Counsel's obligation to assist in filing the notice of appeal necessarily encompassed assistance with the statement required by Cal. Penal Code § 1237.5.


OUTCOME: The court reversed. Advice or assistance of counsel in filing the notice of appeal was meaningless where counsel did not also advise or assist in preparation and filing of the required statement.

County of San Mateo v. Boss


S.F. No. 22769


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 962; 479 P.2d 654; 92 Cal. Rptr. 294; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 385



January 27, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, Wayne R. Millington, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant son appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California) awarding plaintiff county $ 820 and ordering defendant to make future monthly contributions of $ 20 or such other sum as would be determined by the County pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 12101.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff county brought an action pursuant to the Old Age Security Law, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 12100, 12101, to collect from defendant son accrued but unpaid monthly contributions toward the support of his mother and to obtain an order requiring defendant to make such monthly contributions in the future. The trial court rendered judgment in favor of plaintiff concluding that defendant was legally obligated to support his mother under §§ 12100, 12101. On appeal, the court reversed holding that defendant owed his mother neither a statutory nor a common law duty of support. The court held that defendant's mother was not a "poor person" as defined by Cal. Civ. Code § 206, thus defendant owed her no duty of support under § 206, and that at common law there was no liability on a child to support parents, or on parents to support an adult child. Accordingly, there existed no rational basis to sustain the inclusion of defendant within a class, which bore a disproportionately large burden of defraying the costs of the old age security aid program. Thus the imposition of liability pursuant to §§ 12100, 12101 constituted a denial of equal protection of the laws.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment awarding plaintiff county $ 820 and ordering defendant son to make future monthly contributions of $ 20 pursuant to statute because defendant did not owe his mother a statutory or common law duty of support, thus the imposition of liability on defendant under the Old Age Security Law constituted a denial of equal protection of the laws.

San Francisco Unified Sch. Dist. v. Johnson


S.F. No. 22771


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 937; 479 P.2d 669; 92 Cal. Rptr. 309; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 384



January 26, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner school district filed an action in mandamus to compel respondent school complex planning officer to execute a requisition for a computer study of school assignments as a method of desegregating city schools based on a serious racial imbalance pursuant to Cal. Educ. Code § 1009.5.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner school district commissioned a study to recommend methods of desegregating city schools because schools displayed serious racial imbalance. Based on the report, petitioners resolved to establish two elementary school complexes. A student living within the attendance zone for a complex was assigned to one of the schools in the complex. Under Cal. Educ. Code § 1009.5, the proposed complexes would not compel any student to ride on a school bus, but it did involve assignment of students to schools under circumstances such that a school bus was the most practical means of transportation. The plan did not provide for solicitation of parental consent to pupil assignments. Respondent complex planning officer refused to execute a study of school assignments alleging that the complex did not contemplate parental consent to pupil assignments, thus the legality was doubtful. Petitioners filed an action in mandamus to compel respondent to execute the requisition for the study. The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate because statute did not violate constitution and statutory construction showed that the plan was an efficient method of achieving school integration.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate to compel respondent complex planning officer to execute a requisition for a study of school assignments as a method of desegregating schools based on a serious racial imbalance, because petitioner school district's plan for school complexes did not compel a student to ride a bus but only involved assignment of students to schools such that a bus was the most practical means of transportation.

 

158 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Parr v. Municipal Court for Monterey-Carmel Judicial Dist.


S.F. No. 22748


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 861; 479 P.2d 353; 92 Cal. Rptr. 153; 1971 Cal. LEXIS 376



January 18, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, Anthony Brazil, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed with directions to the superior court to issue the peremptory writ of prohibition as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant merchant challenged an order from the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which denied her petition for a preemptory writ. Appellant was arrested under respondent city's "hippie" ordinance while sitting on the grass in a city park and charged with unlawful sitting on a public lawn. Appellant's arrest took place during her participation in a public assembly called to discuss the new "hippie" ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city passed an ordinance regulating use of public parks, disallowing, among other things, tree climbing, walking, standing, or sitting upon monuments or sitting on the lawns, or littering and loitering. The court held that the ordinance was invalid because it had an underlying discriminatory purpose. The court found a predictable discriminatory impact when the ordinance used official code language in its emergency declaration to single out the social group "hippies." When the ordinance language stigmatized "hippies" as "undesirable" and "unsanitary," the city council violated the constitutional guaranty of the equal protection of the laws. The description of "undesirable" and "unsanitary" persons referred to as "transients" could not be squared with the claimed neutral purpose of preventing all persons from sitting or lying on the grass in order to protect it from undue wear. The irrefutable implication was that the city sought to rid the city of the blight it perceived to be created by the presence of the "hippies." The court concluded that the ordinance violated the equal protection clause, and reversed the order denying appellant's petition for a writ of prohibition.


OUTCOME: Respondent city's "hippie" ordinance was found to violate the United States Constitution because, while it claimed neutrality in its purpose of keeping all people from sitting on the grass, it branded "hippies" as an undesirable group of transients whose presence threatened the well-being of the city, thus showing an underlying discriminatory purpose. The court reversed the order denying appellant merchant's petition for a writ of prohibition.

 

159 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court of Yolo County


Sac. No. 7859


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 807; 478 P.2d 449; 91 Cal. Rptr. 729; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 249; 45 A.L.R.3d 559



December 31, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  The alternative writ of mandamus is discharged and the peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People sought a writ of mandate to reverse an order of respondent, the Superior Court of Yolo County (California), granting a motion to suppress filed by defendants, the real parties in interest, in a prosecution for unlawful possession and transportation of marijuana under Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 11530 and 11531.


OVERVIEW: As defendants, the real parties in interest, were pulling over after being detained for speeding, the officer saw one bend over and reach under the seat. The driver exited the car and exited toward the officer. The officer immediately opened the passenger car door and looked inside, saw some stems and seeds, and conducted a search which revealed marijuana. At trial, the officer testified he wanted to see what had been hidden. The trial court granted defendants' motion to suppress and the People sought a writ of mandate. The court denied the writ, finding that the search was unreasonable. The circumstances justifying the arrest for speeding, including the furtive gesture, did not furnish probable cause to search the car. Defendants were cooperative and admitted speeding. A search prompted by a general curiosity to ascertain what, if anything, was within defendants' vehicle was manifestly exploratory in nature, and violated U.S. Const. amend. IV. The officer did not testify to any facts that would have given him cause to believe defendants were hiding contraband, and probable cause was tested by facts known to the officer at the time the search was made.


OUTCOME: The court denied the peremptory writ sought by the people. The court found that the suppression order was supported by substantial evidence. The court held that the officer unlawfully entered the vehicle in violation of the Fourth Amendment because the mere motion of placing an item under the seat did not provide probable cause to believe defendants were hiding contraband.

 

160 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


H. v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 29761


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 709; 478 P.2d 32; 91 Cal. Rptr. 600; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 241



December 28, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  Let the writ of mandate issue directing respondent court to reconsider petitioner's amenability to treatment as a juvenile in accordance with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner juvenile offender sought a writ of mandate directing respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County to vacate its order that certified petitioner for trial as a person not amenable to treatment as a juvenile stemming from murder and assault with intent to commit murder charges. Petitioner claimed that respondent erred by citing that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 1800 et seq. was likely unconstitutional as the reason for its finding.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner juvenile offender was certified for trial as a person not amenable to treatment as a juvenile after he was charged with murder and assault with intent to commit murder. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to direct respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles to vacate the order, contending that respondent inappropriately found that he was not amenable to treatment as a juvenile by stating as its reason that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 1800 was likely unconstitutional in that it gave the Youth Authority power to detain petitioner beyond his 21st birthday. The court agreed with petitioner's contention. The court found that respondent's belief that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 1800 was possibly unconstitutional was not an appropriate criterion under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707 for determining whether petitioner was amenable to treatment as a juvenile. As a result, respondent did not completely review petitioner's case and did not fully consider all the facts involved. Accordingly, the court issued the writ of mandate and directed respondent to reconsider petitioner's amenability to treatment as a juvenile.


OUTCOME: The court issued the writ of mandate requested by petitioner juvenile offender and directed respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County to reconsider petitioner's amenability to treatment as a juvenile. The court found that respondent applied an inappropriate criterion, namely, the possible unconstitutionality of a statute, in determining whether petitioner was amenable to treatment as a juvenile.

 

161 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Candelario


Crim. No. 14327


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 702; 477 P.2d 729; 91 Cal. Rptr. 497; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 240



December 21, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  The amendment to the abstract of judgment being in excess of the court's jurisdiction is ineffective for any purpose. The Adult Authority is therefore directed to fix petitioner's sentence and determine his eligibility for parole on the basis of the original abstract of judgment of May 27, 1964. Since petitioner is not now entitled to his release in the absence of action by the Adult Authority, the order to show cause is discharged and the writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner convicted narcotics dealer presented a writ of habeas corpus after the trial court amended abstract of judgment adding a prior conviction to the finding of guilt for selling narcotics, thereby enhancing petitioner's sentence. Petitioner claimed that the trial court was without jurisdiction to amend the abstract of judgment, and that in so doing, he was subjected to double jeopardy.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner convicted narcotics dealer was found guilty of selling heroin in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11501 and admitted to a prior felony conviction for possession of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530. After judgment was entered, the trial court amended the abstract of judgment to reflect the prior conviction and thereby enhance petitioner's sentence. In his petition for writ of habeas corpus, petitioner argued that the trial court was without jurisdiction to amend the judgment and that in so doing double jeopardy was violated. The court did not find that double jeopardy was violated, but otherwise agreed with this contention. The amendment was invalid because the amendment materially altered petitioner's rights and hence could not have been made under the trial court's authority to correct a clerical error. The court reasoned the record's silence as to the prior conviction operated as a finding that the prior conviction was not true. Accordingly, the court denied the writ, but directed that petitioner's sentence and his eligibility for parole was to be determined by the original abstract of judgment.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner convicted narcotics dealer's writ of habeas corpus and directed that petitioner's sentence and parole was to be based on the original abstract of judgment. The court found that the amendment of the judgment was invalid because the amendment materially altered petitioner's rights, and that the original abstract's silence as to the prior felony operated as a finding that the prior conviction was not true.

 

162 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Mannheim v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L.A. No. 29776


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 678; 478 P.2d 17; 91 Cal. Rptr. 585; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 238



December 21, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the real party in interest for a rehearing was denied January 20, 1971.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue requiring respondeat superior court, at the time it determines to distribute the residue of decedent's estate, to order distribution to the heirs of decedent's predeceased spouse pursuant to Probate Code section 228 as amended, unless known heirs of decedent are entitled thereto, in accordance with the views herein expressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney for heirs of decedent's predeceased husband sought a petition for hearing after the Court of Appeals (California) denied a peremptory writ of mandate. Petitioner claimed that respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County erroneously denied petitioner's motion to enter a supplemental order of distribution pursuant to Cal. Prob. Code § 228.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney for heirs of decedent's predeceased husband presented a motion to enter a supplemental order distributing to his clients the unallocated portion of decedent's estate pursuant to an amendment to Cal. Prob. Code § 228. Petitioner's request for a peremptory writ of mandate seeking to compel respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County to grant the motion was denied. On petition for hearing, petitioner contended that respondent should have granted the motion. The reviewing court agreed with this contention. The court found that the amendment to Cal. Prob. Code § 228 applied to decedent's estate because the legislature intended its applicability to all estates not vested in or escheated to the state of California, regardless of when decedent died. The court reasoned that the state of California lacked any present interest in the property because the probate court had not yet made an order of distribution to the state under Cal. Prob. Code § 1027. Therefore, the retroactive application of Cal. Prob. Code § 228 did not constitute an unconstitutional gift of public funds. Accordingly, the court issued the peremptory writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate requiring respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County to order distribution to the heirs of decedent's predeceased spouse pursuant to the request of petitioner attorney for heirs of decedent's predeceased spouse. The court found that an amendment to the applicable probate statute applied retroactively and prevented decedent's estate from escheating to real party in interest State of California.

 

163 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. West


Crim. No. 14453


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 595; 477 P.2d 409; 91 Cal. Rptr. 385; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 233



December 3, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On December 30, 1970, the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5(m), challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), denying his motion to suppress evidence on the basis of which he pleaded nolo contendere to a charge of opening or maintaining a place for the selling, giving away, or using narcotic in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1157 instead of possession of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, charged with possession of marijuana under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530, sought to suppress the incriminating evidence under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5. After the trial court denied his motion, with the consent of the district attorney, he pleaded nolo contendere to opening or maintaining a place for the selling, giving away, or using of a narcotic under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11557, and was convicted. The court affirmed the conviction. The court held that a plea of guilty or nolo contendere did not become involuntary because defendant entered into it pursuant to a plea bargain with the district attorney that another and more serious charge would not be pressed. The court further held that a trial court could accept a plea of nolo contendere to a lesser offense reasonably related to the offense charged. Thus, the trial court properly exercised its jurisdiction in accepting defendant's plea.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

164 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Regents of Univ. of Cal. v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 29731


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 529; 476 P.2d 457; 91 Cal. Rptr. 57; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 227



November 12, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  The alternative writ of mandate heretofore issued is discharged and the petition for a peremptory writ of mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Regents of the University of California sought a writ of mandate pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 400 to command respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to transfer an action to the Superior Court of Alameda County and to vacate a summary judgment entered after the denial of petitioner's motion to transfer venue.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff individuals filed a taxpayers action against petitioner Regents of the University of California for constitutional violations in certain resolutions and expending public tax monies in furtherance of those resolutions. Petitioner filed for a change of venue, alleging that it legally resided in Alameda County. Respondent denied petitioner's request and granted a summary judgment filed by plaintiffs. Petitioner then requested a writ of mandate from the appellate court, but the appellate court determined a peremptory writ of mandate should issue instructing the trial court to vacate it order granting the summary judgment and to transfer the action to Alameda County. The court discharged the writ of mandate and denied the peremptory writ of mandate. The court held that an individual citizen or a number of individual taxpayers should bear no greater burden in challenging the acts of respondent than in challenging the acts of any other government agency. The court found that because public funds were expended in Los Angeles County, it is in that county where the taxpayers suffered the alleged injury.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the writ of mandate and denied the peremptory writ of mandate because an individual citizen or a number of individual taxpayers should have borne no greater burden in challenging respondent than in challenging any other government agency. The court found that since public monies were spent in the county where the suit was filed, that was where the taxpayers suffered their injury.

 

165 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Johnson


Crim. No. 13672


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 404; 475 P.2d 841; 90 Cal. Rptr. 569; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 220



October 29, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  The writ is granted, and petitioner is remanded to the Superior Court of the County of San Diego for further proceedings in accordance with the views expressed herein. 5



5 As we indicated in Tenorio, petitions for a discretionary dismissal of a prior narcotics conviction are to be filed with the superor court with territorial jurisdiction for transfer to the sentencing court. Petitioner in the instant case sought relief here before we decided Tenorio.

Future petitions challenging a prior federal narcotics conviction under section 4744 of title 26 of the United States Code (or its predecessor) should be filed with the superior court with territorial jurisdiction. Whenever such a petition would, if meritorious, render an inmate eligible for probation, and whenever such a petition also seeks a discretionary dismissal under Tenorio, the case should be transferred to the sentencing court. In other cases, the court with territorial jurisdiction should dispose of the case by striking any prior which is invalid under Leary.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner urged the court to issue a writ of habeas corpus to the Superior Court of the County of San Diego (California) and claimed a 1950 federal conviction must be voided because the underlying statute had been held unconstitutional. Petitioner alleged that his sentence was unconstitutional, that he was prejudiced by the introduction of the priors at trial, and that he was denied the effective assistance of counsel.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted under an indictment supplemented to charge a prior that was predicated on a federal statute later held unconstitutional. The court held that petitioner could challenge his sentence to the extent that it was augmented by an unconstitutional prior, and issued the writ of habeas corpus. The court held that the decision which invalidated the statute was to be applied retroactively, and that it was unnecessary to decide whether such application was essential if the rule was invoked to challenge a prior used to augment sentence. The court reasoned that an inmate convicted of violating a statute subsequently declared unconstitutional was entitled to relief regardless of whether it was entered upon a guilty plea, because an unconstitutional law was void, an offense created by it was not a crime, and a conviction under it was not merely erroneous, but was illegal and void. The court held that because petitioner was convicted at a bench trial and the priors were not used for impeachment, the trial judge was presumed to have properly disregarded the priors in determining petitioner's guilt.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner application for a writ of habeas corpus and remanded the case to the trial court to relieve petitioner of the effect of the void prior and for the trial court to exercise its discretion as to whether to strike an unrelated sentence augmenting prior.

In re Wallace


Crim. No. 14529


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 289; 475 P.2d 208; 90 Cal. Rptr. 176; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 207



October 16, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  Let a writ of habeas corpus issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner demonstrators sought a writ of habeas corpus after they were convicted of trespass in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 602(j).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner demonstrators sought a writ of habeas corpus after the trial court convicted them of trespass in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 602(j). The court granted the petition. As a preliminary matter, the court concluded that special circumstances were present which relieved petitioners from the necessity of fully exhausting their appellate remedy before seeking habeas corpus. The court found that the record was wholly devoid of evidence to support petitioners' conviction of violating § 602(j). It found that the record was devoid of evidence that petitioners obstructed the business of the fair. The court observed that the uncontradicted testimony of one of the petitioners established that petitioners had no intent to obstruct the business of the fair when they entered the premises. The court found that petitioners were not arrested because of any obstruction they may have caused but because they insisted on their right to distribute leaflets and to display signs.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner demonstrators a writ of habeas corpus. The court found that special circumstances relieved petitioners of the necessity of exhausting their appellate remedy and that the statute which the trial court convicted petitioners of violating did not prohibit their conduct.

In re King


Crim. No. 14130


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 226; 474 P.2d 983; 90 Cal. Rptr. 15; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 202



October 2, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied October 28, 1970.



DISPOSITION: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied October 28, 1970.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, convicted of failure to support his children in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 270, and whose conviction was adjudged a felony based on petitioner's absence from the state for at least 30 days, sought habeas corpus relief.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of failure to support his children in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 270, and the conviction was adjudged a felony based on petitioner's absence form the state for at least 30 days. Petitioner sought habeas corpus relief and the court held that the provision of § 270 that punished nonsupporting fathers who stayed out of the state more heavily that those that stayed in the state violated the equal protection clauses of the U. S. Const. amend. XIV, and of the Cal. Const., art. I, §§ 11, 21. The court directed the trial court to correct its record to reflect that the offense for which petitioner was convicted was a misdemeanor. The court held that the felony classification also violated the privileges and immunities clause of the U. S. Const. art. IV, § 2, because its effect was to arbitrarily discriminate against non-California citizens. The court found that there was no compelling governmental interest justifying the felony classification for fathers who stayed out of the state.


OUTCOME: The court directed the trial court to correct its record to reflect that the offense for which petitioner was convicted was a misdemeanor because the Cal. Penal Code § 270 punishment of nonsupporting fathers who stayed out of the state weighed more heavily on those that stayed in the state and violated the equal protection clauses of the United States and California constitutions and was not justified by a compelling governmental interest.

 

168 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Cox


Crim. No. 13648


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 205; 474 P.2d 992; 90 Cal. Rptr. 24; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 201



October 1, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied October 28, 1970, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: The writ of habeas corpus is denied and the order to show cause is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, who was charged with violating San Rafael, Cal., Ordinance § 8.12.210, which prohibited remaining on business premises after being notified by the person in charge thereof to remove therefrom, sought a writ of habeas corpus to be released from constructive custody.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, who went to a shopping center intending to make a purchase, struck up a conversation with a friend who was already present at the center. A security guard ordered them to leave and they refused. Petitioner was arrested and charged with violating San Rafael, Cal., Ordinance § 8.12.210, which prohibited remaining on business premises after being notified by the person in charge thereof to remove therefrom. The court denied petitioner's writ for habeas corpus and held that § 8.12.210 did not endow the shopping center with an absolute power arbitrarily to exclude a would-be customer from its premises because the ordinance had to be read in conjunction with the Unruh Civil Rights Act, which forbade a business establishment generally open to the public from arbitrarily excluding a prospective customer. The court found that the shopping center could exclude petitioner if it did so on a rational basis but that it was not possible to determine from the record whether the exclusion was reasonable. The court found that § 8.12.210 was neither preempted by state law, nor void for vagueness.


OUTCOME: The court denied habeas corpus to petitioner, who was charged with violating a municipal ordinance, which prohibited remaining on business premises after being asked to leave, and discharged the order to show cause. The court held that the ordinance did not arbitrarily confer absolute power on the business owners because the ordinance had to be read in conjunction with the Unruh Civil Rights Act, which required that the exclusion be reasonable.

 

169 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Lockridge v. Superior Court of Los Angeles County


L.A. No. 29729


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 166; 474 P.2d 683; 89 Cal. Rptr. 731; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 198



September 29, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' application for a rehearing was denied October 28, 1970. Peters, J., Tobriner, J., and Sullivan, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ heretofore issued is discharged. The petition for a peremptory writ of mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed a petition for writ of mandate to compel reversal of the decision of respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their motion to suppress testimony as fruit of an illegal search pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5 in their criminal trial for robbery.


OVERVIEW: A gun was recovered pursuant to a search warrant. The warrant was subsequently found to be insufficient. However, the gun led the police to an arrest report signed by the victims of a robbery. The police interviewed the victims, who identified defendants by photographs as the perpetrators of the robbery. Defendants contended that but for the illegal search and seizure of the gun, the police would not have connected them with the robbery and therefore the testimony of the victims was the product of the illegal search and seizure, and should have been suppressed. The court denied defendants a writ of mandate and held that the purpose of deterring unlawful police conduct would not have been further advanced by suppression of the victims' testimony because such would have been admissible to establish defendants' guilt of the crime, notwithstanding the chance disclosure of their connection during a wholly unrelated police investigation. The court found that the illegal search had not been directed towards discovery of the victims, and that the purpose of deterring unlawful police conduct had been adequately served by suppression of the gun and evidence of other crimes.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants a writ of mandate to compel respondent trial court to suppress victims' testimony as fruit of an illegal search, although the gun which led to defendants' charges for robbery was obtained by an insufficient warrant, because the connection to the victims was not sufficient to characterize the victims' testimony as tainted, and that the illegal search had not been directed to the discovery of the victims.

 

170 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Canales v. Alviso


S.F. No. 22724


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 118; 474 P.2d 417; 89 Cal. Rptr. 601; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 194



September 17, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, Stanley R. Evans and John E. Longinotti, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant electors of the city of Alviso challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) which found that the respondent city and elections board had not engaged in any misconduct which would void the election approving consolidation of the City of Alviso and the City of San Jose.


OVERVIEW: Appellant electors of the city of Alviso filed a claim contesting the results of an election to determine whether Alviso would consolidate with respondent city. At trial, appellant submitted evidence that individuals who were not residents of Alviso were permitted to vote, that residents of Alviso were promised consideration for their votes, and that some voters were not required to take the statutory residence oath prior to voting. Appellants demonstrated that nine of the illegal votes came from individuals who had signed a petition for consolidation. Though the trial court found that 11 of the votes were illegal, and total vote difference was 9 votes, the trial court only found that it could determine how two of these votes were cast. The trial court split the remaining 9 votes 50-50 and upheld the election. In reversing the judgment, the court found that since nine of the illegal votes came from voters who signed a pro-consolidation petition, and respondent made no effort to show that these votes were cast inconsistently with the petition, the necessary conclusion was that all nine votes were cast for consolidation, and thus the trial court's split of these votes were error.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court, because it erred in splitting the illegal votes found in the election 50-50. Since nine of the 11 illegal votes were from individuals who had signed a petition favoring consolidation, and respondent city did not make any effort to show that these votes were cast inconsistently with the wording of the petition, the record supported the conclusion that the nine votes were cast for consolidation.

 

171 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Antazo


Crim. No. 13857


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 100; 473 P.2d 999; 89 Cal. Rptr. 255; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 193



September 3, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  The writ is granted. Petitioner is discharged from the custody of the sheriff of Santa Clara County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner filed a writ of habeas corpus asking the court to require respondent sheriff to discharge him from custody on the grounds that his sentence to probation and payment of a fine or imprisonment, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 1205 and 13521, violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV as applied to him because it discriminated on the basis of wealth and was not necessary to a compelling state interest.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner prisoner filed a writ of habeas corpus, claiming that Cal. Penal Code §§ 1205 and 13521 violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV as applied to him. After a criminal conviction, petitioner, who was indigent, was sentenced to probation and payment of a fine, or imprisonment if he did not pay the fine. Because of the constitutional question, the court held petitioner presented special circumstances such that he did not need to exhaust appeals. The court held the sentence required imprisonment because petitioner could not pay the fine whereas a wealthier criminal could escape confinement, and, therefore, discriminated on the basis of wealth, a suspect classification. The court applied strict scrutiny to §§ 1205 and 13521 and held imprisonment was not constitutionally necessary in this case to promote the assumed state interest in rehabilitation because other alternatives for collection of fines were available, which did not discriminate on the basis of poverty, and that such options must be made available to an indigent offender. The court ordered petitioner's sentence be modified to credit him against his fine for time served and give him an alternative means to pay the balance.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner prisoner's writ of habeas corpus and ordered petitioner discharged from the custody of respondent sheriff. The court held petitioner's sentence to probation and payment of a fine or imprisonment violated the Equal Protection Clause because it discriminated against him, because of his inability to pay, on the basis of wealth and was not necessary to serve a compelling state interest.

Magezis v. Municipal Court for San Francisco


S.F. No. 22713


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 54; 473 P.2d 353; 88 Cal. Rptr. 713; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 188



August 26, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  The alternative writ of mandate is discharged, and a peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought a writ of mandate requiring respondent court to order a reporter's transcript of evidence and proceedings prepared at county expense for their use in appealing their convictions for loitering near a school where children normally congregate in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 653g.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners were convicted of loitering near a school where children normally congregate, a misdemeanor under Cal. Penal Code § 653g, and they sought a writ of mandate compelling respondent court to pay the cost of a reporter's transcript of the proceedings to be used in their appeal. Petitioners' attorney indicated that he did not have adequate notes to show what points were raised and arguments made and that he needed the prosecution testimony transcribed. The motion was denied by respondent, and petitioners sought a writ of mandate to compel the preparation of the transcript at county expense. The trial court denied the writ and petitioners appealed. The court affirmed because respondent had made no attempt to obtain a settled statement of facts for purposes of appeal, which might have sufficed under the circumstances. The court held that petitioners must show the reasons why they could not inform the reviewing court by a settled statement of the claimed inadequacies and errors.


OUTCOME: The alternative writ of mandate was discharged and a peremptory writ was denied because the petitioners failed to show why they could not utilize a settled statement instead of a transcript of the proceedings.
