In re William M.


Crim. No. 14469


Supreme Court of California


3 Cal. 3d 16; 473 P.2d 737; 89 Cal. Rptr. 33; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 186



August 24, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  The order to show cause, having served its purpose, is discharged, and the writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner father brought a writ of habeas corpus, challenging the ruling of the juvenile court (California) refusing to release his minor son from detention prior to his jurisdictional hearing. The court issued a show cause order and directed that petitioner's son be released from custody pending his jurisdictional hearing.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner's minor son was arrested for selling marijuana to a police officer in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11531. At his detention hearing, the juvenile court indicated that the minor would be detained until his jurisdictional hearing because it was that court's policy to detain juveniles who sold marijuana. The juvenile court did not hear any testimony by the probation officer, consider his report under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 628, or examine the minor, his parents, or his character witnesses under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 635. Petitioner filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus, and the case was transferred to the court. The court issued a show cause order and directed that the minor be released from custody until his jurisdictional hearing. The court rejected respondent's contention that the case was moot; although the minor's proceedings were over, his case represented a line of cases that was capable of repetition, yet evading review. The juvenile court erred in formulating a policy detaining juveniles charged with certain crimes. A consideration of the relevant factors under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 635 was required prior to ordering detention.


OUTCOME: The court discharged its order to show cause and denied the requested writ of habeas corpus because the juvenile had already been sentenced. The court denied respondent's assertion that the case was moot, finding that it was reviewable because the situation was capable of repetition, yet evading review. The juvenile court erred in establishing a rule that all juveniles accused of a specified type of offense should automatically be detained.

 

174 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Johnson v. Workmen's Comp. Appeals Bd.


S.F. No. 22712


Supreme Court of California


2 Cal. 3d 964; 471 P.2d 1002; 88 Cal. Rptr. 202; 35 Cal. Comp. Cases 362; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 365



July 30, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  The decision of the Workmen's Compensation Appeals Board is annulled and the cause remanded to that board for proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner widow sought review of an order from the Workmen's Compensation Appeals Board (California), which dismissed, on its own motion and before the filing of any responsive pleading, her application for death benefits, on the ground that her husband entered into a compromise and release of all claims.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner widow filed a claim for worker's compensation death benefits arising from the death of her husband. Prior to the husband's death, he entered into a compromise and release agreement in which, through a Workmen's Compensation Appeal's Board form, he agreed to release his employer and insurance carrier from all claims including claims from his dependents. The trial court, on its own motion, dismissed petitioner's application on the ground that her husband signed the release. Upon appeal, the court reversed. The court held that petitioner's husband under Cal. Lab. Code § 5000 was entitled to compromise any claims of dependents to worker's compensation death benefits. The court found that petitioner did not have a independent and separate right to the death benefit because § 5000 specifically conferred the power to an employee to release workmen's compensation benefits. The court held the trial court prematurely dismissed petitioner's application where no responsive pleadings asserting the affirmative defense were filed and where petitioner was entitled a hearing to argue the release was invalid due to fraud, mistake, duress, or undue influence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed an order that dismissed petitioner widow's application for workmen's compensation death benefits. The court held that petitioner's husband was entitled to compromise any claims of dependents to death benefits pursuant to a statute. The court found that the trial court precipitately denied petitioner's claim where no responsive pleadings had been filed and petitioner was entitled to hearing.

 

175 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cressey


Crim. No. 13933


Supreme Court of California


2 Cal. 3d 836; 471 P.2d 19; 87 Cal. Rptr. 699; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 311



July 8, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Maurice T. Leader, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of violating Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11556 for being present in an apartment where, with his knowledge, marijuana was being unlawfully used.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of violating Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11556 after police, who were serving defendant with an arrest warrant for nonsupport, found him and another person at his apartment where marijuana was being used unlawfully. Defendant was charged with possession of marijuana and violating § 11556, and a jury found defendant guilty of possession. However, the trial court reduced the offense pursuant to Cal. Pen. Code § 1181, after denying defendant's motion for a new trial. Defendant appealed, and the court affirmed the trial court's decision because the arrest and subsequent search rested upon an affidavit that set forth sufficient underlying facts for the magistrate to have independently found probable cause to arrest defendant for nonsupport, and the warrant was properly indorsed for nighttime service under Cal. Pen. Code § 840. The court also rejected defendant's contention that § 11556 violated his right of association and due process rights in that it punished presence without participation in criminal conduct because the statute required knowledge of the unlawful activity and because he controlled the area where the marijuana was being used.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision convicting defendant of being present when, with his knowledge, marijuana was being used unlawfully because marijuana was discovered during the proper execution of a lawful arrest warrant. Furthermore, the court held that the statute could be constitutionally applied to defendant because he controlled the area in which the marijuana was being used.

Westbrook v. Mihaly


S.F. Nos. 22706, 22707


Supreme Court of California


2 Cal. 3d 765; 471 P.2d 487; 87 Cal. Rptr. 839; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 306



June 30, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  In each of the proceedings before us (S.F. 22706 and S.F. 22707), the alternative writ of mandate is discharged and the petition for a peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner registered voters sought review of Cal. Const. art. XI, § 18 (renumbered as art. XIII, § 40) claiming that its application by respondent local officials violated their rights to equal protection pursuant to U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner registered voters challenged the actions of respondent local officials following an election regarding two municipal bond issue proposals which received the approval of a majority of voters but less than the two-thirds required by Cal. Const. art. XI, §18. Petitioners contended that the two-thirds vote requirement violated their rights to equal protection pursuant to U.S. Const. amend. XIV by diluting affirmative votes, and sought peremptory writs of mandate commanding respondents to certify that the bond issues were approved and to offer the bonds for sale. The court held that the two-thirds requirement violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court reasoned that the right to vote was a fundamental right, thus, the provision was subject to strict scrutiny, and that respondents failed to prove that it was necessary for any compelling state interest. The court found that the provision was not necessary to insure the solvency of local governments and that it did not warrant diluting votes of those in favor of spending. The court held that its decision had only prospective effect and, therefore, denied petitioners' request for peremptory writs.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner registered voters' request for peremptory writs commanding respondent local officials to certify that bond issues receiving the approval of a majority of voters were approved and to offer the bonds for sale because the decision that the requirement of a two-thirds majority vote to approve the bond issues violated petitioners' equal protection rights was applied prospectively only.

 

177 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Harrell


Crim. Nos. 13222, 13383, 13855, 13888


Supreme Court of California


2 Cal. 3d 675; 470 P.2d 640; 87 Cal. Rptr. 504; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 300



June 18, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application in Nos. 13383 and 13855 for a rehearing was denied July 15, 1970, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: Except to the extent that the relief prayed for has been provided herein, in each of the above proceedings (Crim. 13222, Crim. 13383, Crim. 13855, and Crim. 13888) the order to show cause is discharged and the petition for a writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prison inmates sought writs of habeas corpus complaining of limitations placed by prison regulations and authorities upon their rights to give and receive mutual legal assistance, to have personal access to the courts, and to receive and have books and other printed material. One petitioner also claimed he was denied his right to appeal his judgment of conviction.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner inmates sought writs of habeas corpus complaining of limitations placed by prison regulations and authorities upon their rights to give and receive mutual legal assistance, to have personal access to the courts, and to receive and have books and other printed materials. The court found that pursuant to a recent decision, mutual inmate legal assistance was constitutionally necessary. Thus, a prison regulation prohibiting the possession of legal papers belonging to another inmate was invalid. To the extent one petitioner complained that he was prevented from filing an application for relief for another prisoner, his writ was denied. It was the prisoner for whom the application for relief was to have been filed who had the right to gain access to the courts. Petitioners did not have a right to correspond with inmates at other institutions or to interview inmates in isolation. Limitation of access to the prison library was not applied in a discriminatory manner or with a view to limit access to the courts. Under Cal. Penal Code § 2600, prison authorities had no authority to exclude published materials which were accepted for distribution by the United States Post Office.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writs of habeas corpus to allow petitioner inmates to give and receive mutual legal assistance, to have access to the courts, and to receive published materials. As to the remainder, the order to show cause was discharged, and the petition for a writ of habeas corpus was denied.

In re H.


Crim. No. 14091


Supreme Court of California


2 Cal. 3d 513; 468 P.2d 204; 86 Cal. Rptr. 76; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 288



April 30, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  The order to show cause is discharged and a writ of habeas corpus is granted. The order of commitment of August 6, 1969, is vacated and petitioner is discharged from the custody of the California Youth Authority.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner juvenile sought a writ of habeas corpus challenging the validity Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 903.1 making parents and other responsible relatives liable to the county for the cost of legal services rendered to the minor in juvenile court proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner juvenile was committed to the custody of the California Youth Authority and detained for participating in a burglary. After having been informed of his right to legal counsel through all stages of a juvenile court proceeding, petitioner formally waived counsel, admitted the truth of the petition's allegations, and was thereupon committed. Petitioner then challenged the validity of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 903.1 which made parents and other responsible relatives liable to the county for the cost of legal services rendered to the minor in juvenile court proceedings. He contended that it unreasonably and arbitrarily discriminated against the parents of indigent juveniles, thereby denying petitioner and his parents equal protection of the laws. On review, The court held that petitioner's waiver was induced by fear, coercion or threats and was ineffective. The juvenile court should not permit minors to waive their right to appointed counsel for the purpose of avoiding the operation of the reimbursement requirement. Writ of habeas corpus was granted.


OUTCOME: Petitioner juvenile was granted a writ of habeas corpus challenging the validity of the statute making parents responsible relatives liable to the county for the cost of legal services rendered to the minor in juvenile court proceedings. The juvenile court should not permit minors to waive their right to appointed counsel for the purpose of avoiding the operation of the reimbursement requirement.

 

179 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Smith


Crim. No. 13961


Supreme Court of California


2 Cal. 3d 508; 467 P.2d 836; 86 Cal. Rptr. 4; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 287



April 30, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for a writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner requested a writ of habeas corpus following his conviction by a trial court (California) for possession of narcotics other than marijuana, violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500. Petitioner alleged that his sentence was improperly increased by a prior narcotics conviction under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11721, for portions of § 11721 had been held unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend. VIII.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of the use of or addiction to narcotics in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11721. That portion of the statute dealing with addiction to narcotics as a criminal offense was later declared unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend. VIII. Petitioner was subsequently convicted of violating Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500 for possession of narcotics other than marijuana, and his sentence was increased due to the prior conviction. In a habeas corpus proceeding, the court denied his petition, holding that his sentence had been properly increased by the prior conviction. The court found that petitioner had not met his burden of proving that his prior conviction had been based not upon the constitutional but upon the unconstitutional provisions of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11721. He presented no evidence sufficient to overcome the presumption of regularity that attached to his first conviction or to overcome the showing offered by the state that petitioner had pleaded guilty both to the use and addiction to narcotics.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for a writ of habeas corpus. Petitioner's sentence was not shown to have been improperly increased by the prior narcotics use or addiction conviction because petitioner did not meet his burden of proving that his prior conviction was based upon the unconstitutional addiction provisions, rather than the valid use provisions, of the partially unconstitutional statute.

 

180 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Lifschutz


Crim. No. 14131


Supreme Court of California


2 Cal. 3d 415; 467 P.2d 557; 85 Cal. Rptr. 829; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 280; 44 A.L.R.3d 1



April 15, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus securing his release from imprisonment after being adjudicated in contempt of court for not obeying a San Mateo Superior Court (California) order instructing him to answer questions and produce records relating to communications with his former patient, plaintiff in the pending assault action.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner psychiatrist sought a writ of habeas corpus to secure his release from imprisonment after being adjudicated in contempt of court for refusing to obey a trial court's order to answer questions and produce records regarding his former patient, the plaintiff in the pending assault action. The appellate court denied the writ, holding the order did not infringe on petitioner's constitutional rights, as he had no absolute privilege concerning the confidentiality of psychotherapeutic communications. The presence of a legislatively created absolute clergyman-penitent privilege did not render the absence of an absolute psychotherapist-patient privilege a denial of equal protection because of the legislatively tolerated scope of the clergyman-penitent relationship. Petitioner had no statutory authority for noncompliance with the court order and could not assert the nondisclosure privilege of his patient because by instituting the pending suit, he put his mental and emotional condition into issue under Cal. Evid. Code § 1016.


OUTCOME: In denying the writ of habeas corpus, appellate court held that petitioner had no absolute privilege relating to the confidentiality of psychotherapeutic communications and that he could not assert his patient's rights because by instituting the pending assault action, his former patient placed his emotional and mental condition into issue.

 

181 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Carmel-by-the-Sea v. Young


S. F. No. 22728


Supreme Court of California


2 Cal. 3d 259; 466 P.2d 225; 85 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 271; 37 A.L.R.3d 1313



March 26, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, Ralph M. Drummond, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. This decision is final forthwith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), decreeing that a financial interest public disclosure statute was constitutional. Plaintiff sought declaratory relief in its action against defendant district attorney, attacking the constitutional validity of the statute.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff city brought a declaratory action against defendant district attorney, attacking the constitutionality of a financial interests public disclosure statute that by its terms applied to a myriad of public officers and employees. The statute required every public officer and each candidate for state or local public office, to file, as a public record, a statement describing the nature and extent of his investments and those owned by either spouse or by a minor child. Plaintiff alleged that some of its officers and officials would resign rather than be subjected to an unconstitutional invasion of their privacy by requiring them to file such a statement of assets disclosing their personal financial affairs and those of their spouses and children. The trial court found the statute constitutional. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, holding that the statute was unconstitutional because the statute undertook an overbroad intrusion into the right of privacy and thereby restricted the right to seek public office or employment. The court further held that the disclosure requirement reached beyond legitimate state interest.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment that declared the financial interests public disclosure statute constitutional. The court held that the statute was unconstitutional because the statute undertook an overbroad intrusion into the right of privacy and thereby restricted the right to seek public office or employment. The court further held that the disclosure requirement reached beyond legitimate state interest.

Castro v. State


L.A. No. 29693


Supreme Court of California


2 Cal. 3d 223; 466 P.2d 244; 85 Cal. Rptr. 20; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 269



March 24, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Ralph H. Nutter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded with directions to amend the findings of fact and conclusions of law and to enter judgment ordering the issuance of a peremptory writ of mandate commanding respondents to determine the qualifications of petitioners to vote in accordance with the views herein expressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, eligible Spanish literate voters, sought a review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which upheld Cal. Const. art. II, § 1 that conditioned the right to vote upon an ability to read the English language as applied to persons, who though qualified to vote, were literate in Spanish but not in English.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, eligible Spanish literate voters, were denied registration to vote on the basis of their inability to read English and brought an action against respondents, state and associated officials, challenging the English literacy requirement. Petitioners sought a declaration that the English literacy requirement of Cal. Const. art. II, § 1 was unconstitutional as applied to them and to other Spanish literates and mandatory relief requiring respondents to register petitioners to vote. The trial court found for respondents, concluding that the English language requirement was a rational state policy. The court reversed the trial court's judgment because, as applied to petitioners and to all residents of Los Angeles County who were otherwise qualified to vote and literate in the Spanish language, the English literacy requirement of art. II, § 1 violated their right to the equal protection of the laws. The court remanded the case to the trial court with instructions to enter judgment ordering the issuance of a peremptory writ of mandate, commanding respondents to determine the qualifications of petitioners to vote in accordance with the court's views.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision upholding the English literacy provision of the state Constitution because the provision violated the equal protection rights of petitioners, eligible Spanish literate voters. The case was remanded for the issuance of a writ of mandate commanding respondents, state and associated individuals, to determine the qualifications of petitioners to vote.

In re Lopez


Crim. No. 13485


Supreme Court of California


2 Cal. 3d 141; 465 P.2d 257; 84 Cal. Rptr. 361; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 262



March 2, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  The writ is granted and petitioner is discharged from custody.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus to review a judgment of the Los Angeles Superior Court (California), which ordered civil commitment for treatment as a narcotics addict under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3050 based on an underlying misdemeanor conviction for using and being under the influence of narcotics.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner challenged a judgment which ordered civil commitment for treatment as a narcotics addict under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3050, entered after he was convicted of using and being under the influence of narcotics. Petitioner contended that he was not informed of his right to counsel and that his request for court-appointed counsel was rejected in the criminal proceedings before the municipal court. Further, petitioner argued that he did not waive his right to counsel and that the unconstitutionality of the underlying conviction vitiated his subsequent commitment. The court held that based upon the official docket entry, petitioner did in fact plead guilty without counsel and was informed of his constitutional rights prior to entering his plea. However, the record did not reflect that petitioner had validly waived his right to counsel, thus, his right to counsel was violated. Therefore, the court held that petitioner's conviction on a guilty plea was invalid and that the validity of a commitment as a narcotics addict was affected by the validity of the prerequisite criminal conviction. The writ of habeas corpus was granted and petitioner was discharged from custody.


OUTCOME: Petitioner was granted a writ of habeas corpus and discharged from custody after being civilly committed for treatment as a narcotics addict based on an underlying misdemeanor conviction. The court held that petitioner's conviction was invalid because his right to counsel was violated and that, because the prerequisite criminal conviction was invalid, the validity of the commitment for narcotics treatment was also affected.

Boreta Enterprises, Inc. v. Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control


S.F. Nos. 22660, 22661


Supreme Court of California


2 Cal. 3d 85; 465 P.2d 1; 84 Cal. Rptr. 113; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 258



February 26, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Joseph Karesh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant California Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control appealed a decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted a peremptory writ of mandate setting aside defendant's decision ordering revocation of plaintiff corporation's license.


OVERVIEW: Defendant California Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control sought, under Cal. Const. art. XX, § 22, and Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24200(a), to revoke the on-sale general bona fide public eating place license of plaintiff corporation, alleging that plaintiff permitted its female employees to display and expose their breasts to patrons. Defendant charged that the acts were contrary to public welfare or morals. Defendant's hearing officers found that plaintiff's license should be revoked, which defendant adopted. The Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board affirmed defendant's decision. The trial court found in plaintiff's favor and ordered the issuance of a peremptory writ of mandate commanding defendant to set aside its decision, and found that there was no substantial evidence to show that the presence of topless waitresses was contrary to public health. The court affirmed the trial court's decision because defendant's decision was not supported by the findings and hence constituted an abuse of discretion. The court held that the action of defendant could not be approved on the basis that topless waitresses were per se contrary to public welfare or morals.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision ordering the issuance of a peremptory writ of mandate commanding defendant California Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control to set aside its decision that revoked plaintiff corporation's license. The court held that the decision of defendant was not supported by the findings and hence constituted an abuse of discretion.

 

185 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Barrows v. Mun. Court of L.A. Judicial Dist.


L. A. No. 29672


Supreme Court of California


1 Cal. 3d 821; 464 P.2d 483; 83 Cal. Rptr. 819; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 352



January 30, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the real party in interest and respondent for a rehearing was denied February 25, 1970. McComb, J., and Burke, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, James G. Whyte, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed and the court is directed to issue the writ of prohibition as prayed for.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners actors challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which charged them under Cal. Penal Code §§ 647(a) and 311.6 for their performances of the play with willfully and unlawfully aiding and abetting in these violations. Petitioners seek a writ of prohibition to restrain respondent trial court from proceeding to trial.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed and directed issuance of writ of prohibition preventing trial court from proceeding to trial on petitioners actors charges of violating Cal. Penal Code §§ 647(a) and 311.6 for performing in a play and aiding and abetting in these violations. The court concluded that these statutes were not intended to apply to a live theatrical performance before an audience, and that the application of the provisions to such performance violated petitioners' constitutional rights to free speech, due process and equal protection of the laws, under U.S. Const. amends. I and XIV, and Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 9, 11, 13 and 21. The court found that the purpose of § 647 was to punish the crime of vagrancy, and that nothing in the legislative history indicated that it was intended to apply to activities, like theatrical performances, which are within the ambit of amend I. protection. The court concluded it would be irrational to impose upon an actor in a theatrical performance or its director a lifetime requirement of registration as a sexual offender under Cal. Penal Code § 290 because he may have performed or aided in the performance of an act, perhaps an obscene gesture, in a play.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order charging petitioners actors and directed issued the writ of prohibition as prayed for concluding that the consequences of applying the statutes, to acts performed in a play, the language of the statutes when read in the light of the definition of obscenity, and the legislative history of both sections, all indicate that the legislature did not intend those statutes to apply to theatrical performances.

 

186 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Kay


Crim. No. 13533


Supreme Court of California


1 Cal. 3d 930; 464 P.2d 142; 83 Cal. Rptr. 686; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 362



January 30, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  The writ is granted. The judgment against petitioners is set aside.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner protestors were convicted of disturbing a lawful meeting in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 403, a misdemeanor, and were sentenced to four months in jail. After the appellate department of the superior court affirmed their conviction, and the court of appeal refused certification, petitioners sought a writ of habeas corpus, claiming violation of free speech and due process of law of the United States and California Constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner protestors attended a meeting at which a congressman spoke, and they disturbed his speech by rhythmical clapping and shouting. They were convicted of disturbing a lawful meeting in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 403. After their appeal was denied, they sought a writ of habeas corpus to determine the constitutionally permissible bounds of Cal. Penal Code § 403. The court granted the writ and set aside the judgment. If literally applied with the breadth of coverage that its terms could encompass, § 403 would be unconstitutionally overbroad. Rather than enacting monolithic standards, § 403 drew its content from the implicit customs and usages or explicit rules germane to a given meeting. Violation of customs or rules by one who knew or, as a reasonable man, should have known of them would justify the application of § 403. The prosecution offered no evidence that clapping, flag waving, and sloganeering were not generally accepted and permitted at a public meeting, addressed by controversial elected officials, or that the activities substantially impaired the conduct of the meeting.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ and set aside the judgment against petitioners, concluding that their conduct did not fall within the constitutionally compelled meaning of the statute, and that the trial court erred in failing to designate the constitutionally compelled limits of the broad statutory language. Habeas corpus was the appropriate remedy, as the statute could not constitutionally be applied to the facts of the case.

 

187 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Nat'l Adver. Co. v. County of Monterey


S. F. No. 22659


Supreme Court of California


1 Cal. 3d 875; 464 P.2d 33; 83 Cal. Rptr. 577; 1970 Cal. LEXIS 356



January 30, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, Stanley Lawson, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  The judgment is reversed with respect to the 31 fully amortized signs, but is affirmed otherwise. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which entered judgment declaring certain provisions of defendant's zoning ordinance to be null and void and enjoined enforcement thereof as against plaintiff outdoor advertising company.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff outdoor advertising company owned billboards on various private parcels that it leased in defendant county. Defendant county adopted a zoning ordinance that caused nonconforming uses to be removed after five years. Plaintiff owned billboards that were nonconforming and filed an action against defendant, alleging that such ordinance was null and void. The lower court held that defendant's ordinance was null and void, and defendant appealed. The court held that the removal requirements of the ordinance could validly be applied to certain of plaintiff's billboards, but that as to others removal should await expiration of appropriate amortization periods. The court further held that plaintiff's evidence failed to show arbitrariness and unreasonableness with respect to the signs that had fully been amortized. The court noted that plaintiff produced no evidence with respect to present actual value of any of the signs, or relating to amortization of any such value, nor did it prove that the remaining signs could not be used elsewhere. Plaintiff failed to meet its burden of proof, and thus the judgment of the lower court was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court with respect to plaintiff outdoor advertising company's signs that had been fully amortized, but it affirmed the judgment otherwise because plaintiff could await expiration of a reasonable amortization period in order to permit it to recover its original cost for the signs remaining.

Supreme Court of California

OVERVIEW: Petitioner, an attorney and a private pilot, put forth grievances to the local airport regarding unsafe runway conditions and made appearances at airport board meetings. In relation to conduct during one of the board meetings, petitioner was found guilty of disturbing the peace in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 415. OUTCOME: The court granted the writ of habeas corpus and discharged petitioner from the custody imposed. The court concluded that the jury instruction given by the trial court was improper because it made criminal certain conduct that was protected by the First Amendment.

Supreme Court of California

People v. Gilbert

Supreme Court of California


1 Cal. 3d 475; 462 P.2d 580; 82 Cal. Rptr. 724; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 223



December 23, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Walter H. Odemar, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted defendant of fraudulently obtaining more than $ 200 in Aid to Families With Dependent Children (AFDC) benefits, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 484.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was receiving AFDC benefits, lived with a man and accepted money from him. Defendant was convicted by the trial court of a theft offense under Cal. Penal Code § 484. People v. Brawley


Crim. No. 10838


Supreme Court of California


1 Cal. 3d 277; 461 P.2d 361; 82 Cal. Rptr. 161; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 208



November 21, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Robert W. Conyers, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are reversed insofar as they relate to the penalty for the murder; in all other respects they are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which entered a jury verdict of guilty against them for first degree murder and first degree robbery, fixing their penalty at death for the murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were indicted for the murder and robbery of a cab driver. Fingerprint, shoe prints, possession of decedent's wallet, and evidence of both defendants being together supported their convictions. The trial court found them guilty. They appealed based on multiple contentions. The court found that defendants' statements were properly admitted as being in furtherance of the conspiracy because they involved items that might be used in the robbery and killing. The court also held that because California procedure contained more safeguards for a defendant than federal procedure, California procedure could not violate the confrontation clause of the Sixth Amendment where the United States Supreme Court had already held that federal procedure did not violate the confrontation clause. Thus the asserted misconduct afforded no basis for concluding that the court erred in denying the motion for severance. However, the court held that the exclusion of veniremen based on their view of capital punishment warranted reversal of their penalties for the murder, but not the convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of convictions, but reversed insofar as it related to the penalty for the murder.

 

192 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Morrison v. State Board of Education


L. A. No. 29632


Supreme Court of California


1 Cal. 3d 214; 461 P.2d 375; 82 Cal. Rptr. 175; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 204



November 20, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Ralph H. Nutter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court denying the writ of mandate is reversed, and the cause is remanded to the superior court for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant teacher challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, California, which denied his request for a writ of mandate to compel respondent State Board of Education to set aside its decision and restore his life diplomas.


OVERVIEW: Appellant teacher counseled a friend going through marital and financial difficulties and was experiencing severe emotional stress. For a one-week period, appellant and his friend engaged in a homosexual relationship. Respondent State Board of Education was advised of this relationship about a year later and revoked appellant's life diplomas. The lower court denied appellant's request for a writ of mandate compelling respondent to set aside its decision and restore his life diplomas. This court reversed the decision and remanded the matter for further proceedings. It found that disciplinary measures were only authorized for conduct that indicated an unfitness to teach and that there was no evidence to support the conclusion that appellant's conduct indicated his unfitness to teach. Respondent failed to show that appellant's conduct in any manner affected his performance as a teacher. Further, the diplomas were revoked three years after the incident, appellant's motives at the time of the incident involved neither dishonesty nor viciousness, and the emotional pressures on both appellant and his friend suggested the presence of extenuating circumstances.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the lower court denying the writ of mandate was reversed and the cause remanded for further proceedings. There was no evidence that appellant teacher's conduct indicated his unfitness to teach.

Bishop v. San Jose


S. F. No. 22677


Supreme Court of California


1 Cal. 3d 56; 460 P.2d 137; 81 Cal. Rptr. 465; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 192



October 30, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied November 26, 1969. Draper, J. pro tem., * and Sims, J. pro tem., * sat in place of Tobriner, J., and Sullivan, J., who deemed themselves disqualified. Peters, J., Mosk, J., and Sims, J. pro tem., * were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara Couny, O. Vincent Bruno, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a resident and taxpayer (resident), appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), in favor of defendant city, which declared the prevailing wage provisions found in certain sections of the Cal. Lab. Code § 1770 et seq., to be inapplicable to the employees of the city, and denied relief sought by way of injunction and damages.


OVERVIEW: The resident filed an action against the city contending that from 1958 to 1966, the city unlawfully failed to pay its electricians the prevailing rate of per diem wages for work of a similar character, pursuant to the prevailing wage law. The trial court entered judgment in favor of the city, finding that the setting and payment of salaries for the city's own employees were municipal affairs to which, under the home rule provisions of Cal. Const. art. XI, §§ 6, 8, the prevailing wage provisions of Cal. Lab. Code § 1770 et seq. did not apply. The court held that the trial court correctly ruled that Cal. Lab. Code §§ 1724, 1771, 1773, indicated that the legislature had not intended that the prevailing wage laws apply to the setting of the salaries of the city's employees, and, specifically, that § 1771 did not apply to work carried out by a public agency with its own forces.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the city.

 

194 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Tijerina


Crim. No. 13547


Supreme Court of California


1 Cal. 3d 41; 459 P.2d 680; 81 Cal. Rptr. 264; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 190



October 24, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Patrick Coleman, Court Commissioner, Judge pro tem.



DISPOSITION: In No. 307540, the order revoking probation is reversed.

In No. A-220328, the judgment is affirmed.

In No. A-226235, the appeal from the order denying a new trial is dismissed, and the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In three separate cases, the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) convicted defendant of grand theft in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 487, petty theft with a prior conviction of a felony in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 667, and assault with a deadly weapon in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 245. Defendant's motion for a new trial was denied and his probation was revoked. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant argued that there was insufficient evidence that he had committed grand theft, that his waiver to a jury trial was ineffective, and that the order revoking his probation was void because there was no stipulation to permit the commissioner to act as a temporary judge. The court held that (1) there was sufficient evidence of the fair market value of the stolen goods to support his conviction for grand theft, (2) that defendant personally waived his right to a jury trial at his trial for petty theft after he had waived his right to a jury trial at his trial for grand theft, and (3) because there was no stipulation authorizing the commissioner to act as a temporary judge, the order revoking defendant's probation was void.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that revoked probation. Defendant's convictions were affirmed and the order denying his motion for a new trial was affirmed as well.

 

195 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Kapellas v. Kofman


S. F. No. 22666


Supreme Court of California


1 Cal. 3d 20; 459 P.2d 912; 81 Cal. Rptr. 360; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 189



October 24, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied November 19, 1969, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Mosk, J., and Burke, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, William H. Brailsford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal of the third count is affirmed. The judgment of dismissal of the first two counts is reversed with directions to the trial court to overrule the demurrer as to these two counts and allow defendant to answer.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff politician sought review of the dismissal by the Superior Court of Alameda County of her claims for libel and her children's claims for invasion of privacy against defendant newspaper publisher who relied on privilege and public interest in his materials as defenses to the claims.


OVERVIEW: While running for office, a politician, who was a mother of six children, received negative press in the local newspaper. The newspaper printed an editorial that stated that the politician was not putting her children's needs first and that her performance as a mother was less than adequate. The editorial also mentioned that the politician's children had been on trouble with the local police. Immediately after the editorial appeared, the politician demanded a correction, but it was never published. She filed an action against the publisher claiming that the article was libelous against her and her children and that it invaded her children's privacy. The publisher claimed that his qualified privilege protected him from libel and that the politician had not given the publisher adequate notice of her demand for a correction. As to the children's right of privacy, the publisher claimed that the matters were of public interest. The lower court agreed and dismissed the case. On appeal, the court affirmed the dismissal of the right to privacy claim. The court reversed on the other claims because it found that no privilege applied and that the politician adequately requested a correction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the demurrer by the lower court, which dismissed the invasion of privacy claims by the politician's children, and held that the matters were of public interest. The court reversed the dismissal of the libel counts, holding that the qualified privilege did not apply.

 

196 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Big Boy Liquors, Ltd. v. Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Bd.


S.F. No. 22623


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 1226; 459 P.2d 674; 81 Cal. Rptr. 258; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 316



October 21, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 19, 1969. Tobriner, J., and Sullivan, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING to review a decision of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board affirming a decision by the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control suspending a retail off-sale general liquor license.



DISPOSITION: Decision of department reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner liquor store owner challenged the judgment of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board (California), which affirmed a decision suspending its retail off-sale general liquor license.


OVERVIEW: A liquor store was investigated for selling whisky at a price below the minimum retail price. The department of alcohol beverage control (department) suspended its license. On appeal, the liquor store claimed, inter alia, that the department did not have the power to suspend its license in light of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24755.1, which purported to prevent the sanctions of suspension or revocation for such sales. The court found that the department improperly ordered the suspension. Although the department properly found that the liquor store had violated the minimum retail price maintenance provisions of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24755, it exceeded its power by imposing such penalty contrary to the provisions of Bus. & Prof. Code, § 24755.1 which was constitutional. The judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: The judgment suspending the liquor store's license was reversed.

 

197 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Samson Market Co. v. Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Bd.


L. A. No. 29584


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 1215; 459 P.2d 667; 81 Cal. Rptr. 251; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 315



October 21, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 19, 1969. Tobriner, J., and Sullivan, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING to review a decision of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board affirming a decision by the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control revoking a retail off-sale general liquor license.



DISPOSITION: Decision of department reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner licensee challenged the decision of respondent Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board (California), which affirmed the revocation of the licensee's retail off-sale general liquor license.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that if there was substantial evidence to show that the price schedules involved were published in a trade journal of general circulation prior to the effective dates of such schedules, the court was required to sustain the findings that those price schedules were effective. The court held that the licensee had the burden of proving that the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control (department) did not regularly perform its official duty, in that it failed to ascertain whether the schedules had been properly published. The court held that in this respect the licensee failed to meet the burden of proof necessary to rebut the presumption. The court held that the prohibition in Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24755.1 against license suspension or revocation was constitutional and properly qualified the constitutional power to license granted the department by Cal. Const. art. XX, § 22. The court reversed the affirmation of the revocation, holding that although it was properly held that the licensee violated the minimum retail price maintenance provisions of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24755, the revocation of the license was contrary to the provisions of § 24755.1.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board, which had affirmed the revocation of the licensee's retail off-sale general liquor license. The court remanded the case for reconsideration of the penalty.

 

198 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Kirby v. Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board


S. F. No. 22622


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 1200; 459 P.2d 657; 81 Cal. Rptr. 241; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 314



October 21, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 19, 1969. Tobriner, J., and Sullivan, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING to review a decision of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board reversing a decision of the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control revoking a retail off-sale liquor license.



DISPOSITION: Decision of the department reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, director of the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control (department), appealed a decision by respondent, the California Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board (board), which suspended the retail off-sale general liquor license of the real party in interest, licensee.


OVERVIEW: The department accused the licensee of violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24755 and Cal. Code Regs. tit. 4, § 99(a) for the publication of liquor sales at less than the prices listed in the minimum retail price schedule. The department rejected the decision of a hearing officer to suspend the license, and instead revoked the license. The board reversed. On appeal, the court reversed the department's decision and remanded for reconsideration of a penalty consistent with Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24755.1. The court held that the publication of the minimum retail price schedule in a trade journal of general circulation in the licensee's trade area satisfied the publication requirements of § 24755. The court also held that § 24755 and Cal. Code Regs. tit. 4, § 99 were constitutional. However, the department lacked the authority to revoke the license because § 24755 only allowed a monetary penalty. The court reasoned that Cal. Const. art. XX, § 22 subjected the department's decisions regarding penalties to legislative oversight. As a result, the monetary penalties of § 24755.1 were constitutional and controlled the department's determination of a penalty.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the decision of the department for reconsideration of a penalty within statutory limitations.

 

199 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Byers v. Justice Court for Ukiah Judicial Dist.


S. F. No. 22634


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 1039; 458 P.2d 465; 80 Cal. Rptr. 553; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 304



September 16, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 15, 1969. McComb, J., and Burke, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Mendocino County. Robert L. Winslow, Judge.

Proceeding in prohibition to restrain a justice court from proceeding further in a "hit and run" prosecution.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment granting writ affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff driver, who had been charged with violations of Cal. Veh. Code §§ 21750, 20002(a), obtained a writ of prohibition from the Superior Court of Mendocino County (California) against defendant Justice Court for the Ukiah Judicial District of Mendocino County. The justice court was thereby restrained from proceeding against the driver, and the prosecution appealed.


OVERVIEW: The driver had been accused of improper and unsafe passing, Cal. Veh. Code § 21750, and with leaving the scene of an automobile accident without furnishing to the owner of the damaged property information required by Cal. Veh. Code § 20002(a). The driver urged that § 20002(a), a "hit-and-run" provision, was unconstitutional as applied because it violated the driver's Fifth Amendment privilege against self-incrimination. The court held that while the Fifth Amendment privilege was applicable to the question of the driver's compliance with the "hit-and-run" statute, the driver should have complied because of use-restrictions which he could only have speculated might exist. While the judgment could not be affirmed on the theory that the driver was privileged to withhold the § 20002(a) information, fairness dictated that the driver should not be punished for his failure to comply with § 20002(a). Where compliance with § 20002(a) would otherwise be excused by the self-incrimination privilege, compliance was mandatory; and state prosecuting authorities were precluded from using the disclosed information or its fruits in connection with any criminal prosecution related to the accident.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. No valid purpose would be served by requiring the driver to belatedly comply with the "hit-and-run" statute.

 

200 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Belous


Crim. 12739


Supreme Court of California


71 Cal. 2d 954; 458 P.2d 194; 80 Cal. Rptr. 354; 1969 Cal. LEXIS 299



September 5, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 1, 1969. Pierce, J. Pro Tem., * Sat in Place of Mosk, J., who Deemed Himself Disqualified. McComb, J., Burke, J., and Sullivan, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment (order granting probation) of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. John G. Barnes, Judge.

Prosecution for abortion and conspiracy to commit abortion.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment of conviction reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) sentencing him to two years probation after he was convicted of abortion and conspiracy to commit an abortion.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a physician, was convicted of abortion, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 274, and conspiracy to commit an abortion, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 182. The trial court imposed a $ 5000 fine and placed defendant on probation for two years. On appeal, defendant challenged the order imposing probation. The court found that the validity of § 274 could not be sustained. The court explained that the pressures on a physician to decide not to perform an absolutely necessary abortion were, under § 274, enormous, and because § 274 authorized and required a doctor to decide, at his peril, whether an abortion was necessary, a woman whose life was at stake could be as effectively condemned to death as if the law flatly prohibited all abortions. Because § 274 was invalid, defendant was wrongfully convicted.


OUTCOME: The order convicting defendant of abortion and conspiracy to commit an abortion was reversed and the indictment against him was dismissed.
